<leme no="278">

<page no="12">
<section type="Languages">

<heading>The Languages.</heading>

FRom the people we will now proceede to 
the languages. Heere would Schollers 
shew you the first confusion of langua&shy;
ges out of <f type="i">Moses</f>, that the gods had their 
peculiar tongue out of <f type="i">Homer</f>, that brute 
beasts, birdes, and fishes, had their owne 
proper languages out of <f type="i">Clemens Alexandrius</f>. They 
would teach you out of <f type="i">Euphorus</f>, that there were but 52. 
</page>

<page no="13">
tongues in the world, because so many soules out of <f type="i">Iacob</f> 
descended into <f type="i">&AElig;gypt</f>, and out of <f type="i">Arnobius</f>, that there were 
seaventie and two: Albeit <f type="i">Timosthenes</f> reporteth that in 
<f type="i">Dioscurias</f> a mart towne of <f type="i">Colchis</f>, their trafficked 300. 
Nations of divers languages: And howsoever our <f type="i">Indian</f> or 
<f type="i">American</f> discouerers say, that in every fourescore mile in 
<f type="i">America</f>, and in every valley almost of <f type="i">Peru</f> you shall finde 
a new language. Neither would they omit the Iland where 
the people have cloven tongues out of the fabulous <f type="i">Narra&shy;
tions</f> of <f type="i">Diodorus Siculus</f>: yea, they would lash out of the <f type="i">Vto&shy;
pian</f> language with 

<f type="i"><term lang="un">Volvola Barchin hemam, la lalvola drame pagloni.</term></f>

whenas it is a greater glory now to be a <f type="i">Linguist</f>, then a <f type="i">Re&shy;
alist</f>. They would moreover discourse at large, which I will 
tell you in a word. First the British tongue or Welsh (as we 
now call it) was in vse onely in this Island, having great af&shy;
finitie with the olde <f type="i">Galliqu&aelig</f>; of <f type="i">Gaule</f>, now <f type="i">Fraunce</f>, from 
whence the first inhabitants in all probability came hither. 
Afterward the <f type="i">Latin</f> was taken vp when it was brought in&shy;
to the forme of a Proviner, about the time of <f type="i">Domitian</f>, ac&shy;
cording to that notable place of <f type="i">Tacitus</f>, where he report&shy;
teth that <f type="i">Iulius Agricola</f> Governour heere for the <f type="i">Romans</f>, 
preferred the <f type="i">Britans</f>, as able to doe more by witte, then the 
<f type="i">Gaules</f> by studie: <f type="i"><term lang="la">Vt qui</term></f> (saieth he) <f type="i"><term lang="la">mod&ograve; linguam Romanam 
abnuebant, eloquentiam concupiscerent. Inde etiam habitus no&shy;
stri honor &amp; frequens toga</term></f>. But the <f type="i">British</f> overgrewe the 
<f type="i">Latine</f>, and continueth yet in <f type="i">Wales</f>, and some villages of <f type="i">Cornwall</f> intermingled with some <f type="i">Provinciall Latine</f>. After 
the Irish tongue was brought into the Northwest partes of 
the Isle, out of <f type="i">Ireland</f> by the auntient Scottishmen, and 
there yet remaineth. Lastly, the <f type="i">English-Saxon</f> tongue came 
in by the <f type="i">English-Saxons</f> o<emend err="n">u</emend>t of <f type="i">Germany</f>, who valiantly and 
wisely performed heere all the three things, which implie a 
full conquest, viz. the alteration of lawes, language, and at&shy;
tire. 

This English tongue extracted out of the olde <f type="i">German</f>, 
as most other from <f type="i">Island</f> to the <f type="i">Alpes</f>, is mixed as it is now, 
</page>

<page no="14"> 
of the olde <f type="i">English-Saxon</f> &amp; <f type="i">Norman French of the Frecnh</f> 
of <f type="i">Latine, German</f>, and the olde <f type="i">Gallique</f>, the <f type="i">Italian of  La&shy;
tine</f> and <f type="i">German-Gotish</f>, and the <f type="i">Spanish</f> of <f type="i">Latine, Gotish&shy;
German</f> and <f type="i">Arabique, Saracen</f>, or <f type="i">Morisque</f>. And to the 
honour of our progenitors the <f type="i">English-Saxons</f> be it spoken, 
their conquest was more absolute heere over the <f type="i">Britaines</f>, 
than either of the <f type="i">Francs</f> in <f type="i">Fraunce</f> over the <f type="i">Gaules</f>, or the 
<f type="i">Gothes</f> and <f type="i">Lombardes</f> in <f type="i">Italie</f> over the <f type="i">Romans</f>, or of the 
<f type="i">Gothes, Vandales</f>, and <f type="i">Moores</f> over the auntient <f type="i">Spaniards</f> 
in <f type="i">Spaine</f>. For in these nations much of the provinciall La&shy;
tine (I meane the Latine vsed whilest they were Provinces 
of the <f type="i">Romans</f>) remaineth, which they politikely had spread 
over their Empire, by planting of Colonies and enfranchi&shy;
sing all Nations subiect vnto them. But the <f type="i">English-Saxon</f> 
conquerors, altred the tongue which they found here who&shy;
ly: so that no <f type="i">British</f> words, or provinciall <f type="i">Latine</f> appeared 
therein at the first: &amp; in short time they spread it over this 
whole <f type="i">Iland</f>, from the <f type="i">Orcades</f> to Isle of <f type="i">Wight</f>, except a few 
barren corners in the <f type="i">Westerne</f> parts, wherevnto the reliques 
of the <f type="i">Britans</f> and <f type="i">Scots</f> retyred, reserving in them both their 
life and their language. For certainely it is that the greatest 
and best parts, the East and South of <f type="i">Scotland</f>, which call 
themselves the <f type="i">Lawland-men</f>, speake the English tongue va&shy;
ried onely in <f type="i">Dialect</f>, as descended from the <f type="i">English-Saxons</f>: 
and the old <f type="i">Scottish</f>, which is the verie <f type="i">Irish</f>, is vsed onely by 
them of the West, called the <f type="i">Hechtland-men</f>, who call the 
other as the Welsh call vs <f type="i">Sassons, Saxons</f>, both in respect of 
language and originall, as I shewed before.

I dare not yet heere affirme for the antiquitie of our lan&shy;
guage, that our great-great-great-grandsires tongue came 
out of <f type="i">Persia</f>, albeit the wonderfull Linguist <f type="i">Ioseph Scaliger</f> 
hath observed, <f type="i">Fader; Moder, Bruder, Band &amp;c</f>. in the <f type="i">Persian</f> 
tongue in the very sence as we now vse them.

It will not be vnproper I hope to this purpose, if I note 
out of the epistles of that learned Ambassadour <f type="i">Busbequius</f>, 
how the inhabitants of <f type="i">Taurica-Chersonessus</f>, in the vtter&shy;
most part of <f type="i">Europe</f> eastward, have these words, <f type="i">Wind, Sil&shy; 
</page>

<page no="15">
ver, Korne, Salt, Fish, Son, Apple, Waggen, Singen, Ilanda, Beard</f>, 
with many other in the very same sence and signification, 
as they now are in vse with vs, whereat I mervailed not a 
little when I first read it. But nothing can bee gathered 
thereby, but that the <f type="i">Saxons</f> our progenitours, which plan&shy;
ted themselves heere in the West, did also to their glorie 
place <f type="i">Colonies</f> likewise there in the east.

As in the Latine tongue, the learned make in respect of 
time, foure <f type="i">Idioms</f>, the <f type="i">Antient</f>, the <f type="i">Latine</f>, the <f type="i">Roman</f>, the 
<f type="i">Mixt</f>: so we in ours may make the <f type="i">Antient English-Saxon</f>, and 
the <f type="i">Mixt</f>. But that you may seeme how powerable time is 
in altering tongues as all things else, I will set downe the 
Lords prayer as it was translated in sundrie ages, that you 
may see by what degrees our tongue is risen, and thereby 
coniecture how in time it may alter and fall againe.

If we could set it downe in the antient <f type="i">Saxon</f>, I meane 
in the tongue which the English vsed at their first arrivall 
heere, about 420. yeares after Christs birth, it would seeme 
most strange and harsh Dutch or Gebrish, as women call 
it; or when they first embraced Christianitie, about the 
yeare of Christ 600. But the antientst that I can finde, was 
about 900. yeare since, about the yeare of Christ 700. 
found in an antient <f type="i">Saxon</f>, glossed <f type="i">Evangelists</f> in the hands 
of my good friend M. <f type="i">Robert Bowyer</f>, written by <f type="i">Eadfride</f> 
the eight Bishop of <f type="i">Lindiffarne</f>, (which after was translated 
to <f type="i">Durrham</f>,) and divided according to the antient <f type="i">Canon</f> of 
<f type="i">Eusebius</f>, not into chapters, for <f type="i">Stephen Langton</f>, Archbishop 
of <f type="i">Canturburie</f>, first divided the holy Scriptures into chap&shy;
ters, as <f type="i">Robert Stephan</f> did lately into verse; and thus it is.

<f type="i">Our Father which art in heaven</f>
<term lang="oe">Vren Fader thic arth in heaofnas,</term>

<f type="i">be hallowed thin name. come</f>
<term lang="oe">Sie gehalgud thin noma, to cymeth</term>

<f type="i">thy kingdom. Be thy will so as in</f>
<term lang="oe">thin ric. Sie thin willa sue is in</term>
</page>

<page no="16">
<f type="i">heaven and in earth. Oure lofe</f>
<term lang="oe">heofnas, and in eortho. Vren hlaf</term>

<f type="i">Super-substantiall give vs to day, and</f>
<term lang="oe">ofer wirtlic sel vs to daeg, and</term>

<f type="i">forgive vs debts euaes so we for-</f>
<term lang="oe">forg<emend err="">e</emend>f vs scylda urna, sue we for.</term>

<f type="i">geve debts oures, and do not led</f>
<term lang="oe">gefan scyldgum vrum, and no inlead</term>

<f type="i">vs into temptation. But deliver everyone</f>
<term lang="oe">vsith in custnung. Ah gefrig vrich</term>

<f type="i">from evill</f>.
<term lang="oe">from ifle. Amen.</term>

Some two hundred yeeres after, I finde this somewhat 
varied in two translations.

<term lang="oe">Thu vre fader the eart on heofenum
Si thin nama gehalgod. Cum thin ric.
Si thin willa on eorthan, swa swa on heofo&shy;
num. Syle vs to daeg vrn d&aelig;gthanlican</term>
<f type="i">dayly</f> 
<term lang="oe">hlaf.
And forgif vs vre gyltas</term> 
<f type="i">trespasses</f> 
<term lang="oe">swa, swa we for&shy;
gifath tham the with</term> 
<f type="i">against</f> vs 
<term lang="oe"><f type="i">vs</f> agyltath</term> 
<f type="i">have trespassed</f>. <term lang="oe">And ne
led the vs on costnung, Ac alys vs from
yfle.</term>
<f type="i">Be it so.</f>
<term lang="oe"><f type="i">Si it swa.</f></term>

About an hundred and three score yeeres after, in the 
time of king <f type="i">Henry</f> the second, I find this in time sent from 
</page>

<page no="17">
<f type="i">Rome</f> by Pope <f type="i">Adrian</f> an Englishman, to be taught to the 
people.

<term lang="me"><f type="i">Ure fadyr in heaven rich,
Thy name be halyed everlich:
Thou bring vs thy michell blisse,
Als hit in heaven y-doe,
Evar in yearth beene it also:
That holy bread that lasteth ay,
Thou send it ous this ilke day.
Forgive ous all that we havith don,
As wee forgivet vch other mon:
Ne let ous fall into no founding,
Ac sheild ous fro the fowle thing. Amen</f>.</term>

Neither was there any great variation in the time of king 
<f type="i">Henry</f> the third, as appeereth in this of that age, as I con&shy;
iecture by the Character; 

<term lang="me"><f type="i">Fader that art in heavin blisse,
Thin helge nam it wurth the blisse.
Cumen &amp; mot thi kingdom,
Thin holy will it be all don,
In heaven and in erdh also,
So it shall bin full well Ic tro.
Gif vs all bread on this day,
And forgif vs vre sinnes,
A<emend err="i">s</emend> we do vre wider winnes:
Let vs not in fonding fall,
O ac fro evill thu syld vs all. Amen</f>.</term>

In the time of king <f type="i">Richard</f> the second about a hundred
and odde yeeres after, it was so mollified, that it came to be
thus, as it is in the Translation of <f type="i">Wickliffe</f>, with some La&shy;
tine wordes now inserted, whereas there was not one be&shy;
fore.

<term lang="me"><f type="i">Our fadyr, that art in heaven, halloed be thy name,
thy kingdom com to, be thy will done, so in heaven,</term>
</page>

<page no="18">
<term lang="me">and in erth: gif to vs this day our bread over other
substance: and forgif to vs our dettis, as we forge&shy;
ven to our detters, and leed vs nott into temptation, 
but deliver vs fro evill. Amen</f>.</term>

Hitherto will our sparkefull Youth laugh at their great
grandfathers <f type="i">English</f>, who had more care to do well, than to
speake minion-like, and left more glory to vs by their ex&shy;
ploiting of great actes, than we shall do by our sonnetting.

Great verily was the glory of our tongue before the <f type="i">Nor&shy;
man</f> Conquest in this, that the olde <f type="i">English</f> could expresse
most aptly, all the conceiptes of the minde in their owne
tongue without borrowing from any. As for example:

The holy service of God, which the <f type="i">Latines</f> called <f type="i">Religi&shy;
on</f>, because it knitted the mindes of men together, and most
people of <f type="i">Europe</f> have borrowed the same from them, they
called most significantly <f type="i"><term lang="oe">Fan-fastnes</term></f>, as the one and onely 
assurance and fast anker-holde of our soules health.

The gladsome tidings of our salvation, which the <f type="i">Greekes</f>
called <f type="i"><term lang="gk">Evangelion</term></f>, and other Nations in the same word, they
called <f type="i"><term lang="me">Godspel</term></f>, that is, <f type="i">God speech</f>.

For our <f type="i">Saviour</f>, which wee borrowed from the <f type="i">French</f>,
and they from the <f type="i">Latin <term lang="la">Salvator</term></f>, they called in their owne
word, <f type="i"><term lang="oe">Haelend</term></f> from <f type="i"><term lang="oe">Hael</term></f>, that is, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Salus</term></f>, safetie, which we
retaine still in <f type="i">Al-hael</f>, and <f type="i">Was-hael</f>, that is, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Ave, Salve, Sis 
salvus</term></f>.

They could call the disciples of Christ, <f type="i"><term lang="oe">Leorning Cnihtas</term></f>,
that is, <f type="i">Learning Servitours</f>. For <f type="i"><term lang="oe">Cniht</term></f> which is now a name
of worship, signified with them an <f type="i">Attendant</f>, or servitour.

They could name the Pharises</f> according to the <f type="i">Hebrew,
Sunder-halgens</f>, as holy religious men which had sundred
and severed themselves from other.

The Scribes they could call in their proper significati&shy;
on, as <f type="i">Booke-men, Bocer</f>. So they called parchment which
wee have catcht from the Latine <f type="i"><term lang="la">Pergamenum</term>, <term lang="oe">Boc-fell</term></f> in 
respect of the vse.

So they could call the sacrament <f type="i"><term lang="oe">Haligdome</term></f>, as holy
iudgement. For so it is according as we receive it.
</page>

<page no="19">
They could call <f type="i">Fertilitie</f> and fruitfulnesse of land sig&shy;
nificatively <f type="i"><term lang="oe">Eordes-wela</term></f>, as wealth of the earth.

They could call a <f type="i">Comet</f>, a <f type="i"><term lang="oe">Faxed starre</term></f>; which is all one 
with <f type="i"><term lang="la">Stella Crinita</term></f>, or <f type="i"><term lang="la">Cometa</term></f>. So they did call the iudge&shy;
ment seate <f type="i"><term lang="me">Domesettle</term></f>.

That which we call the <f type="i">Parlament</f> of the <f type="i">French <term lang="fr">Parier</term></f>
to speake, they called a <f type="i"><term lang="me">Witten mot</term></f>, as the meeting and as&shy;
sembly of wise men.

The certaine and inward knowledge of that which is
in our minde, be it good or bad, which in the Latine word
we call <f type="i">Conscience</f>, they called <f type="i"><term lang="me">Inwit</term></f>, as that which they 
did inwardly wit and wote, that is, know certainely.

That in a river which the Latines call <f type="i"><term lang="la">Alveus</term></f>, and <f type="i"><term lang="la">Cana&shy;
lis</term></f>, and from thence most nations of <f type="i">Europe</f> name the <f type="i">Cha&shy;
nel, Kanel, Canale</f>, &amp;c. they properly called the <f type="i"><term lang="me">Streame&shy;
race</term></f>.

Neither in the degrees of kinred they were destitute of
significative woordes; for he whom we of a <f type="i">French</f> &amp; <f type="i">En&shy;
glish</f> compound word call <f type="i">Grandfather</f>, they called <f type="i"><term lang="me">Eald-fa&shy;
der</term></f>, whom we call <f type="i">Great Grandfather</f>, they called <f type="i"><term lang="me">Thirda&shy;
fader</term></f>. So <f type="i"><term lang="la">Proavus</term></f>, which we call <f type="i">Great Great Grandfather</f>, 
they called <f type="i"><term lang="me">Fortha-fader</term></f>, as <f type="i"><term lang="la">Abavus</term>, <term lang="me">Fiftha-fader</term></f>.

An <f type="i">Eunuch</f>, for whome we have no name, but from the
<f type="i">Greekes</f>, they could aptly name <f type="i"><term lang="me">Vnstana</term></f>, that is, without
stones, as we vse <f type="i">Vnspotted</f> for without spotte, <f type="i">Vnlearned</f>, for,
without learning.

A Covetous man whome we so call of the <f type="i">French <term lang="fr">Con&shy;
voitise</term></f>, they truely called <f type="i"><term lang="me">Git-sor</term></f>, as a sore &amp; eagre <f type="i">Getter</f>, and
<f type="i">Gatherer</f>.

That which the Latines call <f type="i"><term lang="la">Abortus</term></f>, and wee in many
wordes, <f type="i">Vntimely Birth</f>, or, <f type="i">Borne before the full time</f>, they 
called <f type="i"><term lang="me">Miss-borne</term></f>.

A <f type="i">Porter</f>, which wee have received from the <f type="i">French</f>,
they could in their own word as significatively call <f type="i"><term lang="me">A Dore&shy;
ward</term></f>.

I could particulate in many more, but this woulde ap&shy;
peare most plentifully, if the labours of the learned Gen&shy; 
</page>

<page no="20">
tlemen Maister <f type="i">Laurence Nowell</f> of <f type="i">Lincolnes Inne</f>, who first 
in our time recalled the studie heereof, Maister <f type="i">William
Lambert</f>, Maister <f type="i">I: Ioscelin</f>, Maister <f type="i">Fr: Tate</f> were once
published. Otherwise it is to bee feared, that devouring
<f type="i">Time</f>, in few yeeres will vtterly swallow it, without hope
of recoverie. 

The alteration and innovation in our tongue as in all o&shy;
thers, hath beene brought in by entrance of Strangers, as
<f type="i">Danes, Normans</f>, and others which have swarmed hither, by
trafficke, for new words as well as for new wares, have al&shy;
waies come in by the tyranne <f type="i">Time</f>, which altereth all vn&shy;
der heaven, by <f type="i">Vse</f>, which swayeth most, and hath an abso&shy;
lute command in words, and by <f type="i">Pregnant wits</f>: specially since
that learning after long banishment, was recalled in the
time of King <f type="i">Henry</f> the eight, it hath beene beautified and
enriched out of other good tongues, partly by enfranchi&shy;
sing and endenizing strange words, partly by refining and
mollifying olde words, partly by implanting new wordes
with artificiall composition, happily containing themselves
within the bounds prescribed by <f type="i">Horace</f>. So that our tong
is (and I doubt not but hath beene) as copious, pithie, and
significative, as any other tongue in <f type="i">Europe</f>: and I hope we
are not yet and shall not heereafter come to that which <f type="i">Se&shy;
neca</f> saw in his time,<f type="i">When mens mindes beginne once to iniure 
themselves to dislike whatsoever is vsuall, is disdained. They af&shy;
fect noveltie in speech, they recall forworne and vncuth words,
they forge new phrases, and that which is newest, is best liked;
there is presumptuous and farre fetching of words. And some
there are which thinke it a grace if their speech doe hover,
and thereby hold the hearer in suspence</f>: you know what fol&shy;
loweth. 

Omitting this, pardon me and thinke me not overbal&shy;
lanced with affection, if I thinke that our <f type="i">English</f> tongue
is (I will not say as sacred as the <f type="i">Hebrew</f>, or as learned as the 
<f type="i">Greeke</f>,) but as fluent as the <f type="i">Latine</f>, as courteous as the <f type="i">Spa&shy;
nish</f>, as courtlike as the <f type="i">French</f>, and as amorous as the <f type="i">Italian</f>,
as some Italianated amorous have confessed. Neither hath 
</page>

<page no="21">
any thing detracted more from the dignitie of our tongue,
than our owne affection of forraine tongues, by admiring,
praising, and studying them above measure: whereas the
wise <f type="i">Romans</f> thought no small part of their honour to con&shy;
sist in the honour of their language, esteeming it a disho&shy;
nour to answer any forraine in his owne language. As for
a long time the English placed in the Borrough townes of
<f type="i">Ireland</f> and <f type="i">Wales</f>, would admit neither Irish nor Welsh a&shy;
mong them. And not long since for the honour of our na&shy;
tive tongue, <f type="i">Henry Fitz-Allan</f> Earle of <f type="i">Arundell</f>, in his tra&shy;
vaile into <f type="i">Italie</f>, and the Lord <f type="i">William Howard</f> of <f type="i">Effing&shy;
ham</f>, in his government of <f type="i">Calice</f>, albeit they were not igno&shy;
rant of other forraine tongues, would answer no strangers
by word or writing, but onely in English. As in this consi&shy;
deration also before them Cardinall <f type="i">Wolsey</f> in his ambas&shy;
sage into <f type="i">France</f>, commaunded all his servaunts to vse no
French, but meere English to the French, in all communica&shy;
tion whatsoever. 

As for the <f type="i">Monosyllables</f> so rife in our tongue which were
not so originally, although they are vnfitting for verses and
measures, yet are they most fit for expressing briefly the first
conceipts of the minde, or <f type="i"><term lang="la">Intentionalia</term></f> as they call them in 
schooles: so that we can set downe more matter in fewer
lines, than any other language. Neither do we or the Welsh
so curtall <f type="i">Latine</f>, that we make all therein <f type="i">Monosyllables</f>, as
<f type="i">Ioseph Scaliger</f> chargeth vs; who in the meane time forget&shy;
teth that his Frenchmen have put in their <f type="i"><term lang="la">Proviso</term></f> in the e&shy;
dict of <f type="i">Pacification</f> in the <f type="i">Grammaticall</f> warre, that they 
might not pronounce <f type="i">Latine</f> distinctly, as the English com&shy;
mon Lawyers obtained then a <f type="i">Reservation</f> that they might
write false <f type="i">Latine</f>, and the Irish not to observe quantitie of 
syllables. I cannot yet but confesse that we have corruptly
contracted most names both of men and places, if they were
of more then two sillables, and thereby hath ensued no lit&shy;
tle obscuritie. 

Whereas our tongue is mixed, it is no disgrace, whenas
all the tongues of <f type="i">Europe</f> doe participate interchangeably 
</page>

<page no="22">
the one of the other, and in the learned tongues, there hath
been like borrowing one from another. Yet is it false which
<f type="i">Gesner</f> affirmeth, that our tongue is the most mixt and cor&shy;
rupt of all other. For if it may please any to compare but the
Lords Prayer in other languages, he shall finde as few <f type="i">La&shy;
tine</f> and borrowed forraine words in ours, as in any other
whatsoever. Notwithstanding the diversitie of Nations
which have swarmed hither, and the practise of the Nor&shy;
mans, who as a monument of their Conquest, would have
yoaked the English vnder their tongue, as they did vnder
their command, by compelling them to teach their children
in schooles nothing but French, by setting downe their
lawes in the Norman-French, and enforcing them most ri&shy;
gorously to pleade and to be impleaded in that tongue one&shy;
ly, for the space of three hundred yeares, vntill K. <f type="i">Edward</f>
the third enlarged them first from that bondage. Since which 
time, our language hath risen by little, and the proverbe pro&shy;
ved vntrue, which so long had beene vsed, <f type="i">Iacke would be a
gentleman, if he could speake any French</f>. 

Heerein is a notable argument of our Ancestors sted&shy;
fastnes in esteeming and retaining their owne tongue. For
as before the Conquest they misliked nothing more in K.
<f type="i">Edward</f> the Confessor, than that he was Frenchified, &amp; ac&shy;
counted the desire of forraine language, then to be a fore&shy;
token of the bringing in of forraine powers, which indeede
happened. In like manner after the Conquest, notwithstan&shy;
ding those enforcements of the Normans in supplanting it,
and the nature of men, which is most pliable with a curious
iolitie to fashion &amp; frame themselves according to the man&shy;
ners, attyre, and language of the Conquerours. Yet in all
that long space of 300. yeares, they intermingled very few
French-Norman words, except some termes of law, hun&shy;
ting, hawking, and dicing, whenas wee within these 60.
yeares, have incorporated so many Latine and French, as
the third part of ou<emend err="r!">r</emend> tongue consisteth now in them. But
like themselves, continue still those old Englishmen which
were planted in <f type="i">Ireland</f>, in <f type="i">Fingall</f>, &amp; the Country of <f type="i">Weys&shy; 
</page>

<page no="23">
ford</f>, in the time of K. <f type="i">Henry</f> the second, who yet still conti&shy;
nue their antient attyre and tongue, in somuch that an En&shy;
glish gentleman not long since, sent thither in Comm<emend err="">i</emend>ssion
among them, said that he would quickly vnderstand the
Irish, when they spake the antient English. So that our An&shy;
cestors seemed in part as iealous of their native language, as
those <f type="i">Britans</f> which passed hence into <f type="i">Armorica</f> in <f type="i">France</f>,
and marrying strange women there, did cut out their tongs,
lest their children should corrupt their language with their
motters tongues, or as the <f type="i">Germans</f> which have most of all
Nations opposed themselves against all innovations in ha&shy;
bite, and language. 

Whereas the <f type="i">Hebrew Rabbines</f> say, and that truly, that
Nature hath given man five instruments for the pronoun&shy;
cing of all letters, the lips, the teeth, the tongue, the palate,
and throate; I will not denie but some among vs do pro&shy;
nounce more fully, some flatly, some broadly, and no few
mincingly, offending in defect, excesse, or change of letters,
which is rather to be imputed to the persons and their edu&shy;
cation, than to the language. Whenas generally wee pro&shy;
nounce by the confession of strangers, as sweetely, smooth&shy;
ly, and moderately, as any of the Northerne Nations of the
world, who are noted to soupe their words out of the throat
with fat and full spirits.

This variety of pronuntiation hath brought in some di&shy;
versitie of Orthographie, and heere-vpon Sir <f type="i">Iohn Price</f>, to
the derogation of our tongue, and glorie of his <f type="i">Welsh</f>, re&shy;
porteth that a sentence spoken by him in <f type="i">English</f>, &amp; penned
out of his mouth by foure good Secretaries, severally, for tri&shy;
al of our Orthography, was so set downe by them, that they
all differed one from the other in many letters: whereas so
many <f type="i">Welsh</f> writing the same likewise in their tongue vari&shy;
ed not in any one letter at all. Well, I will not derogate from
the good Knights credite; yet it hath beene seene where
tenne English writing the same sentence, have all so con&shy;
curred, that among them all there hath beene no other
difference, than the adding, or omitting once or twice of 
</page>

<page no="24">
our silent <f type="i">E</f>, in the end of some wordes. As for the <f type="i">Welsh</f>,
I could never happen on two of that Nation together, that
would acknowledge that they could write their owne lan&shy;
guage. 

Sir <f type="i">Thomas Smith</f> her Maiesties secretarie not long since,
a man of great learning and iudgement, occasioned by som
vncertainty of our Orthographie, though it seeme groun&shy;
ded vpon <f type="i">Sound, Reason</f>, and <f type="i">Custome</f>, laboured to reduce
it to certaine heads; Seeing that whereas of Necessity there
must be so many letters in every tongue, as there are sim&shy;
ple and single sounds, that the Latine letters were not suf&shy;
ficient to expresse all our simple sounds. Therefore he wi&shy;
shed that we should have A short, and A long, bicause <f type="i">a</f> in 
<f type="i">Man</f>, and in <f type="i">M&acirc;n</f> of horse hath different sounds; E long as
in <f type="i">M&ecirc;n</f> moderate, and <f type="i">e</f> short as in <f type="i">Men</f>, and an English <f type="i">e</f>
as in <f type="i">w&eacute;e, thee, he, me</f>: I long, and I short, as in Bi, <f type="i">per</f>,
and B&icirc;, <f type="i">emere</f>: O short, and O long, as in sm&#333;k of a wo&shy;
man, and smok of the fire: V long, as in B&ucirc;t, <f type="i">Ocrea</f>, and 
V short, as in B&#363;t, <f type="i">Sed</f>: and v or y <f type="i">Greeke</f>, as slu, nu, tru.
For consonants he would have C be never vsed but for Ch,
as it was among the olde English, and K in all other words;
for Th, he would have the <f type="i">Saxon</f> letter <f type="i">Thorne</f>, which was
a D with a dash through the head, or <term lang="oe">&thorn;</term>; for I consonant the
<f type="i">Saxon</f> &#439;, as <f type="i">&#439;et</f>, not Ieat for Ieat-stone, <f type="i">&#439;ay</f> for Iay:
<f type="i">Q</f>, if he were king of the A, B, C, should be putte to the
horne, and banished; and <f type="i">Ku</f> in his place, as <f type="i">Kuik</f>, not 
<f type="i">quik, Kuarel</f>, not <f type="i">Quarel: Z</f>; he would have vsed for the 
softer <f type="i">S</f>, or eth, and es, as <f type="i">d&icirc;z</f> for dieth, <f type="i">liz</f> for lies, and
the same <f type="i">S</f> inverted for <f type="i">sh</f>, as <f type="i">Sal</f> for <f type="i">shall, fles</f> for <f type="i">flesh</f>.
This briefly I have set you downe his devise, which albeit
<f type="i">Sound</f> and <f type="i">Reason</f> seemed to countenance, yet that Tyranne 
<f type="i">Custome</f> hath so confronted, that it will never be admit&shy;
ted.

If it be any glorie which the <f type="i">French</f> and <f type="i">Dutch</f> do brag
of, that many wordes in their tongues doe not differ from
the <f type="i">Greeke</f>, I can shew you as many in the <f type="i">English</f>; where&shy;
of I will give you a few for a taste, as they have offred them&shy; 
</page>

<page no="25">
selves in reading; but withall, I trust you will not gather
by consequence, that wee are descended from the <f type="i">Gr&aelig;ci&shy;
ans</f>. Who dooth not see an identitie in these wordes, as if
the one descended from the other.

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="gk">&#x0395;&#x03BB;&#x03B1;&#x03C3;&#x03C3;&#x03C9;&#x03BD;</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="less(adv)">lesse.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="gk">&#x03A3;&#x03BA;&#x03AF;&#x03C0;&#x03C4;&#x03B5;&#x03C9;</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="scoff(v)">to scoffe.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="gk">&#x03A3;&#x03C4;&#x03C1;&#x03CE;&#x03BD;&#x03C5;&#x03BC;&#x03B9;</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="strew(v)">to strowe.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="gk">&#x03A7;&#x03AC;&#x03C1;&#x03BC;&#x03B7;</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="skirmish(n)">a skirmish.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="gk">&#x039A;&#x03C5;&#x03C1;&#x03B9;&#x03B1;&#x03BA;&#x1F20;</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="church(n)">a Church.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="gk">&#x03A0;&#x03BF;&#x03C4;&#x03AE;&#x03C1;&#x03B9;&#x03BF;&#x03BD;</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="pot(n)">a potte.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="gk">&#x039C;&#x03C5;&#x03C3;&#x03C4;&#x03AC;&#x03C7;&#x03B5;&#x03C2;</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="mustache(n)">Mustaches.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form  lang="gk">&#x0398;&#x03CD;&#x03C1;&#x03B1;</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="door(n)">a doore.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="gk">&#x1F4B;&#x03BB;&#x03BA;&#x03B1;&#x03C2;</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="hulk(n)">a hulke.</xpln></wordentry>
 
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="gk">&#x039A;&#x03B1;&#x03BA;&#x03AC;&#x03C9;</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="to you know what(v)">to you know what.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

With many more
if a man would be so idle to gather them with <f type="i">Budans, Baisi&shy;
us, Iunius, Pichardus</f>, and others. 

Heereby may be seene the originall of some english
words, and the <f type="i">Etymology</f> or reason whence many other are
derived, beside them alreadie specified may as well be found
in our tongue, as in the learned tongues, although hardly;
for that heerein as in other tongues, the truth heth hidden
and is not easilie found, as both <f type="i">Varro</f> and <f type="i">Isidor</f> do acknow&shy;
ledge. But an indifferent man may iudge that our name of
the most divine power, God, is better derived from Good,
the chiefe attribute of God, than <f type="i">Deus</f> from <term lang="gk">&#x0394;&#x03B5;&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</term>, because
God is to be feared. So <f type="i">Winter</f> from Winde, <f type="i">Sommer</f> from
the Sonne, <f type="i">Lent</f> from springing, because it falleth in the
spring, for which our Progenitours the <f type="i">Germans</f>, vse <f type="i">Glent</f>. 
The feast of Christs Rising, <f type="i">Easter</f>, from the old world <f type="i">East</f>,
which we now vse for the place of the rising of the <f type="i">Sunne,
Sayle</f> as the <f type="i">Sea-haile, Windor</f> or <f type="i">Windowe</f>, as a doore against
the winde, <f type="i">King</f> from <f type="i">Conning</f>, for so our Great grandfa&shy;
thers called them, which one word imployeth two most 
</page>

<page no="27">```
important matters in a Governour, <f type="i">Power</f> and <f type="i">Skill</f>, and
many other better answering in sound and sence, then those
of the <f type="i">Latines, <term lang="la">Frater quasi fer&egrave; alter, Tempestas quasi Tem&shy;
pus pestis, Caput &agrave; capiendo, Digiti quia decent&egrave;r iuncti, Cura 
quia cor vrit, Peccare quasi pedam capere</term></f>. 

<f type="i">Dionysius</f> a Greeke coyner of <f type="i">Etymologyes</f> is commended
by <f type="i">Athen&aelig;us</f>, in his supper-gulls, table-talkers, or <f type="i">Deipnoso&shy;
phist&aelig</f>;, for making mowse-traps of <f type="i">Musteria</f>: and verily if
that be commendable, the Mint-masters of our <f type="i">Etymilogies</f>,
deserve no lesse commendation: for they have merily for&shy;
ged <f type="i">Mony</f> from <f type="i">My-hony, Flatter</f> from flie-at-her, <f type="i">Shovell</f>
from shove-full, <f type="i">Mayd</f> as my ayd <f type="i">Mastiefe</f> as Mase-thiefe, 
<f type="i">Staffe as Stay of, Beere, Be-heere, Symony See-mony, Stirrup,
a Sayre-vp</f>, &amp;c.

This merry playing with words too much vsed by some
hath occasioned a great and high personage, to say, that as
the <f type="i">Italian</f> tongue is fit for courting, the <f type="i">Spanish</f> for treating,
the <f type="i">French</f> for trafficke; so the <f type="i">English</f> is most fit for trifling
and toying. And so doth <f type="i">Giraldus Cambrensis</f> seem to think
whenas in his time he saith, the <f type="i">English</f> and <f type="i">Welsh</f> delighted
much in licking the letter and clapping together of Ag&shy;
nominations. But now will I conclude this trifling discourse
with a true tale out of an antient Historian.

Of the effectuall power of words, great disputes have
beene of great wits in all ages; the <f type="i">Pithagoreans</f> extolled it,
the impious Iewes ascribed all miracles to a name which
was ingravened in the revestiarie of the Temple, watched
by two brazen dogges, which one stale away and enseamed
it in his thigh, as you may reade in <f type="i">Osorius de Sapientia</f>, and
the like in <f type="i">Rabi Hamas Speculation</f>: and strange it is what 
<f type="i">Samonicus Serenus</f> ascribed to the word ABRADACABRA, a&shy;
gainst agues. But there was one true English word of as
great, if not greater force than them all, now out of all vse
and will be thought for sound barbarous; but therefore of
more efficacie (as it pleaseth <f type="i">Porphyrie</f>) and in signification
it signifieth as it seemeth, no more then abiect, base minded,
false harted, coward, or nidget. Yet it hath levied Armies, 
</page>

<page no="28">
and subdued rebellious enemies; and that I may hold you
no longer, it is <f type="i">Niding</f>. For when there was a daungerous
rebellion against King <f type="i">William Rufus</f>, and <f type="i">Rochester</f> Ca&shy;
stle then the most important &amp; strongest fort of this Realm
was stowtly kept against him, after that he had but proclai&shy;
med that his subiects should repaire thither to his Campe,
vpon no other penaltie, but that whosoever refused to come,
should be reputed a <f type="i">Niding</f>: they swarmed to him immedi&shy;
atly from all sides in such numbers, that he had in few daies
an infinite Armie, and the rebells therewith weere so terri&shy;
fied, that they forthwith yeelded. While I runne on in this
course of English tongue, rather respecting matter then
words, I forget that I may be charged by the minion refi&shy;
ners of English, neither to write State-English, Court-En&shy;
glish, nor Secretarie-English, and verily I acknowledge it.
Sufficient it is for me, if I have waded hither-vnto in the
fourth kinde, which is plaine English, leaving to such as are
compleat in all, to supply whatsoever remaineth. 
</section>

<section type="Christian Names">
<heading>Christian Names.</heading> 

NAmes called in Latine <f type="i"><term lang="la">Nomina quasi
Notamina</term></f>, were first imposed for the
distinction of persons, which wee call
now Christian names: After for diffe&shy;
rence of families which wee call Sur&shy;
names, and have beene especially re&shy;
spected as whereon the glorie and cre&shy;
dite of men is grounded, and by which 
</page>

<page no="29">
the same is convayed to the knowledge of posteritie. 

Every person had in the beginning one onely proper
name, as among the Iewes, <f type="i">Adam, Ioseph, Solomon</f>; among the 
&AElig;giptians, <f type="i">Anubis, Amasis, Busuris</f>; among the Chald&aelig;ans, 
<f type="i">Ninus</f>, <f type="i">Ninias, Semiramis</f>; among the Medians, <f type="i">Astiages, Bar&shy;
danes, Arbaces</f>; among the Grecians, <f type="i">Diomedes, Vlisses, Ore&shy;
stes</f>; among the Romans, <f type="i">Romulus, Remus, Faustulus</f>; among
the old Gaules, <f type="i">Litavicus, Cavarillus, Divitiacus</f>; among the
Germans, <f type="i">Ariovistus, Arminius, Nassua</f>; among the Britans, 
<f type="i">Cassibellin, Caratac, Calgac</f>; among the antient English, 
<f type="i">Hengest</f>, <f type="i">&AElig;lla, Kenrie</f>, likewise among all other Nations, ex&shy;
cept the savages of Mount <f type="i">Atlas</f> in <f type="i">Barbary</f>, which were re&shy;
ported to be both namelesse and dreamelesse. 

The most antient Nation of the Iewes gave the name
at the Circumcision the eight day after the nativitie; the
Romans to females the same day, to males the ninth day,
which they called <f type="i"><term lang="la">Dies lustricus,</term></f> as it were the cleansing day;
vpon which day they solemnized a feast called <f type="i">Nominalia</f>,
and as <f type="i">Tertulian</f> noteth, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Fata scribenda advocabantur</term></f>, that is,
as I conceive, their nativitie was set. At what time other
Nations in auntient times gave names I have not read:
but since Christianitie, most Nations for the time followed
the Iewes, celebrating baptisme the eight day after the birth,
onely our Ancestours in this Realme, vntill latter time bap&shy;
tized, and gave name the very birth day, or next day after,
following therein the counsell of S. <f type="i">Cyprian</f>, in his third E&shy;
pistle <f type="i"><term lang="la">Ad Fidum</term></f>. But the Polonians gave name in the
seaventh yeare, at which time they did first cut their chil&shy;
drens haire. 

The first imposition of Names was grounded vpon so
many occasions, as were hard to be specified, but the most
common in most antient times among all nations, as well as
the <f type="i">Hebrewes</f>, was vpon future good hope conceived by
parents of their children, in which you might see their first
and principall wishes toward them. Whereupon Saint <f type="i">Hie&shy;
rome</f> saith, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Votiva &amp; quasi ob virtutis auspicium imponuntur 
vocabula hominibus, &amp; appellativa vertuntur inpropria, sicut a&shy;</term> 
</page>

<page no="30">
<term lang="la">pud Latines, Victor, Probus, Castus</term></f>, &amp;c. And such hopefull
luckie names called by <f type="i">Cicero</f>, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Bona nomina,</term></f> by <f type="i">Tacitus, <term lang="la">Fan&shy;
sta nomina</term></f> were ever first enrolled and ranged in the <f type="i">Roman</f>
Musters; first called out to serve at the first sacrifices, in the
foundation of <f type="i">Colonies</f>, as <f type="i">Statorius, <term lang="la">Faustus, Valerius,</term></f> which
implied the persons to be stowt, happy, and valorous. As
contrariwise <f type="i"><term lang="la">Atrius Vmber</term></f> is accounted in <f type="i">Livie, <term lang="la">abominandi
ominis nomen,</term></f> an abhominable name, for that it participa&shy;
ted in signification with dismall darkenes, dead ghosts, and
shadowes. And you remember what <f type="i">Plautus</f> saieth of one
whose name was <f type="i">Lyco</f>, that is, a <f type="i">Greedy Woolfe</f>; 

<f type="i"><term lang="la">Vosmet nunc facite coniecturam c&aelig;ter&ugrave;m
Quid id sit hominis, cui Lyco nomen siet.</term></f>

Yea such names were thought so happy and so fortu&shy;
nate, that in the time of <f type="i">Galienus</f> one <f type="i">Regilianus</f>, which com&shy;
manded in <f type="i">Illyricum</f>, got the Empire there, only in favour
of 
<emend err="of"></emend> his name. For when it was demanded at a supper from
whence <f type="i">Regilianus</f> was derived, one answered, <f type="i"><term lang="la">&agrave; Regno</term></f>, an&shy;
other beganne to decline <f type="i"><term lang="la">Rex, Regis, Regi, Regilianus</term></f>; where&shy;
at the souldiers (which in all actions are forward.) beganne
with acclamation, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Ergo potest Rex esse, Ergo potest regere,
Deus tibi regis nomen imposuit:</term></f> and so invested him with im&shy;
periall roabes. In this Isle also at <f type="i">Silcaster</f> in Hamshire, <f type="i">Con&shy;
stantinus</f> a militarie man of some reputation, in hope of his
luckie name, and that he would proue another <f type="i">Constantinus
Magnus</f> to the good of the people, was by the Britan Ar&shy;
mie proclaimed emperour against <f type="i">Honorius</f>: who exploy&shy;
ted great matters in his owne person in <f type="i">Gallia</f>, and by his
son in <f type="i">Spaine</f>. So in former times the name of <f type="i">Antoninus</f> in 
remembrance of <f type="i">Antoninus Pius</f>, was so amiable among the
Romans, as he was supposed vnfit for the empire, who
bare not that name, vntill <f type="i">Antoninus Elagabalus</f> with his fil&shy;
thie vices distained the same. We reade also that two Am&shy;
bassadours were sent out of <f type="i">France</f> into <f type="i">Spaine</f>, to King <f type="i">Al&shy;
phonse</f> the 9, to demand one of the daughters that he begat 
of the daughter of King <f type="i">Henry</f> the second of <f type="i">England</f>, to be
married to their Soveraigne, King <f type="i">Lewes</f> the eight: one of 
</page>

<page no="31">
these Ladies was very beautifull called <f type="i">Vrraca</f>, the other
not so beautifull, but named <f type="i">Blanche</f>. When they were pre&shy;
sented to the Ambassadours, all men held it as a matter re&shy;
solved that the choyce would light vpon <f type="i">Vrraca</f>, as the elder
and fairer: But the Ambassadours enquiring each of their
names, tooke offence at <f type="i">Urraca</f>, and made choyce of the
Lady <f type="i">Blanche</f>, saying, That her name would be better re&shy;
ceived in <f type="i">France</f> than the other, as signifying faire and beau&shy;
tifull, according to the verse made to her honour. 

<f type="i"><term lang="la">Candida, candescens candore, &amp; cordis, &amp; oris.</term></f> 

So that the greatest Philosopher <f type="i">Plato</f> might seeme, not
without cause, to advise men to be carefull in giving faire
and happie names: as the <f type="i">Pythagoreans</f> affirmed the mindes,
actions, and successes of men to be according to their <f type="i">Fate,
Genius</f>, and <f type="i">Name</f>. One also well observeth that these seven 
things; Virtue, good Parentage, Wealth, Dignity or Office,
good Presence, a good Christian name, with a gratious Sur&shy;
name, and seemely attire, doe especially grace and adorne a
man. And accordingly saieth <f type="i">Panormitan; <term lang="la">Ex bono nomine
oritur bona pr&aelig;sumptio.</term></f> As the common Proverb, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Bonum
nomen, bonum omen.</term></f>

The divell neverthelesse who alwayes maligneth God
and goodnesse, wrought by the cruelty of <f type="i">Valens</f> the em&shy;
perour the destruction of many men of worth, who hadde
happy names beginning with <f type="i">Theo</f> signifying God, as <f type="i">The&shy;
odorus, Theodubis, Theodoretus, Theodosius</f>, &amp;c. For that di&shy;
verse curious companions had found by the falling of a ring
magically prepared, vpon those letters onely of all the <f type="i">Al&shy;
phabet</f>, graven in a Charger of sundry mettals, and set vpon
a Laurell trivet; that one who had his name beginning with
<f type="i">Theod</f>, should succeede in the empire, which was verified
in <f type="i">Theodosius</f> not long after. 

In times of Christianity the names of most holy and ver&shy;
tuous persons, and of their most worthy progenitors were
given to stir vp men to the imitation of them, whose names
they bare. But succeeding ages (little regarding saint <f type="i">Chry&shy;
sostoms</f> admonition to the contrary<emend err=",">)</emend> have recalled prophane 
</page>

<page no="32">
names, so as now <f type="i">Diana, Cassandra, Hyppolitus, Venus, Lais</f>,
names of vnhappy disastre are as rife some-where, as ever
they were in <f type="i">Paganisme</f>: Albeit in our late reformation, some
of good consideration have brought in <f type="i">Zachary, Malachy,
Iosias</f>, &amp;c. as better agreeing with our faith, but without 
contempt of our countrey names (as I hope) which have
both good and gratious significations, as shal appeare here&shy;
after. 

Whereas in late yeeres Surnames have beene given for
Christian names, among vs, and no where else in Christen&shy;
dome; although many dislike it, for that great inconveni&shy;
ence will ensue: neverthelesse it seemeth to proceede from
hearty good-will and affection of the Godfathers to show
their love, or from a desire to continue and propagate their
owne names to succeeding ages. And is in no wise to be dis&shy;
liked, but rather approoved in those which matching with
heires generall of worshipful antient families have given
those names to their heires, with a mindefull and thankefull
regard of them, as we have now, <f type="i">Pickering Wotton, Grevill 
Varney, Bassingburne Gawdy, Culthorp Parker, Pecsall Brocas,
Fuz-Raulfe Chamberlaine</f>, who are the heires of <f type="i">Pickering,
Grevill Bassingburn, Grevill, Calthorp</f>, &amp;c. For beside the con&shy;
tinuation of the name, we see that the selfe name, yea &amp; som&shy;
time the similitude of names doth kindle sparkles of love
and liking among meere strangers.

Neither can I beleeve a waiward olde man, which would
say, that the giving of Surnames for Christian names first
began in the time of king <f type="i">Edward</f> the sixt, by such as would
be Godfathers, when they were more then halfe fathers,
and thereupon would have perswaded some to change such
names at the Confirmation. Which (that I may note by
the way) is vsuall in other countries, as wee remember two
sonnes of king <f type="i">Henry</f> the second of <f type="i">France</f>, christned by the
names of <f type="i">Alexander</f> and <f type="i">Hercules</f>, chaunged them at their
Confirmation into <f type="i">Henry</f> and <f type="i">Francis</f>.

But two Christian names are rare in <f type="i">England</f>, and I only
remember now his Maiesty who was named <f type="i">Charles Iames</f>, 
</page>

<page no="33">
as the Prince his sonne <f type="i">Henry Frederic</f>; and among private
men, <f type="i">Thomas Maria Wingfield</f>, and sir <f type="i">Thomas Posthumus
Hobby</f>. Although it is common in <f type="i">Italie</f>, to adioyne the
name of some Saint, in a kinde of devotion to the Christi&shy;
an name, as <f type="i">Iohannes Baptista Spinalu, Iohannes Franciscus
Borhomeus, Marcus Antonius Flaminius</f>: and in <f type="i">Spaine</f> to 
adde the name of the Saint on whose day the childe was
borne. 

If that any among vs have named their children <f type="i"><term lang="la">Remedi&shy;
am amoris, Imago s&aelig;culi,</term></f> or with such like names, I knowe
some will thinke it more then a vanitie, as they do but lit&shy;
tle better of the new names, <f type="i">Free-gift, Reformation, Earth,
Dust, Ashes, Delivery, More fruite, Tribulation, The Lord is
neare, More triall, Discipline, Ioy againe, From above</f>: which
have lately beene given by some to their children with no
evill meaning, but vpon some singular and precise conceit.
That I may omit another more vaine absurditie, in giving
names and surnames of men, yea and of the best families
to dogges, beares, and horses. Whenas wee reade it was
thought a capitall crime in <f type="i">Pomposiamas</f> for calling his base 
bondslaves by the name of grand captaines. Here I might
remember how some mislike the giving of parents names
successively to their heires, for that if they should be forced
to proove descent, it will be hard to proove the <f type="i">Doner</f> and 
the <f type="i">Done</f> in <f type="i">Formedon</f>, and to distinguish the one from the
other. 

It were impertinent to note heere, that destinies were
superstitiously by <f type="i">Onomantin</f> desciphered out of names, as
though the names and natures of men were sutable, and fa&shy;
tall necessitie concurred heerein with voluntary motion, in
giving the name, according to that of <f type="i">Ausonius</f> to <f type="i">Probus</f>.

<f type="i"><term lang="la">Qualem cravit moribus,
Iussit vocari nomine.
Mundi supremus arbiter.</term></f>

And after, where he playeth with bibbing mother <f type="i">Me&shy;
ro&ecirc;</f>, as thogh she were so named, bicause she would to drink 
</page>

<page no="34">
meere wine without water, or as he pleasantly calles it <f type="i"><term lang="la">Me&shy;
rum Merum;</term></f> for as he saieth; 

<f type="i"><term lang="la">Qui primus Mero&ecirc; nomen tibi condidit, ille
Thesida nomen condidit Hippolito.
Nam divinare est, nomen componere, qu&ograve;d sit
Fortuna, morum, vel nocis indicium.</term></f>

For <f type="i">Hyppolitus</f> the sonne of <f type="i">Theseus</f> was torne in peeces
by his coach horses, according to his name. So <f type="i">Agamemnon</f>,
signified he should linger long before <f type="i">Troy. Piramus</f> that
he should be redeemed out of bondage in his childehoode,
<f type="i">Tantalus</f>, that he should be most wretched, because
<term lang="gk">&#x0391;&#x03B3;&#x03B1;&#x03BD; &#x03BC;&#x03AD;&#x03BD;&#x03C9;&#x03BD;</term>, in the one, and <term lang="gk">&#x03A0;&#x03C1;&#x03AF;&#x03B1;&#x03BC;&#x03B5;&#x03BD;&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</term>, in the other, and <term lang="gk">&#x03A4;&#x03B1;&#x03BB;&#x03AC;&#x03BD;&#x03C4;&#x03B1;&#x03C4;&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</term>
in the third implieth such accidents vnto them. Hither al&shy;
so may be referred that of <f type="i">Claudius Rutilius</f>.

<f type="i"><term lang="la">Nominibus certis credam decurrere mores?
Moribus aut poti&ugrave;s nomina certa dari?</term></f>

But to confront Poet with Poet, our good <f type="i">Epigramma&shy;
ticall</f> Poet, olde <f type="i">Godfrey</f> of <f type="i">Winchester</f> thinketh no ominous
forspeaking to lie in names, in that to <f type="i">Faustus</f>: 

<f type="i"><term lang="la">Mult&ugrave;m Fauste tua de nobilitate superbis,
Quod<expand type="q,">que</expn> bono Faustus omine nomen habes,
Sed nullum nomen momenti, sit lic&egrave;t omen.</term></f>

Memorable is that which may be observed out of hi&shy;
stories, how that men of the selfe same name have begun
and ended great states and empires: as <f type="i">Cyrus</f> the sonne of 
<f type="i">Cambises</f> beganne the Persian Monarchy, <f type="i">Cyrus</f> the sonne
of <f type="i">Darius</f> ruinated the same. <f type="i">Darius</f> the sonne of <f type="i">Histaspes</f>
restored it. And againe, <f type="i">Darius</f> the sonne of <f type="i">Arsamis</f> vt&shy;
terly overthrew it. <f type="i">Philip</f> the sonne of <f type="i">Amintas</f> especially
enlarged the kingdome of <f type="i">Macedonia, Philip</f> the sonne of 
<f type="i">Antigonus</f> wholy lost the same. <f type="i">Augustus</f> was the first e&shy;
stablished emperour of <f type="i">Rome, Augustulus</f> the last. <f type="i">Constan&shy;
tinus Magnus</f> borne in this Isle first beganne the Empire of 
<f type="i">Constantinople, Constantinus</f> the last left it to the Turkes, and 
vtterly lost the same, &amp;c.
</page>

<page no="35">
Such like curious observations bred the superstitious
kinde of Divination called <f type="i">Onomantia</f>, condemned by the
last generall Counsell, by which the <f type="i">Pithagoreans</f> iudged
the even number of vowells in names to signifie imperfe&shy;
ctions in the left sides of men, and the odde number in the
right. By this <f type="i">Augustus</f> the emperour encouraged himselfe,
and conceived good hope of victory, whenas the night be&shy;
fore the sea-battell at <f type="i">Actium</f>, the first man hee mette was a
poore wayfaring man driving his asse before him, whose
name when hee demaunded, he answered, <f type="i">Eutyches</f>, that
is, <f type="i">Happy man</f>; and that his asses name was <f type="i">Nicon</f>, that is,
<f type="i">Victor</f>. In which place when he accordingly had obtained
the victory, he builded the Cittie <f type="i">Nicopolis</f>, that is, <f type="i">the citty 
of Victory</f>, and there erected brasen Images of the man and
his asse. By this <f type="i">Theodatus</f> king of the <f type="i">Gothes</f>, when he was 
curious to knowe the successe of his warres against the <f type="i">Ro&shy;
mans</f>, an <f type="i">Onomanticall</f> or <f type="i">Name-wisard</f> Iew willed him to
shut vp a number of swine in little hog-sties, and to give
some of them Roman names, to other Gotish names, with
severall markes, and there to leave them to a certaine day;
At the day appointed, the king with the Iew repaired to
the hog-sties, where they found them only dead to whom
they had given the Gotish names, and those alive to whom
they had given the Roman names, but yet with their bris&shy;
sels more then halfe shed. Whereupon the Iew foretolde,
that the <f type="i">Gothes</f> should wholy be discomfited, and the <f type="i">Ro&shy;
mans</f> should loose a great part of their forces. By this <f type="i">Vespa&shy;
sian</f> was incouraged to take vpon him the empire, when
comming to the Temple of <f type="i">Serapis</f> at <f type="i">Alexandria</f>, and be&shy;
ing there alone at his devotion, he sodainly sawe in a vision,
one <f type="i">Basilides</f>, a noble man of <f type="i">&AElig;gypt</f>, who was then foure 
score miles off. Vpon which name of <f type="i">Basilides</f> derived from 
<f type="i">Basileus</f>, signifying a King, hee assured himselfe of royaltie,
and the empire which hee then complotted for. As con&shy;
cerning this <f type="i">Onomantia</f> a German lately set foorth a Table,
which I wish had beene suppressed, for that the divell by
such vanities, doth abuse the credulitie of youth to greater 
</page>

<page no="36">
matters, and sometimes to their owne destructions. 

I can not tell how you would like it if I should but re&shy;
member how the <f type="i">Greekes</f> superstitiously iudged them more
happy, in whose names the numerall letters added toge&shy;
ther made the greater summe, and therefore <f type="i">Achilles</f> for&shy;
sooth must needes vanquish <f type="i">Hector</f>, because the numerall
Greeke letters rose to a greater number in his name then in
the others. Or how the amorous <f type="i">Romans</f> kissed the Cup
with a health so often at their meetings, as there were let&shy;
ters in their Mistresse names, according to that of merry
<f type="i">Martiall</f> of his two wenches, <f type="i">Navia</f> which had six letters, 
and <f type="i">Iustina</f> that had seaven in her name; 

<f type="i"><term lang="la">Navia sex cyathis, septem Iustina bibatur.</term></f>

Our Nation was farre from those and such curious toies;
therefore heere will I overpasse them, and set downe <f type="i">Alpha&shy;
betically</f> the names which wee now call Christian names,
most vsuall to the English Nation, with their significations.
For this is to be tak<expan type="e+_">en</expan> as a granted veritie, that names among
all nations and tongues (as I partly noted before) as signi&shy;
ficative and not vaine senselesse sounds. Among the <f type="i">He&shy;
brewes</f> it is certaine out of sacred Scriptures, S. <f type="i">Hierome</f>, and
<f type="i">Philo</f> likewise among the <f type="i">Greekes, Romans, German, French</f>, 
&amp;c. yea among the barbarous Turks, for with them <f type="i">Maho&shy;
met</f> signifieth glorified or laudable, <f type="i">Homar</f> lively, <f type="i">Abdalla</f> 
Gods servant, <f type="i">Seliman</f> peaceable, <f type="i">Agmad</f> good, <f type="i">Haniza</f> rea&shy;
die. <f type="i">Neama</f> pleasant. And the savages of <f type="i">Hispaniola</f> and all
<f type="i">America</f>, name their children in their owne languages, <f type="i">Gli&shy;
stering light, Sunne bright, Gold-bright, Fine gold, Sweete, Rich 
feather</f>, &amp;c. as they of <f type="i">Congo</f>, by names of birdes, pretious
stones floures, &amp;c. 

So that it were grosse ignorance and to no small reproach, 
of our Progenitours, to thinke their names onely nothing
significative, because that in the daily alteration of our tong,
the signification of them is lost, or not commonly knowne,
which yet I hope to recover, and to make in some part
knowne, albeit they cannot easily and happily be transla&shy; 
</page>

<page no="37"> 
ted because as <f type="i">Porphyrie</f> noteth; Barbarous names (as hee
termeth them,) were very emphaticall and very short. But
in all the significations of these names, you shall see the good
and hopefull respects which the devisers of the names had,
that there is an <f type="i">Orthetes</f> or certitude of names among all
Nations according to <f type="i">Plato</f>, and thereby perceive that many
were translated out of the Greeke and Latine. With all we
may make this fruit by consideration of our names, which
have good, hopefull, and luckie significations, that accor&shy;
dingly we do carrie and conforme our selves; so that wee
faile not to be answerable to them, but be <f type="i"><term lang="la">Nostri nominis
homines,</term></f> and <term lang="gk">&#x03C2;&#x03B5;&#x03C1;&#x03CE;&#x03BD;&#x03C5;&#x03BC;&#x03BF;&#x03B9;</term>, as <f type="i">Severus, Probus</f>, and <f type="i">Aureolus</f> are
called <f type="i"><term lang="la">Sui nominis imperatores.</term></f> And accordingly it seemeth
to have beene the manner at giving of names, to wish the
children might performe and discharge their names, as
when <f type="i">Gunthram</f> King of the French, named <f type="i">Clatharius</f> at
the font, he said; <f type="i"><term lang="la">Crescat puer, &amp; huius sit nominis executer.</term></f> 

But before I proceed farther, this is to be noted. In most
antient times the Britans had heere their peculiar names,
for the most part taken from colours, (for they vsed to paint
themselves) which are now lost, or remaine among the
Welsh. Afterward they tooke Roman names when they
were Provincialls, which either remaine corrupted among
them, or were extinguished in the greatest part of the
Realme, after the entrance of the English <f type="i">Saxons</f>, who
brought in their German names, as <f type="i"><term lang="oe">Cridda, Ponda, Oswald,
Edward, Vchtred, Edmund</term></f>, &amp;c. Then to say nothing of the
Danes, who no doubt brought in their names, as <f type="i"><term lang="da">Suayn, Ha&shy;
rold, Knute</term></f>, &amp;c. The Normans conquest brought in other
German names, for they originally vsed the German tong,
as <f type="i">William, Henry, Richard, Robert, Hughe, Roger</f>, &amp;c. as the 
Greeke names, <f type="i"><term lang="gkt">Ablabius</term>, i</f>. Innocent, <f type="i"><term lang="gkt">Aspasious</term> i</f>. Delightful, 
<f type="i"><term lang="gkt">Bo&euml;thius, Symmachus</term>. i</f>. Helper, <f type="i"><term lang="gkt">Texetius</term>. i</f>. Archer, &amp;c. were
brought into <f type="i">Italy</f> after the division of the Empire. After
the Conquest, our Nation (who before would not admitte
strange and vnknowne names, but avoyded them therefore
as vnluckie) by little and little beganne to vse Hebrew and 
</page>

<page no="38">
sacred names, as <f type="i">Mathew, David, Sampson, Luke, Simon</f>, &amp;c. 
which were never received in Germany, until after the death
of <f type="i">Frederick</f> the second, about some 300. yeares since. 

So that the <f type="i">Saxons, Danish, Norman</f>, and <f type="i">British</f> tongues,
are the fittest keyes to open the entrance for searching out
of our antient names yet in vse. For the Hebrew I wil follow
the common tables of the Bible, which every one may do
as well, and <f type="i"><term lang="la">Philo De nominibus mutatis.</term></f> For the Greeke the
best Glossaries with mine owne little skill. For the Welsh,
I will sparingly touch them, or leave them to the learned of
that Nation. But for old English names, which heere are the
scope of my care, I must sift them as I may out of old En&shy;
glish-<f type="i">Saxon</f> treatises, as I have happened vpon heere and
there: and some coniecturally, referring all to the iudgement
of such, as shall be more happy in finding out the truth, ho&shy;
ping that probability may either please, or be pardoned by
such as are modestly learned in histories and languages; to
whose iudgement in all humilitie, I commit all that is to be
said. For that they c<expan type="a+_">an</expan>not but observe the diversity of names,
from the originall in divers languages, as how the French
have changed <f type="i">Petrus</f> into <f type="i">Pierre, Iohannes</f> to <f type="i">Iehan, Benedi&shy;
ctus</f> to <f type="i">Benoist, Stephanus</f> to <f type="i">Estien, Radulphus</f> to <f type="i">Raoul</f>: how
the Italians have changed <f type="i">Iohannes</f> into <f type="i">Giovanni, Constans</f> 
into <f type="i">Gostante, Christopherus</f> into <f type="i">Christophano, Iacobus</f> into
<f type="i">Iacopo, Radulphus</f> into <f type="i">Ridulpho, Laurentius</f> into <f type="i">Lorenz</f>.
How the Welsh have altered <f type="i">Ioannes</f> into <f type="i">Even, &AElig;gidius</f> 
into <f type="i">Silim, George</f> into <f type="i">Sior, Lawrence</f> into <f type="i">Lowris, Constan&shy;
tinus</f> into <f type="i">Custenith</f>. How the English have changed <f type="i">Gerrard</f>
into <f type="i">Garret, Albric</f> into <f type="i">Aubry, Alexander</f> into <f type="i">Sanders, Con&shy;
stantin</f> into <f type="i">Custance, Benedict</f> into <f type="i">Bennet</f>. How the <f type="i">En&shy;
glish</f> and <f type="i">Scottish</f> borderers do vse <f type="i">Roby</f> and <f type="i">Rob</f> for <f type="i">Robert,
Lokky</f> for <f type="i">Luke, Iokie</f> and <f type="i">Ionie</f> for <f type="i">Iohn, Cristie</f> for <f type="i">Christopher</f>,
&amp;c. That I may omit the Spaniard which have turned <f type="i">Iohn</f>
into <f type="i">Iuan</f>, and <f type="i">Iacobus</f> into <f type="i">Iago</f> and <f type="i">Diego</f>: as the Ger&shy;
mans which have contracted <f type="i">Iohannes</f> into <f type="i">Hanse</f>, and <f type="i">The&shy;
odoric</f> into <f type="i">Deric</f>. These and the like, whosoever will lear&shy;
nedly consider, will not thinke any thing strange which shal 
</page>

<page no="39">
heereafter follow; howsoever the vnlearned will boldly cen&shy;
sure it. I had purposed heere, lest I might seeme heereafter
to lay my foundations in the sands of coniecture, and not
on grounds of truth and authoritie, to have given you the
signification of such words as offer themselves most fre&shy;
quent in the compositions of our meere English names, <f type="i">viz</f>. 

&AElig;l 
Al 
&AElig;lf 
Ard 
Ar 
Bert 
Bald 
Cin 
Cuth 
Ead 
Fred 
Gisle

Wi 
Will 
Win, &amp;c.

And these not out of suppositive coniectures, but out of
<f type="i">Alfricus Grammer</f>, who was a learned Archbishop of <f type="i">Can&shy;
terbury</f>, well neere six hundred yeare since, and therefore
not to be supposed ignorant of the English tongue, out of
the <f type="i">English-Saxon Testament, Psalter</f>, and <f type="i">Lawes</f>, out of <f type="i">Wil&shy;
leramus Paraphrasis</f> vpon the <f type="i">Canticles</f>, and the learned
Notes thereon by a man skilfull in the Northerne tongues,
as also out of <f type="i">Beatus Rhonanus</f>, M. <f type="i">Luther, Dasipodius, Kil&shy;
lianus</f>, who have laboured in illustration of the old <f type="i">German</f> 
tongue, which vndoubtedly is the matrix and mother of
our English. But I thinke it most fitting to this purpose, to
shew those my grounds in their proper places heereafter.
</section>

Welsh, <f type="i">Sax</f>. Saxon, or old English.

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Abel(pn)">ABEL</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="just(a)"><f type="i">Heb</f>. Iust.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Adam(pn)">ADAM</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="man(n)" lexeme="earthly(a)" lexeme="red(a)"><f type="i">Heb</f>. Man, earthly, or red.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Adrian(pn)">ADRIAN</form>, <xpln lang="en" >see <f type="i">Hadrian</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Alan(pn)">ALAN</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="hound(n)" lexeme="sun-bright(a)">is thought by <f type="i">Iulius Scaliger</f> (some of
whose progenitors bare that name) to sig&shy;
nifie an hownd in the <f type="i">Sclavonian</f> tongue,
and <f type="i">Chaucer</f> vseth <f type="i">Aland</f> in the same sense:
neither may it seem strange to take names
from beasts. The <f type="i">Romanes</f> had their <f type="i">Ca&shy;
ninius, Apur, Asinus</f>, &amp;c. and the Christians,
<f type="i">Leo, Lupus, Ursula</f>. But whereas this came
into <f type="i">England</f> with <f type="i">Alan</f> earle of <f type="i">Britaine</f>, to
whome the Conqueror gave the greatest
part of <f type="i">Richmondshire</f>, and hath bin most
common since that time in the Northern
parts, in the yonger children of the noble
house of <f type="i">Percies</f>, and the family of <f type="i">Zouch</f>,
descended from the Earles of <f type="i">Britaine</f>; I
would seeks it rather out of the <f type="i">British</f>,
than <f type="i">Sclavonian</f> tongue, and will beleeve 
with an antient Britan, that it is corrupted
from <f type="i">&AElig;lianus</f>, that is, Sunne-bright, as
they corrupted <f type="i">Vitelianus</f> into <f type="i">Guidalan</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Aubry(pn)">AUBRY</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="kingdom(n)" lexeme="rich(a)">in Latine <f type="i"><term lang="la">Albericus</term></f>, deduced from
the German name <f type="i"><term lang="gr">Alberic</term></f>, Given in wish,
and hope of royall power, empire, king&shy; 
</page>

<page no="41">
dome, wealth and might, as <f type="i">Plutarchus, Ar&shy;
chitas, Crates, Craterus, Polycrates, Pancratius</f>,
with the <f type="i">Greekes, Regulus Opimius</f>, &amp;c. with
the <f type="i">Latines</f>. The king of the <f type="i">Gothes</f>, which
sacked <f type="i">Rome</f> bearing his name, was called by
the <f type="i">Romans <term lang="la">Allaricus</term></f>, the olde Englishmen
turned it into <f type="i">Alric</f>, the <f type="i">Normans</f> into <f type="i">Alberic</f>.
That <f type="i">Ric</f>, as it signified a kingdome, so also it
signified, rich, wealthy, mighty, able power&shy;
ful, attributes to a kingdome; the word yet re&shy;
maines in that sence among all the <f type="i">German</f>
nations dispersed in <f type="i">Europe</f>, and little mollifi&shy;
ed dooth sufficiently proove. The <f type="i">Italians</f> re&shy;
ceiving it from the <f type="i">Longobardes</f>, have turned it
into <f type="i"><term lang="it">Ricco</term></f>, the <f type="i">Spaniards</f> from the <f type="i">Gothes</f> in&shy;
to <f type="i"><term lang="sp">Rico</term></f>, the <f type="i">French</f> from the <f type="i">Frankes</f> into 
<f type="i"><term lang="fr">Riche</term></f>, we from the <f type="i">Saxons</f> into <f type="i">Rich</f>, &amp;c. <f type="i">For&shy;
tunatus Venantius</f>, who lived about a thousand
yeares since, translated it by <f type="i">Potens</f>, and <f type="i">Fortis</f>
in these verses to <f type="i">Hilperic</f> king of <f type="i">Fraunce</f>:

<f type="i"><term lang="la">Hilperice potens, si interpres barbarus adsit,
Adiutor fortis hoc quo<expan type="q3">que</expan> nomen habet.
Nec fuit in vanum sic te vocitare parentes,
Pr&aelig;sagum hoc totum laudis, &amp; omen erat.</term></f>

As that <f type="i">Hilperic</f> did signifie, puissant and
mightie helper. This name is vsually written
<f type="i">Chilperic</f>, but the <f type="i">C</f> was set before for <f type="i">Coning</f>, 
that is, <f type="i">King</f>, as in <f type="i">Clotharius, Clodovens, Cheri&shy;
bertus</f>, for <f type="i">Lotharius, Lodov&aelig;us, Heribertus. Au&shy;
bry</f> hath beene a most common name in the
honorable familie of <f type="i">Vere</f> earles of <f type="i">Oxford</f></xpln></wordentry>.

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Alban(pn)">ALBAN</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="white(a)" lexeme="high(a)"><f type="i">Lat. White</f>, or <f type="i">High</f>, as it pleaseth other.
The name of our <f type="i">Stephen</f> and first Martyr of
<f type="i">Britaine</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Alvin(pn)">ALWIN</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="victorious(a)"><f type="i">Sax. All victorious</f>, or <f type="i">Winning all</f>,

<page no="42">

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Albert(pn)">ALBERT</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="all-bright(a)"><f type="i">Germ. All-bright</f>, as <f type="i"><term lang="la">Epiphanius, Ph&aelig;&shy;

<f type="i">Ecbert, Sebert, Ethelbert</f>, in the latter are writ&shy;

<f type="i">Bert</f> in composition of names dooth not sig&shy;
nifie <f type="i">Beard</f>, as some translate it.</xpln></wordentry> 

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Alfred(pn)">&AElig;LFRED</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="peace(n)"><f type="i">Sax. All peace</f>, not varying much in

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Alphonse(pn)">ALPHONS</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="help(n)">if it be a German name, and came in&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Amy(pn)">AMIE</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="beloved(n)" lexeme="fair-spoken(a)">from the French <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Ami&egrave;,</term></f> that is, <f type="i">Beloved</f>,

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Arthur(pn)">ARTHVR</form>, <xpln lang="en" >a Latin name in <f type="i">Iuvenal</f> drawne from

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Baldwin(pn)">BALDWIN</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="speedy conqueror(n)" lexeme="victorious power(n)"><f type="i">Ger</f>. if we beleeve <f type="i">Luther, Spee&shy;

<page no="45">
inverted, <f type="i">Ethelbald</f> nobly bold, <f type="i">Willibald</f> very

the names wherein <f type="i">Win</f> is found, seeme to im&shy;
ply victorie, as <f type="i">Tatewin</f>, Learned victor, <f type="i">Bert&shy;

cetus</term></f> in Greeke. Accordingly we may iudge
that most names wherein <f type="i">Win</f> is found, to re&shy;
semble the Greeke names, <f type="i"><term lang="gkt">Nicetes, Nicocles,
Nichomachus, Nicander, Polynices</term></f>, &amp;c. which

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Edgar(pn)">EADGAR</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="happy(a)" lexeme="blessed(a)" lexeme="honorable(a)"><f type="i">Sax</f> for <f type="i">Eadig-ar, Happy</f>, or <f type="i">blessed

<f type="i">Ear</f>, or <f type="i">Ar</f>, signifie <f type="i">Honor</f>, it appeareth in the 
<f type="i">Saxon</f> lawes, and in <f type="i">Ionas Turson Danish Vo&shy;
cabulary</f>, as <f type="i">Artic</f> &amp; <f type="i">Earlic</f>, Honourable. And

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Edmund(pn)">EDMVND</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="happy(a)" lexeme="blessed(a)" lexeme="peace(n)"><f type="i">Sax</f>. for <f type="i">Eadmund</f>, Happy, or blessed,

signifie their noblest gentlemen.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Gervase(pn)">GERVAS</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="constant(n)"><f type="i"><term lang="la">Gervasius</term></f> in Latine, for <f type="i"><term lang="gr">Gerfast,</term></f> (as
some Germans coniecture) that is, All sure,
firme, or fast. If it be so, it is onely <f type="i"><term lang="la">Constans</term></f>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Geoffrey(pn)">GEFFREY</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="joyful peace(n)"><f type="i">Ger</f>. from <f type="i"><term lang="gr">Gaufred</term></f>, Ioyfull peace. <f type="i">Ki&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Gilbert(pn)">GILBERT</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="gold-like bright(a)" lexeme="yellow-bright(a)" lexeme="bright pledge(n)"><f type="i">germ</f>. I supposed heretofore to signi&shy;
fie Gold-like-bright, as <f type="i"><term lang="la">Aurelius</term></f> or <f type="i"><term lang="la">Aure&shy;
lianus</term></f>: or yellow bright, as <f type="i"><term lang="la">Flavius</term></f> with the
Romans. For <f type="i">Geele</f> is yellow in old Saxon,
&amp; still in Dutch, as <f type="i"><term lang="la">Gilvus</term></f> according to some
in Latin. But because it is written in Dooms&shy;
day booke <f type="i">Gislebert</f>. I iudge it rather to signi&shy;
fie Bright or brave pledge; for in old Saxon,
<f type="i">Gisle</f> signifieth a pledge, &amp; in the old English
booke of S. <f type="i">Augustines</f> of <f type="i">Canterbury</f>, sureties
and pledges for keeping the peace are called
<f type="i">Fredgisles</f>. So it is a well fitting name for chil&shy;
dren which are the onlye sweet pledges and
pawns of love between m<expan type="a+_">an</expan> &amp; wife, &amp; accor&shy;
dingly called <f type="i"><term lang="la">Dulcia pignora</term></f> &amp; <f type="i"><term lang="la">Pignora amoris.</term></f></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Giles(pn)">GILES</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="little kid(n)">is miserably disjoynted from <f type="i"><term lang="gkt">&AElig;gidius</term></f>, as 
<f type="i">Gillet</f> from <f type="i"><term lang="gkt">&AElig;gidia</term></f>, by the French, as appea&shy;
reth in histories by the name of Duke of <f type="i">Rol&shy;
los</f> wife It may seeme a Greeke name, for that
S. <f type="i"><term lang="gkt">Giles,</term></f> the first that I have read so named,
was an Athenian, and so drawne from <f type="i"><term lang="gkt">Aigi&shy;
dion</term></f>, that is, Little Kid, as we know <f type="i">Martia&shy;

some no lesse probably fetch <f type="i">Giles</f> from <f type="i"><term lang="la">Iu&shy;
lius</term></f>, as <f type="i">Gilian</f> from <f type="i"><term lang="la">Iuliana</term></f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Godfrey(pn)">GODFREY</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="God's peace(n)" lexeme="godly(a)"><f type="i">ger</f>. From <f type="i">Godfred</f>, Gods-peace, or

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Godard(pn)">GODARD</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="godly(a)" lexeme="towardness(n)" lexeme="aptness(n)"><f type="i">ger</f>. Strength of God, or Gods-man

<f type="i">Giffard</f>, Liberal dispositi<expan type="o+_">on</expan>, as <f type="i">Largus; Bernard</f>,

Victorious in God.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Godrich(pn)">GODRICH</form>. <xpln lang="en" lexeme="rich(a)"><f type="i">ger</f>. Rich, or powerfull in God.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Gregory(pn)">GREGORY</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="watching(a)" lexeme="watchful(a)"><f type="i">gre</f>. Watching, watchfull, as <f type="i"><term lang="gkt">Vigilan&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Griffith(pn)">GRYFFITH</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="strong-faithed(a)"><f type="i">Brit</f>. Some Britans interpret it,

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Gruffin(pn)">GRVFFIN</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="red(n)"><f type="i">Brit</f>. If it be not the same with <f type="i">Grif&shy;
fith</f>, some do fetch from <f type="i">Rufinus, Red</f>. as many
other Welsh names are derived fr<expan type="o+_">om</expan> colours.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Grimbald(pn)">GRIMBALD</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="power(n)"><f type="i">ger</f>. But truly <f type="i">Grimoald</f>, power over
anger, as <f type="i">Rodoal&agrave</f>; power of councel [Luther]</f>
a name most vsuall in the old family of <f type="i">Paun&shy;
cefoote</f>.</xpln></wordentry>
