<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Hector(pn)">HECTOR</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="defender(n)"><f type="i">gr</f>. Defendour, according to <f type="i">Plato</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Henry(pn)">HENRY</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="rich(a)" lexeme="powerful(a)"><f type="i">ger</f>. in Latine <f type="i"><term lang="la">Henricus.</term></f> A name so fa&shy; 
</page>

<page no="57">
mous since the yeare 920. when <f type="i">Henry</f> the
first was Emperour, that there have beene 7.
Emperours, 8. Kings of <f type="i">England</f>, 4. Kings of
<f type="i">France</f>, as many of <f type="i">Spaine</f> of that name. If <f type="i">Ein&shy;
ric</f> be the originall, it signifieth Ever rich or
powerfull. If it be deduced from <f type="i"><term lang="gr">Herric</term></f>, which
the Germans vse now, it is as much as Rich&shy;
Lord. I once supposed, not without some pro&shy;
babilitie, that it was contracted from <f type="i"><term lang="la">Honori&shy;
cus</term></f>, of which name, as <f type="i">Procopius</f> mentioneth,
there was a Prince of the <f type="i">Vandales</f>, in the time
of <f type="i">Honorius</f>, and therefore likely to take name
of him, as hee did from <f type="i">Honor</f>. And lately I
have found that <f type="i">Fr. Philelphus</f> is of the same
opinion. Howsoever it hath been an ominous
good name in all respects of signification.</xpln></wordentry> 

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Hengest(pn)">HENGEST</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="horseman(n)"><f type="i">Sax</f>. Horse man, the name of him
which led the first Englishmen into this Isle,
somewhat answerable to the Greeke names,
<f type="i"><term lang="gkt">Philippe, Speusiprus, Ctesippus</term></f>, his brother in like
sort was called <f type="i">Horsa</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Harold(pn)">HARHOLD</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="governor(n)" lexeme="general(n)" lexeme="love of army(n)"><f type="i">Sax. Luther</f> interpreteth it Gover&shy;
nour or Generall of an Armie, and so would I
if it were <f type="i">Harwold</f>. But being written <f type="i">Harhold</f>
&amp; <f type="i">Herold</f>, I rather turne it love of the Armie.
For <f type="i">Hold</f> see <f type="i">Rheinhold</f>. For <f type="i">Hare</f> and <f type="i">Here</f> 
that they signifie both an Armie, and a Lord,
it is taken for granted: Yet I suspect this <f type="i">Here</f>,
for a Lord to come from the Latine <f type="i"><term lang="la">Herus.</term></f> See
<f type="i">Ethelwold</f>.</xpln></wordentry> 

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Herbert(pn)">HERBERT</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="famous lord(n)"><f type="i">Germ</f>. Famous Lord, bright Lord, or

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Herwin(pn)">HERWIN</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="victorious lord(n)"><f type="i">ger</f>. Victorious Lord, or Victor in

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Harman(n)" llexeme="Hermon(pn)">HARMAN, or <f type="i">Hermon</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="general(n)">ger</f>. Generall of an Army,

</page>

<page no="58">
dus,]</f> Hence the German Dukes are called
<f type="i"><term lang="gr">Hertogen</term></f>, as Leaders of Armies.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Howell(pn)">HOWEL</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="sun-bright(a)">A Brittish name, the originall whereof

Sound or whole, as wisely as he saith, English&shy;
men were called <f type="i">Angli</f>, because they were
good Anglers. I rather would fetch <f type="i">Hoel</f> from
<f type="i">H&aelig;lius</f>, that is, Sunne-bright, as <f type="i">Coel</f> from 
<f type="i">C&aelig;lius</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Hugh(pn)">HVGH</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="slasher(n)" lexeme="cutter(n)" lexeme="comfort(n)"><f type="i">Aventinus</f> deriveth it from the German
word <f type="i"><term lang="gr">Hougen</term></f>, that is, slasher or cutter. But
whereas the name <f type="i"><term lang="en">Hugh</term></f>, was first in vse a&shy;
mong the French, and <f type="i">Otfrid</f> in the yeare 900.
vsed <f type="i">Hugh</f> for Comfort, I iudge this name to
be borrowed thence, and so it is correspon&shy;
dent to the Greeke names <f type="i"><term lang="gkt">Elpidius,</term></f> and <f type="i"><term lang="gkt">Elpis</term></f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Humphrey(pn)">HVMFREY</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="house peace(n)"><f type="i">ger</f>. for <f type="i"><term lang="gr">Humfred</term></f>, House-peace, a

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Julius(pn)">IVLIVS</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="soft-haired(a)" lexeme="mossy-bearded(a)"><f type="i">gr</f>: Soft haired or mossie bearded, so

<f type="i"><term lang="la">Ilus erat dum res stetit Ilia regno.</term></f> 

The old Englishmen in the North parts turned
<f type="i">Iulius</f> into <f type="i">Ioly</f>, and the vnlearned Scribes of that
time may seeme to have turned <f type="i">Iulianus</f>, into <f type="i">Io&shy;
lanus</f>, for that name doth often occurre in olde
evidences.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Ivon(pn)">IVON</form>, <xpln lang="en" >is the same with <f type="i">Iohn</f>, and vsed by the

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Leodegar(n)" llexeme="Ledger(pn)">LEODEGAR, or LEGER</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="gatherer(n)" lexeme="popular(a)"><f type="i">germ</f>: Gatherer, of peo&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Leopold(pn)">LEODPOLD</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="defender of people(n)"><f type="i">ger</f>: Defender of people, corrupt&shy;
ly <f type="i">Leopold</f>. In our auncient tongue <f type="i">Leod</f> sig&shy;
nified people of one Citie, as <f type="i">Leodscip</f>, was to
them <f type="i">Respublica</f>, The northerne Germans
have yet <f type="i"><term lang="gr">Leud</term></f> in the same sense. So <f type="i">Luti, Liudi,
Leuti</f>, and <f type="i">Leudi</f>, as the Dialect varieth, signi&shy;
fies people. In which sence, the Normans in
the life of <f type="i">Carolus Magnus</f> were called <f type="i">Nort-&shy;
leud</f>. The names wherein <f type="i">Leod</f> are found,
seeme translated from those Greeke names
wherein you shall finde <f type="i"><term lang="gkt">Demos</term></f> and <f type="i"><term lang="gkt">Laos</term></f>, as
<f type="i"><term lang="gkt">Demosthenes</term></f>, that is, Strength of the people.
<f type="i"><term lang="gkt">Dimochares</term></f>, that is, Gratious to the people,
<f type="i"><term lang="gkt">Demophilus</term></f>, that is, Lover of the people. <f type="i"><term lang="gkt">Ni&shy;
codemus</term></f>, that is, Conqueror of people. <f type="i"><term lang="gkt">Lao&shy;
medon</term></f>, that is, Ruler of people. <f type="i"><term lang="gkt">Laodamas</term></f>,
that is, Tamer of people, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Livin(pn)">LIVIN</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="beloved(n)"><f type="i">germ</f>. The same with <f type="i">Amatus</f>, that is,
Beloved [<f type="i">Kilianus</f>]</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Marcellus(pn)">MARCELLVS</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="martial(a)" lexeme="warlike(a)" lexeme="hammer(n)"><f type="i">lat: Plutarch</f> out of <f type="i">Possidonius</f> de&shy;

<page no="63">

cause they valiantly didde hammer and beate
downe their adversaries, See <f type="i">Malmes</f>. pag. 54.</xpln></wordentry> 

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Marmaduke(pn)">MARMADVC</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="more mighty(a)"><f type="i">germ: <term lang="gr">Mermachtig</term></f> as some con&shy;

but most in former times in the noble families
of <f type="i">Tweng, Lumley</f>, and <f type="i">Constable</f>, and thought
to be <f type="i"><term lang="la">Valentinianus</term></f> translated.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Mark(pn)">MARKE</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="high(a)" lexeme="hammer(n)" lexeme="mallet(n)">In Hebrew signifieth High, but in La&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Maximilian(pn)">MAXIMILIAN</form>, <xpln lang="en" >A new name, first devised by

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Othes(pn)">OTHES</form>, <xpln lang="en" >An old name in <f type="i">England</f>, drawne from

<page no="66">
originall, whereof when <f type="i">Suetonius</f> could not

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Owen(pn)">OWEN</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="noble(a)"><f type="i">Lat. <term lang="la">Audoenus</term></f>, if it be the same with S.

cords, as I have beene enformed, was written
<f type="i"><term lang="la">Eugenius Ogle</term></f></xpln></wordentry>.

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Peter(pn)">PETRE</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="stone(n)">For which as the French vsed <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Pierre</term></f>: so

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Reinhold(pn)">REINHOLD</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="pure love(n)"><f type="i">Sax</f>. Sincere or pure love: for the

ver for purenes <f type="i">Rheine</f>, and the old English

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Rhese(pn)">RHESE</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="giant(n)">A British name, deduced as they thinke

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Richard(pn)">RICHARD</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="powerful(a)" lexeme="rich(a)"><f type="i">Sax</f>. Powerfull and rich disposition,
as <f type="i">Richer</f>, an antient Christian name, signified
Powerfull in the Armie, or Rich Lord, and
was but <f type="i">Herric</f> reversed, <f type="i">Aventinus</f> turneth it,

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Robert(pn)">ROBERT</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="famous in counsel(a)"><f type="i">Germ</f>. Famous in Councell, for it is
written most anciently <f type="i">Rodbert. Rod, Red</f>, and
<f type="i">Rod</f>, do signifie councell, See <f type="i">Conrad</f> and <f type="i">Al&shy;
bert</f>. This name was given to <f type="i">Rollo</f>, first
Duke of <f type="i">Normandy</f>, an originall Ancestour
of the Kings of <f type="i">England</f>, who was called first
by the Normans and French <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Rou</term></f>, wherevn&shy;
to, some without ground thinke that <f type="i">Bert</f> was
added: so that it should signifie <f type="i">Rou</f>, the re&shy;
nowmed. Others vntruly turne it <f type="i">Red-beard</f>,
as thogh it were al one with <f type="i"><term lang="la">&AElig;nobarbus</term></f> of the
Latines, or <f type="i"><term lang="it">Barbarossa</term></f> of the Italians, <f type="i">Iohn Bo&shy;
din</f> (or Pudding,) that I may give him his true
English name, maketh it full wisely <f type="i">Red-bara</f>;
but I thinke no <f type="i">Robert</f> which knoweth what
<f type="i">Bardus</f> meaneth, will like of it.</xpln></wordentry> 

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Roger(pn)">ROGER</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="quiet(a)" lexeme="counsel(n)"><f type="i">Ger. <term lang="gr">Ruger</term></f>, Quiet, the same with <f type="i"><term lang="la">Tran&shy;
quilles</term></f> in Latine, <f type="i">Frodoard</f> writeth it alwaies 
<f type="i"><term lang="la">Rottgarius</term></f>, or <f type="i"><term lang="la">Rodgarus</term></f>, so it seemeth to signi&shy;
fie All councell, or strong councell.</xpln></wordentry> 

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Roland(pn)">ROLLAND</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="counsel for the land(n)"><f type="i">Ger</f>. Whereas it was anciently writ&shy;
ten <f type="i">Rod-land</f>, it may seem to signifie Councell
for the Land. And the first that I finde so na&shy;
med, was land-ward<expan type="e+_">en</expan> in <f type="i">France</f>, vnder <f type="i">Carolus
Magnus</f>, against the Piracies of the Normans.
The Italians vse <f type="i"><term lang="it">Orland</term></f> for <f type="i">Rowland</f> by <f type="i">Me&shy;
tathesis</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Roman(pn)">ROMANE</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="strong(a)"><f type="i">Lat</f>. Strong, from the Greeke <term lang="gk">&#x03A1;&#x03C9;&#x03BC;&#x1F20;&#x03C2;</term>
answerable to <f type="i"><term lang="la">Valens</term></f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Ruben(pn)">RVBEN</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="son of visions(n)"><f type="i">Heb</f>. The sonne of visions, or a quick-&shy;
seeing sonne. [<f type="i">Philo</f>.]</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Reinfred(pn)">REINFRED</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="pure peace(n)"><f type="i">Sax</f>. Pure peace.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Theobald(pn)">THEOBALD</form>, 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="God's power(n)" lexeme="powerful(a)">commonly <f type="i">Tibald</f>, and <f type="i">Thibald</f>,
Gods power, as <f type="i">B: Rhenanius</f> noteth.
But certaine it is, that in our Saxon Psalter
<f type="i"><term lang="la">Gentes</term></f> is alwayes translated by <f type="i"><term lang="oe">Theod</term></f>, and in
the English-Saxon old Annales, the English

<f type="i">Lipsius in <term lang="la">Poliorceticis</term></f> affirmeth to be in the
auntient German Psalters. So that <f type="i">Theobald</f>
seemeth in his opinion to signifie Powerfull,
or bolde over people. It was the common
name in the familie of the <f type="i">Gorges</f>; and of the
Lord <f type="i">Verdons</f>, of whom the Earles of <f type="i">Shrews&shy;
burie</f>, and <f type="i">Essex</f> are descended.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Tristram(pn)">TRISTRAM</form>, <xpln lang="en" >I knowe not whether the first of

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="W"><alpha>W</alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Walter(pn)">WALTER</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="pilgrim(n)" lexeme="wood-lord(n)" lexeme="woodman(n)"><f type="i">ger</f>: from <f type="i"><term lang="gr">Waldher</term></f>, for so it is
most auntiently written, a Pilgrime
according to <f type="i">Reneccius</f>; other make it a Wod-&shy;
Lorde, or a Wood-man, aunswerable to the
name of <f type="i">Silvius</f>, <f type="i">Silvanus</f>, or <f type="i">Sylvester</f>. The
old English called a wood, <f type="i">Wald</f>, and an her&shy;
mite living in the woods, a <f type="i">Waldbrooder</f>. But
if I may cast in my conceit, I take it to be <f type="i">Her&shy;
wald</f> inverted, as <f type="i">Herric</f> and <f type="i">Richer, Winbald</f> 
</page>

<page no="73">
and <f type="i">Baldwin</f>. And so it signifieth Governour
or Generall of an Armie, as <f type="i">Hegesistratus</f>, See
<f type="i">Herman</f> and <f type="i">Harold</f></xpln></wordentry><emend err=":">.</emend> 

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Walwin(pn)">WALWIN</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="conqueror(n)">Some have interpreted out of the
German tongue, a Conquerour, as <f type="i"><term lang="gr">Nicho&shy;
laus</term></f> and <f type="i"><term lang="la">Nicodemus</term>, Victor</f> in Latine; but wee
now vse <f type="i">Gawen</f> insteede of <f type="i">Walwyn, Architre&shy;
nius</f> maketh it <f type="i"><term lang="la">Walganus</term></f> in Latine. But if <f type="i">Wal&shy;

as <f type="i">W: Malmesbury</f> noteth, where hee speaketh
of his giant-like bones found in <f type="i">Wales</f>, I re&shy;
ferre the signification to the Britans.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Warin(pn)">WARIN</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="victorious(a)"><f type="i">Iovianus libr. I. de Aspiratione</f> draw&shy;
eth it from <f type="i">Varro</f>. But whereas it is written in
all Records. <f type="i">Guarinus</f>: It may seeme molli&shy;
fied from the Dutch <f type="i"><term lang="du">Gerwm</term></f>, that is, All-vi&shy;
ctorious. See <f type="i">Gertrud</f></xpln></wordentry>.

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="William(pn)">WILLIAM</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="defence(n)"><f type="i">ge</f>: For sweeter sound drawne from
<f type="i"><term lang="gr">Wilhelm</term></f>, which is interpreted by <f type="i">Luther</f>,
Much Defence, or, Defence to many, as
<f type="i"><term lang="gr">Wilwald</term></f>, Ruling many. <f type="i"><term lang="gr">Wildred</term></f>, Much re&shy;
verent feare, or Awfull. <f type="i"><term lang="gr">Wilfred</term></f>, Much peace.
<f type="i"><term lang="gr">Willibert</term></f>, Much brightnesse, or Very bright.
<f type="i"><term lang="gr">Willibrod</term></f> Much increase. So the French that
cannot pronounce <f type="i">W</f> have turnd it into <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Phil&shy;
li</term></f>, as <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Phillibert</term></f>, for <f type="i">Willibert</f>, Much brightnes.
Many names wherein wee have <f type="i">Will</f>, seeme
translated from the Greeke names composed
of <term lang="gk">&#x03A0;&#x03BF;&#x03BB;&#x1F50;&#x03C2;</term>, as <f type="i"><term lang="gkt">Polydamas, Polybius, Polyxenus</term>, &amp;c.
<term lang="gkt">Helm</term></f> yet remaineth with vs, and <f type="i">Villi, Willi</f>,
and <f type="i"><term lang="gr">Bills</term></f> yet with the Germans for <f type="i">Many</f>. O&shy;
ther turne <f type="i">William</f>, a Willing Defender, and
so it answereth the Roman <f type="i"><term lang="la">Titus</term></f>, if it come
from <f type="i"><term lang="la">Tuendo</term></f>, as some will have it. The Ita&shy;
lians that liked the name, but could not pro&shy;
nounce the <f type="i">W</f> if wee may beleeve <f type="i">Gesner</f>, tur&shy;
ned it into <f type="i"><term lang="it">Galeazo</term></f>, retaining the sence in part 
</page>

<page no="74">
for <f type="i">Helme</f>. But the Italians report, that <f type="i"><term lang="it">Ga&shy;
leazo</term></f> the first Viscount of <f type="i">Millaine</f> was so cal&shy;
led, for that many Cockes crew lustily at his
birth. This name hath beene most common
in <f type="i">England</f> since king <f type="i">William</f> the Conqueror,
insomuch that vppon a festivall day in the
Court of king <f type="i">Henry</f> the second, when Sir
<f type="i">William Saint-Iohn</f>, and Sir <f type="i">William Fitz-Ha&shy;
mon</f> especiall Officers had commaunded that
none but of the name of <f type="i">William</f> should dine
in the great Chamber with them, they were
accompanied with an hundred and twentie
<f type="i">Williams</f>, all Knights, as <f type="i">Robert Moutensis</f> re&shy;
cordeth <f type="i">Anno</f> 1173.</xpln></wordentry> 

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Wilfred(pn)">WILLFRED</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="much peace(n)"><f type="i">Sax</f>: Much peace.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Wimund(pn)">WIMVND</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="sacred peace(n)" lexeme="holy peace(n)"><f type="i">Sax</f>: Sacred peace, or holy peace, as
<f type="i">Wibert</f>, Holy and Bright; for <f type="i">Wi</f>, in <f type="i">Wille&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Wischard(n)" lexeme="Guiscard(pn)">WISCHARD, or GVISCARD</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="wily(a)" lexeme="crafty shifter(n)"><f type="i">Norm</f>: Wilie, and

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Wolstan(pn)">WOLSTAN</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="comely(a)" lexeme="decent(a)"><f type="i">Sax</f>: Comely, decent, as <f type="i">Decentius,

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Wulpher(pn)">WVLPHER</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="helper(n)"><f type="i">Sax</f>: Helper, the Saxon name of a

<page no="76">
<section type="Christian Names of Women">
<heading>Christian Names of 
Women.</heading>

<subheading>Lest Women, the most kinde sex, should con&shy;
ceive vnkindenes if they were omitted, some&shy;
what of necessitie must bee saide of their
names.</subheading>

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="A">
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Abigail(pn)">ABIGAEL</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="father's joy(n)"><f type="i">Heb</f>. The fathers ioy.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Agatha(pn)">AGATHA</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="good(a)"><f type="i">Gr</f>. Good, <f type="i">Guth</f> in old Saxon.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Agnes(pn)">AGNES</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="chaste(a)"><f type="i">Gr</f>. Chaste, the French write <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Ignatia</term></f>,
but I know not why<emend err="">.</emend></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Aletheia(pn)">ALETHEIA</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="truth(n)"><f type="i">Gre</f>. Veritie, or t<emend err="">r</emend>uth.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Alice(pn)">ALICE</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="noble(a)" lexeme="defendress(n)"><f type="i">Ger</f>. Abridged from <f type="i"><term lang="gr">Adesiz</term></f>, Noble.
See <f type="i">Ethelbert</f>. But the French make it defen&shy;
dresse, turning it into <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Alexia</term></f></xpln></wordentry>.

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Anna(pn)">ANNA</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="gracious(a)" lexeme="merciful(a)"><f type="i">Heb</f>. Gracious, or mercifull.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Arbela(pn)">ARBELA</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="God has revenged(n)"><f type="i">Heb</f>. God hath revenged, as some tran&shy;
slations have it. [<f type="i"><term lang="la">Index Bibliorum</term></f>.]</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Adelin(pn)">ADELIN</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="noble(a)"><f type="i">Ge</f>. Noble, or descending from nobles.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Maud(n)" lexeme="Matild(pn)">MAVD for MATILD</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="noble(a)" lexeme="lady of maids(n)"><f type="i">ger: <term lang="gr">Matildis, Mathilidis</term></f>,

wench. But for shame it is turned of some to
<f type="i">Venice</f>. In Greeke <f type="i">Venus</f> was called <f type="i"><term lang="gkt">Aphro&shy; 
</page>

<page no="87">
dite</term></f>, not from the foame of the Sea, but as <f type="i">Eu&shy;
ripides</f> saith, from <f type="i"><term lang="gkt">Aphrosune</term></f>, that is, Madde-&shy;
follie</xpln></wordentry>.

tofore of great reputation in honour of <f type="i">Vrsu&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Winifrid(pn)">WINEFRID</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="win(v)" lexeme="get peace(v)" lexeme="fair countenance(n)"><f type="i">Sax</f>. Win, or get peace. If it be a
<f type="i">Britaine</f> word, as some thinke it to be &amp; writ&shy;
ten <f type="i">Guinfrid</f>, it signifieth Faire and Beautifull
countenance. Verily <f type="i">Winifred</f> a native of this
Isle, which preached the Gospel in <f type="i">Germany</f>,
was called <f type="i"><term lang="la">Boniface</term></f>; for his good face, or good
deedes, iudge you.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

OTher vsuall names of women I do not call
to remembrance at this time, yet I know
many other have beene in vse in former ages
among vs, as <f type="i">Dervergild, Sith, Amphilas, &amp;c</f>:
And also <f type="i">Nicholea, Laurentia, Richarda, Gui&shy;
lielma, Wilmetta</f>, drawne from the names of
men, in which number we yet retaine <f type="i">Philip&shy;
pa, Philip, Francisca, Francis, Ioanna, Iana</f>, &amp;c.
</page>

<page no="88">
These English-Saxon, German and other names may be
thought as faire, and as fit for men and women, as those
most vsuall <f type="i"><term lang="la">Pr&aelig;nomina</term></f> among the Romans, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Aulus</term></f> for that
hee was nourished of the gods: <f type="i"><term lang="la">Lucius</term></f> for him that was
borne in the dawning of the day: <f type="i"><term lang="la">Marcus</term></f>, for him that was
borne in March: <f type="i"><term lang="la">Manius</term></f> for him that was borne in the mor&shy;
ning: <f type="i"><term lang="la">Cneus</term></f> for him that had a wart: <f type="i"><term lang="la">Servius</term></f> for him that
was borne a slave, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Quinctius</term></f> for him that was fift borne, &amp;c.
And our womens names more gratious than their <f type="i"><term lang="la">Rutilia</term></f>,
that is, Red-head: <f type="i"><term lang="la">C&aelig;silia</term></f>, that is, Grey-eyed, and <f type="i"><term lang="la">C&aelig;ia</term></f> the
most common name of all among them (signifying Ioy:)
for that <f type="i">Caia C&aelig;silia</f> the wife of King <f type="i">Tarquinius Priscus</f> was
the best distaffe-wife and spinster among them. 

Neither doe I thinke in this comparison of Names, that
any will proove like the Gentleman, who distasting our
names, preferred King <f type="i">Arthurs</f> age before ours, for the gal&shy;
lant, brave, and stately names then vsed, as sir <f type="i">Orson</f>, sir <f type="i">Tor</f>,
sir <f type="i">Quadragan</f>, sir <f type="i">Dinadan</f>, sir <f type="i">Launcelot</f>, &amp;c. which came
out of that forge, out of the which the Spaniard forged the
haughty and lofty name <f type="i"><term lang="sp">Traquitantos</term></f> for his Giant, which
hee so highly admired, when hee had studied many dayes
and odde houres, before hee could hammer out a name so
conformable to such a person as hee in imagination then
conceited.
</section>
</page>

<page no="89">
<section type="Surnames">
<heading>Surnames.</heading> 

SVrnames given for difference of 
families, and continued as heredi&shy;
tary in families, were vsed in no na&shy;
tion antiently but among the Ro&shy;
mans, (and that after the league 
with the <f type="i">Sabines</f>) which called th<expan type="e+_">em</expan>
<f type="i"><term lang="la">Nomina</term></f>, and <f type="i"><term lang="la">Nomina Gentilitia</term></f>, as
the former were called <f type="i"><term lang="la">Pr&aelig;nomina</term></f>.
But the French and wee termed
them <f type="i"><term lang="en">Surnames</term></f>, not because they are names of the sire, or
the father, but because they are super-added to Christian
names, as the Spaniards call them <f type="i"><term lang="sp">Renombres</term></f>, as <f type="i">Renames</f>.

The Hebrewes keeping memory of their Tribe, vsed
in their Genealogies in steede of <f type="i">Surnames</f>, the name of
their father with <f type="i"><term lang="he">Ben</term></f>, that is, Sonne, as <f type="i"><term lang="he">Melchi Ben-Addi,
Addi Ben-Cosam, Cosam Ben-Elmadam</term></f>, &amp;c. So the Gr&aelig;ci&shy;
ans, <term lang="gk">&#x1F3C;&#x03BA;&#x03B1;&#x03C1;&#x03BF;&#x03C2; &#x03C4;&#x03BF;&#x03B0; &#x0394;&#x03B1;&#x03B9;&#x03B4;&#x03AC;&#x03BB;&#x03BF;&#x03C5;</term>, <f type="i"><term lang="gkt">Icarus</term></f> the sonne of <f type="i"><term lang="gkt">Dedalus, Deda&shy;
lus</term></f> the sonne of <f type="i"><term lang="gkt">Eupalmus, Eupalmus</term></f> the sonne of <f type="i"><term lang="gkt">Metion</term></f>.

The like was vsed amongst our auncestors the English,
as <f type="i">Ceoured Ceolwalding, Ceolwald Cuthing, Cuth Cuthwining</f>,
that is, <f type="i">Ceonred</f> soune of <f type="i">Ceolwald, Ceolwald</f> sonne of <f type="i">Cuth,
Cuthe</f> son of <f type="i">Cuthwin</f>, &amp;c. And to this is observed by <f type="i">Willi&shy;
am</f> of <f type="i">Malmesbury</f>, where he noteth that the sonne of <f type="i">Ead&shy;
gar</f> was called <f type="i">Eadgaring</f>, and the son of <f type="i">Edmund, Edmunding</f>. 

The Britans in the same sence with <f type="i">Ap</f> for <f type="i">Mab</f>, as <f type="i">Ap O&shy;
wen, Owen Ap Harry, Harry Ap Rhese</f>, as the Irish with
their <f type="i">Mac</f>, as <f type="i">Donald Mac Neale, Neale Mac Con, Con Mac
Dermott</f>, &amp;c. And the olde Normans with <f type="i">Fitz</f> for <f type="i">Filz</f>, as 
<f type="i">Iohn Fitz-Robert, Robert Fitz-Richard, Richard Fitz-Ralph</f>, 
</page>

<page no="90">
&amp;c. The Arabians onely as one learned noteth, vsed their
fathers names without their owne forename, as <f type="i">Aven-Pace,
Aven Rois, Aven-Zoar</f>, that is, the sonne of <f type="i">Pace, Rois</f> and 
<f type="i">Zoar</f>; As if <f type="i">Pace</f> had a sonne at his circumcision named <f type="i">Haly</f>,
hee would be called <f type="i">Aven Pace</f>, concealing <f type="i">Haly</f>, but his 
sonne, howsoever hee were named, would be called <f type="i">Aven-
Haly</f>, &amp;c. So Surnames passing from father to sonne, and
c<expan type="o+_">on</expan>tinuing to their issue, was not antiently in vse among any
people in the world. 

Yet to these single Names were adioyned oftentimes o&shy;
ther names, as <f type="i"><term lang="la">Cognomina</term></f>, or <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Sobriquetts</term></f>, as the <f type="i">French</f> call
them, and By-names, or Nicke-names, as we terme them,
if that word be indifferent to good and bad, which still did
die with the bearer, and never descended to posteritie. That
we may not exemplifie in other nations (which would af&shy;
forde great plenty,) but in our own. King <f type="i">Eadgar</f> was called
the Peaceable, king <f type="i">Ethelred</f> the Vnreadie, king <f type="i">Edmund</f>
for his Valour, <f type="i">Iron-side</f>; king <f type="i">Harold</f> the Hare-foote, <f type="i">Eadric</f>
the <f type="i"><term lang="oe">Streona</term></f>, that is, the Getter or Streiner, <f type="i">Siward</f> the <f type="i"><term lang="oe">Dege&shy;
ra</term></f>, that is, the Valiant, King <f type="i">William</f> the first, Bastard,
king <f type="i">William</f> the second <f type="i">Rouse</f>, that is, the Red, king <f type="i">Henry</f>
the first <f type="i">Beauclarke</f>, that is, Fine Scholler: so in the house of 
<f type="i">Aniou</f> which obtained the Crowne of <f type="i">England, Geffrey</f> the 
first Earle of <f type="i">Aniou</f> was surnamed <f type="i">Grisogonel</f>, that is, Grey-&shy;
cloake, <f type="i">Fulco</f> his sonne <f type="i">Nerra</f>, his grand-childe <f type="i">Rechin</f>, for
his extortion. Againe, his grand-childe <f type="i">Plantagenet</f>, for
that he ware commonly a broome-stalke in his bonnet. His
sonne <f type="i">Henry</f> the second, king of <f type="i">England, Fitz-Empresse</f>, be&shy;
cause his mother was Empresse, his sonne king <f type="i">Richard</f> had
for surname <f type="i">Corde-Lion</f>, for his lion-like courage, as <f type="i">Iohn</f> 
was called <f type="i">Sans-terre</f>, that is, Without land: So that wher&shy;
as these names were never taken vp by the sonne, I knowe
not why any should thinke <f type="i">Plantagenet</f> to be the surname
of the royall house of <f type="i">England</f>, albeit in late yeeres many
have so accounted it. Neither is it lesse strange, why so ma&shy;
ny should thinke <f type="i">Theodore</f> or <f type="i">Tydur</f>, as they contract it, to 
be the surname of the Princes of this Realme since king 
</page>

<page no="91">
<f type="i">Henry</f> the seaventh. For albeit <f type="i">Owen ap Meredith Tydur</f>,
which married <f type="i">Katharine</f> the daughter of <f type="i">Charles</f> the sixth 
king of <f type="i">France</f>, was grandfather to king <f type="i">Henry</f> the seventh, 
yet that <f type="i">Tydur</f>, or <f type="i">Theodore</f> was but the Christian name of 
<f type="i">Owens</f> grandfather. For <f type="i">Owens</f> father was <f type="i">Meredith ap Ty&shy;
dur, Ap Grone, Ap Tydur</f>, who all without Surnames itera&shy;
ted Christian names, after the olde manner of the <f type="i">Britaines</f>,
and other nations heeretofore noted, and so lineally dedu&shy;
ced his pedegree from <f type="i">Cadwallader</f> king of the <f type="i">Britans</f>, as 
was found by Commission directed to <f type="i">Griffin ap Lewellin;
Gitteu Owen, Iohn King</f>, and other learned men both English
and Welsh in the seaventh yeare of the said king <f type="i">Henry</f> the
seventh. 

Likewise in the line Royall of <f type="i">Scotland, Milcolme</f>, or 
<f type="i">Malcolme</f> was surnamed <f type="i">Canmore</f>, that is, Great head, and
his brother, <f type="i">Donald, Ban</f>, that is, White: <f type="i">Alexander</f> the 
first, the Prowde, <f type="i">Malcolme</f> the fourth, the Virgine,
<f type="i">William</f> his brother the Lion. As amongest the Princes of
<f type="i">Wales, Brochvail Schitrauc</f>, that is, <f type="i">Gaggtothed, Gurind, 
Barmbtruch</f>, that is, Spade-bearded, <f type="i">Elidir Coscorvaur</f>, that
is, <f type="i">Heliodor</f> the Great house-keeper, and so in <f type="i">Ireland Mu&shy;
rough Duff</f>. that is, Blacke: <f type="i">Lib. Landaff. Roo</f>. that is, Red: 
<f type="i">Nemoliah</f>, that is, full of wounds: <f type="i">Patric Ban</f>, that is, White:
<f type="i">Gavelco</f>, that is, Fetters.

To seeke therefore the auntient Surnames of the royall,
and most antient families of <f type="i">Europe</f>, is to seeke that which
never was. And, therefore greatly are they deceived which
thinke <f type="i">Valoys</f> to have beene the surname of the late French
kings, or <f type="i">Borbon</f> of this present king, or <f type="i">Habsburg</f>, or <f type="i">Austri&shy;
ac</f> of the Spanish king, or <f type="i">Steward</f> of the late kings of <f type="i">Scot&shy;
land</f>, and now of BRITAINE, or <f type="i">Oldenburg</f> of the Danish;
For (as all know that have but sipped of Histories) <f type="i">Valoys</f>
was but the Apponage and Earledome of <f type="i">Charles</f> yonger
sonne to <f type="i">Philip</f>; the second, from whome the late kings des&shy;
cended: so <f type="i">Borbon</f> was the inheritance of <f type="i">Robert</f> a yonger 
sonne to saint <f type="i">Lewes</f>, of whom this king is descended: <f type="i">Habs&shy;
burg</f> and <f type="i">Austria</f> were but the olde possessions of the Em&shy;
</page>

<page no="92">
perours and Spanish Kings progenitours. <f type="i">Steward</f> was but
the name of office to <f type="i">Walter</f>, who was high Steward of 
<f type="i">Scotland</f>, the progenitour of <f type="i">Robert</f> first King of Scots of 
that family, and of the King our Soveraigne. And <f type="i">Olden&shy;
burg</f> was but the Erledome of <f type="i">Christian</f> the first <f type="i">Danish</f> king
of this family, elected about 1448. But yet <f type="i">Plantagenet,
Steward, Valois, Borbon, Habsburg</f>, &amp;c. by prescription of
time have prevailed so farre, as they are now accounted
surnames. But for surnames of Princes, well said the learned
<f type="i">Marcus, <term lang="la">Salon de Pace. Reges cognomine now vtuntur, quia vt
Regum famili&aelig;, &amp;c agnationes, &amp; memori&aelig; conserventur, eorum
cognomina non sunt necessaria prout in alijs inferioribus, quo&shy;
rum ipsa cognomina agnationum ac familiarum memoriam
tutantur</term></f>. 

About the yeare of our Lord 1000. (that we may not
minute out the time) surnames beganne to be taken vp in
<f type="i">France</f>, and in <f type="i">England</f> about the time of the Conquest, or 
else a very little before, vnder King <f type="i">Edward</f> the Confessor,
who was all Frenchified. And to this time doe the Scot&shy;
tishmen referre the antiquitie of their surnames, although
<f type="i">Buchanan</f> supposeth that they were not in vse in <f type="i">Scotland</f>
many yeares after. 

But in <f type="i">England</f> certaine it is, that as the better sort, even
from the Conquest by little and little tooke surnames, so
they were not setled among the common people fully, vn&shy;
till about the time of King <f type="i">Edward</f> the second: but still va&shy;
ried according to the fathers name, as <f type="i">Richardson</f>, if his fa&shy;
ther were <f type="i">Richard, Hodgeson</f>, if his father were <f type="i">Roger</f>, or in 
some other respect, and from thenceforth beganne to be e&shy;
stablished, (some say by statute,) in their posteritie.

This will seeme strange to some Englishmen and Sco&shy;
tishmen, which like the Arcadians thinke their surnames as
ancient as the Moone, or at the least to reach many an age
beyond the Conquest. But they which thinke it most
strange, (I speake vnder correction,) I doubt they will hard&shy;
ly finde any surname which descended to posteritie before
that time: Neyther have they seene (I feare) any deede or 
</page>

<page no="93">
donation before the Conquest, but subsigned with crosses
and single names, without surnames in this manner in <f type="i">Eng&shy;
land</f>;  	&#x271A; <f type="i"><term lang="la">Ego Eadredus confirmavi.</term></f> &#x271A; <f type="i"><term lang="la">Ego Edmundus corrobo&shy;
ravi.</f></term> &#x271A; <f type="i"><term lang="la">Ego Sigarius conclusi.</term></f> &#x271A; <f type="i"><term lang="la">Ego Olfstanus consolidavi</term></f>,
&amp;c. Likewise for <f type="i">Scotland</f>, in an old booke of <f type="i">Duresme</f> in 
the Charter, whereby <f type="i">Edgare</f> sonne of King <f type="i">Malcolme</f>,
gave lands neere <f type="i">Coldingham</f> to that Church, in the yeare
1097. the Scottish Noblemen witnesses therevnto, had
no other surnames than the Christian names of their fa&shy;
thers. For thus they signed S. &#x271A; <term lang="la"><f type="i">Gulfi filij Meniani</f></term>, S &#x271A; <term lang="la"><f type="i"> 
Culverti filij Donecani</f></term>, S &#x271A; <term lang="la"><f type="i">Olavi filij Oghe</f></term>, &amp;c. As for my
selfe, I never hitherto found any hereditarie Surname be&shy;
fore the Conquest, neither any that I know: and yet both
I my selfe and divers whom I know, have pored and pus&shy;
led vpon many an old Record and Evidence to satisfie our
selves heerein: and for my part I will acknowledge my selfe
greatly indebted to them that wil cleare me this doubt. 

But about the time of the Conquest, I observed the ve&shy;
ry primary beginnings as it were of many surnames, which
are thought very antient, whenas it may be proved that their
very lineall Progenitors bare other names within these sixe
hundred yeers. <f type="i">Mortimer</f> and <f type="i">Warren</f> are accounted names
of great antiquitie, yet the father of them (for they were bre&shy;
thren) who first bare those names, was <f type="i"><term lang="la">Walterus de sancto
Martino</term></f>. He that first tooke the name of <f type="i">Clifford</f> from his 
habitation, was the sonne of <f type="i">Richard</f>, sonne of <f type="i">Puntz</f> a no&shy;
ble Norman, who had no other name. The first <f type="i">Lumley</f>
was sonne of an antient English man called <f type="i">Liwulph</f>. The
first <f type="i">Gifford</f>, from whome they of <f type="i">Buckingham</f>, the Lords of 
<f type="i">Brimesfeld</f>, and others descended, was the sonne of a Nor&shy;
man called <f type="i">Osbert de Bolebec</f>. The first <f type="i">Windsor</f> descended 
from <f type="i">Walter</f> the sonne of <f type="i">Other Castellan</f> of <f type="i">Windsor</f>. The
first who tooke the name of <f type="i">Shirley</f> was the sonne of <f type="i">Sewall</f>, 
descended from <f type="i">Fulcher</f> without any other name. The first 
<f type="i">Nevill</f> of them which are now, from <f type="i">Robert</f> the sonne of 
<f type="i">Maldred</f>, a braunch of an olde English familie who married 
<f type="i">Isabel</f> the daughter and heire of the <f type="i">Nevills</f> which came out 
</page>

<page no="94">
of <f type="i">Normandy</f>. The first <f type="i">Lovel</f> came from <f type="i">Gonel de Percevall</f>.
The first <f type="i">Montacute</f> was the sonne of <f type="i">Drogo Iuvenis</f>, as it is in
Record. The first <f type="i">Stanley</f> of them now Earles of <f type="i">Derby</f> was 
likewise sonne to <f type="i">Adam de Aldeleigh</f>, or <f type="i">Audley</f>, as it is in the
olde Pedegree in the Eagle tower of <f type="i">Latham</f>. And to omit
others, the first that tooke the name of <f type="i">de Burgo</f>, or <f type="i">Burke</f> in
<f type="i">Ireland</f> was the sonne of an English man called <f type="i">William Fitz 
Aldelme as the first of the <f type="i">Giraldines</f> also in that Countrey
was the sonne of an Englishman called <f type="i">Girald</f> of <f type="i">Windsor</f>. In 
many more could I exemplifie, which shortly after the con&shy;
quest, tooke these surnames, when either their fathers had
none at all, or else most different, whatsoever some of their
posteritie doe overweene of the antiquitie of their names, as
though in the continuall mutabilitie of the worlde, conver&shy;
sions of States, and fatall periods of families, five hundred
yeeres were not sufficient antiquitie for a family or name,
whenas but very few have reached thereunto. 

In the autentical Record of the Exchequer called <f type="i">Domes&shy;
day</f>, Surnames are first found, brought in then by the Nor&shy;
mans, who not long before first tooke them: but most no&shy;
ted with <f type="i">de</f>, such a place as <f type="i">Godefridus de Mannevilla; A. de
Grey. Walterus de Vernon; Robert de Oily</f>, now <f type="i">Doyley; Al&shy;
bericus de Vere; Radulphus de Pomerey; Goscelinus de Dive.
Robertus de Busle Guilielmus de Moiun; R. de Braiose; Roge&shy;
rus de Lacy; Gislebertus de Venables</f>, or with <f type="i">Filius</f>, as <f type="i">Ranul&shy;
phus, Asculphi, Guilielmis; filius Osbernie, Richardus filius Gisle&shy;
berti</f>; or else with the name of their office, as <f type="i">Eudo Dapifer:
Guil: Camerarius, Herv&aelig;us Legatus, Gislebertus Cocus, Radul&shy;
phus Venator</f>: but very many with their Christian names on&shy;
lie, as <f type="i">Olaff, Nigellus, Eustachius, Baldricus</f>, with single names
are noted last in every shire, as men of least account, and
as all, or most vnderholders specified in that Booke. But
shortly after, as the Romans of better sorte had three names
according to that of <f type="i"><term lang="la">Iuvenal, Tanquam habeas tria nomina</term></f>, &amp;
that of <f type="i">Ausonius</f>, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Tria nomina nobiliorum</term></f>. So it seemed a dis&shy;
grace for a Gentleman to have but one single name, as the
meaner sorte and bastards had. For the daughter and heire
</page>

<page no="95">
of <f type="i">Fitz-Hamon</f> a great Lord, as <f type="i">Robert</f> of <f type="i">Gloucester</f> in the
Librarie of the industrious Antiquary maister <f type="i">Iohn Stowe</f>
writeth, when king <f type="i">Henry</f> the first would have married hir
to his base sonne <f type="i">Robert</f>, she first refusing answered; 

It were to me a great shame,
To have a Lord without'n his twa name.

whereupon the king his father gave him the name of <f type="i">Fitz-
Roy</f>, who after was earle of <f type="i">Gloucester</f>, and the onely Wor&shy;
thy of his age.

To reduce surnames to a Methode, is matter for a <f type="i">Ra&shy;
mist</f>, who should happly finde it to be a <f type="i">Typocosmie</f>: I will 
plainely set downe from whence the most have beene de&shy;
duced, as farre as I can conceive, hoping to incurre no of&shy;
fence heerein with any person, when I protest in all sinceri&shy;
tie, that I purpose nothing lesse than to wrong any man in
any respect, or to make the least aspersion vpon any who&shy;
soever. The end of this scribling labour tending onely to
maintaine the honor of our names against some Italianated,
who admiring strange names, doe disdainefully contemne
their owne countrey names: which I doubt not but I shall
effect with the learned and iudicious, to whom I submit all
that I shall write.

The most surnames in number, the most antient, and of
best accompt, have been locall, deduced from places in <f type="i">Nor&shy;
mandy</f> and the countries confining, being either the patri&shy;
monaill possessions or native places of such as served the
Conquerour, or came in after out of <f type="i">Normandy</f>; as <f type="i">Aulbeny</f>,
or <f type="i">Mortimer, Warren, Albigny, Percy, Gournay, Devreux, Tan&shy;
kervil, Saint-Lo, Argenton, Marmion</f>, Saint <f type="i">Maure, Bracy,
Maigny, Nevill, Ferrers, Harecourt, Baskervile, Mortaigne,
Tracy, Beufoe, Valoyns, Cayly, Lucy, Montfort, Bonvile, Bovil,
Auranch</f>, &amp;c. Neither is there any village in <f type="i">Normandy</f>, that 
gave not denomination to some family in <f type="i">England</f>; in which
number are all names, having the French <f type="i">De, Du, Des, De-la</f>
prefixt, &amp; beginning or ending with <f type="i">Font, Fant, Beau, Sainct
Mont, Bois, Aux, Eux, Vall, Vaux, Cort, Court, Fort, Champ, 
Vil</f>, which is corruptly turned in some into <f type="i">Feld</f>, as in <f type="i">Basker&shy; 
</page>

<page no="96">
feld, Somerfeld, Dangerfeld, Trublefeld, Greenefeld, Sackefeld</f>,
for <f type="i">Baskervil, Somervil, Dangervil, Turbervil, Greenevil, Sack&shy;
vil</f>; and in others into <f type="i">Well</f>, as <f type="i">Boswell</f> for <f type="i">Bossevil</f>,
for <f type="i">Freschevil</f>. As that I may note in passage, the <f type="i">Polonian</f> 
Nobilitie take their names from places adding <f type="i">Ski</f> or <f type="i">Ki</f> 
thereunto. 

Out of places in <f type="i">Britaine</f> came the families of Saint <f type="i">Au&shy;
bin, Morley, Dinant</f>, lately called <f type="i">Denham, Dole, Balun, Con&shy;
quest, Valtort, Lascells, Bluet</f>, &amp;c.

Out of other partes of <f type="i">Fraunce</f> from places of the same
names came, <f type="i">Courtney, Corby, Bollein, Crevecuer, Saint-Leger,
Bohun, Saint George, Saint Andrew, Chaworth, Sainct Quintin,
Gorges, Villiers, Cromar, Paris, Reims, Cressy, Fines, Beaumont,
Coignac, Lyons, Chalons, Chaloner, Estampes</f>, or <f type="i">Stampes</f>, and
many more.

Out of the Netherlands came the names of <f type="i">Levayne,
Gaunt, Ipres, Bruges, Malines, Odingsells, Tournay, Doway, Bu&shy;
ers, Beke</f>; and in latter ages <f type="i">Dabridgecourt, Robsert, Many,
Grandison</f>, &amp;c.

From places in <f type="i">England</f> and <f type="i">Scotland</f> infinite likewise. For
every towne, village, or hamlet hath made names to families,
as <f type="i">Darbyshire, Lancaster</f>, (do not looke that I should as the 
<f type="i">Nomenclators</f> in olde time marshall every name according
to his place) <f type="i">Essex, Murray, Clifford, Stafford, Barkley, Leigh,
Lea, Hasting, Hamleton, Gordon, Lumley, Douglas, Booths, Clin&shy;
ton, Heydon, Cleydon, Hicham, Henningham, Popham, Ratcliffe,
Markham, Seaton, Framingham, Pagrave, Cotton, Carie, Hume,
Poinings, Goring, Prideaux, Windsor, Hardes, Stanhope, Syden&shy;
ham, Needehaus, Dimoc, Winnington, Allington, Dacre, Thax&shy;
ton, Whitney, Willoughby, Apseley, Crew, Kniveton, Wentworth,
Fanshaw, Woderington, Manwood, Fetherston, Penrudock, Tre&shy;
maine, Trevoire, Killigrew, Roscarroc, Carminow</f>, and most
families in <f type="i">Cornewall</f>, of whome I have heard this Rythme: 

<f type="i">By Tre, Ros, Pol, Lan, Caer and Pen,
You may know the most Cornish men.</f>

Which signifie a towne, a health, a poole, a church, a castle,
or cittie, and a foreland, or promontory. 
</page>

<page no="97">
In like sort many names among the Romans were taken
from places, as <f type="i">Tarquinius, Gabinus, Volscius, Vatinius, Norba&shy;
nus</f>, from <f type="i">Tarquini, Gabij, Volsici, Vatia, Norba</f>, townes in <f type="i">Italie,
Sigonius</f> and other before him have observed; and likewise
<f type="i">Amerinus, Carrinas, Macenas</f> as <f type="i">Varre</f> noteth. So <f type="i">Ruricius, 
Fonteius, Fundanus, Agellius</f>, &amp;c. Generally, all these follow&shy;
ing are locall names, and all which have their beginning or
termination in them, the significations whereof, for the
most parte, are commonly knowne. To the rest now vn&shy;
knowne, I will adioyne somwhat briefly out of <f type="i">Alfricus</f> and
others, reserving a more ample explication to his proper
place.  
</page>

<page no="98">

<page no="99">
<wordgroup1 type="a" object="C">
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="caer(n)">CAER</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="city(n)" lexeme="fortified place(n)">But a fortefied place,
or Citty.</xpln></wordentry> 

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="camp(n)">CAMPH.</form> <xpln lang="en"></xpln></wordentry> 

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="capell(n)">CAPELL</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="chapel(n)">the same with
Chapell.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="car(n)">CAR</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="pool(n)">A low waterie place
where Alders do grow,
or a poole.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="cairn(n)">CARNES</form>, <xpln lang="en">the same with
stones.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="castle(n)">CASTELL.</form> <xpln lang="en"></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="caster(n)">CASTER</form>, <xpln lang="en" lexeme="city(n)" lexeme="walled place(n)"><f type="i">Chester, Cester,
Chaster</f>, the same vari&shy;
ed in Dialect, a Citty or
