<page no="104">
At a word, all which in English had <f type="i">Of</f> set before them 
which in Cheshire and the North was contracted into <f type="i">A</f>, as
<f type="i">Thomas a Dutton, Iohn a Standish, Adam a Kirkby</f>, and all
which in Latine old Evidences have had <f type="i">De</f> pr&aelig;fixed, as
all heeretofore specified, were borrowed from places. As
those which had <f type="i">Le</f> set before them, were not locall, but gi&shy;
ven in other respects. As <f type="i">Le Marshall, Le Latimer, Le De&shy;
spencer, Le Scroope. Le Savage, Le Vavasour, Le Strange, Le
Norice, Le Escrivain, Le Bland, Le Molineux, Le Bret</f>. As they
also which were never noted with <f type="i">De</f> or <f type="i">Le</f>, in which num&shy;
ber I have observed, <f type="i">Gifford Basset, Arundel, Howard, Talbot,
Bellot, Bigot, Bagot, Taileboise, Talemach, Gernon, Lovell, Lo&shy;
vet, Fortescu, Pancevolt, Tirell, Biund</f> or <f type="i">Blunt, Bisset, Bacun, 
&amp;c</f>. And these distinctions of locall names with <f type="i">De</f>, and o&shy;
ther with <f type="i">Le</f>, or simply, were religiously observed in Re&shy;
cordes vntill about the time of king <f type="i">Edward</f> the fourth.

Neither was there, as I said before, or is there any towne,
village, hamlet, or place in <f type="i">England</f>, but hath made names to
families, and so many names are locall which doe not seeme
so, because the places are vnknowne to most men, and all
knowne to no one man: as who would imagine <f type="i">Whitegift,
Powlet, Bacon, Creping, Alshop, Tirwhit, Antrobus, Heather,
Hartshorne</f>, and many such like to be locall names, and yet
most certainely they are.

Many also are so changed by corruption of speach, and
altered, so strangely to significative wordes by the common
sort, who desire to make all to be significative, as they seeme
nothing lesse than locall names; as <f type="i">Wormwood, Inkepen, Tip&shy;
tow, Moone, Maners, Drinkewater, Cuckold, Goddolphin, Hurle&shy;
stone, Waites, Smalbacke, Loscotte, Devill, Neithermill, Bellows,
Filpot, Wodil</f>, &amp;c. for <f type="i">Ormund, Ingepen, Tiptoft, Mohune, Ma&shy;
nors, Derwentwater, Cukswold, Godolchan, Hudleston, Thwaits,
Smalbach, Luscot, Davill</f>, or <f type="i">D'Eivill, Nettervil, Bell-house, 
Phillipot, Wahul</f>, &amp;c.

Neither is it to be omitted, that many locall names had
<f type="i">At</f> prefixed before them in olde Evidences, as <f type="i">At More, At
Stow, At Ho, At Bower, At Wood, At Downe</f>, &amp;c. which <f type="i">At</f>,  
</page>

<page no="105">
as it hath beene removed from some, so hath it beene con&shy;
ioyned to other, as <f type="i">Atwood, Atslowe, Atho, Atwell, Atmor</f>.
As S also is ioyned to most now, as <f type="i">Mannors, Knoles, Crofts,
Yates, Gates, Thornes, Groves, Hills, Combes, Holmes, Stokes</f>,
&amp;c. 

Rivers also have imposed names to some men,as they
have to Townes situated on them; as that olde Baron <f type="i">Sur-&shy;
Teys</f>, that is, on the river <f type="i">Teys</f> running betweene Yorkshire
and the Bishoppricke of <f type="i">Duresme, Derwent-water, Eden,
Troutbecke, Hartgill, Esgill, Wampull, Swale, Stoure, Temes,
Trent, Tamar, Grant, Tine, Croc, Lone, Lun, Calder</f>, &amp;c. as
some at <f type="i">Rome</f> were called <f type="i">Tiberij, Anieni, Aufidij, &amp;c</f>, bicause
they were borne neere the rivers <f type="i">Tibris, Anien, Aufidus</f>, as
<f type="i">Iulius Paris</f> noteth.

Divers also had names from trees neere their habitations,
as <f type="i">Oke, Aspe, Box, Alder, Elme, Elder, Beach, Coigniers</f>, that is, 
<f type="i">Quince, Zouch</f>, that is, the trunke of a tree, <f type="i">Cursy</f> and <f type="i">Cur&shy;
son</f>, the stocke of a <f type="i">Vine, Pine, Plumme, Chesney</f> or <f type="i">Cheyney</f>,
that is, <f type="i">Oke, Dauney</f>, that is, <f type="i">Alder, Foulgiers</f>, that is, <f type="i">Fearne,
Vine, Ashe, Hawthorne, Furres, Bush, Hasle, Coularay</f>, that is,
<f type="i">Haslewood, Bucke</f>, that is, <f type="i">Beech, Willowes, Thorne, Broome, 
Blocke</f>, &amp;c. which in former time had <f type="i">at</f> pr&aelig;fixed, as 
<f type="i">at Beech, at Furres, at Ashe, at Elme</f>. And heere is to be no&shy;
ted, that diverse of this sorte have beene strangely contra&shy;
cted, as <f type="i">at Ashe</f> into <f type="i">Tash, at Oke</f> into <f type="i">Toke, at Abbey</f> into
<f type="i">Tabbey</f>; at the End into Thend; As in Saints names, Saint
<f type="i">Olye</f> into <f type="i">Tolye</f>, Saint <f type="i">Ebbe</f> into Saint <f type="i">Tabbe</f>, Saint <f type="i">Osyth</f>
into Saint <f type="i">Tows.</f>

Many strangers also comming hither, and residing here,
were named of their Countries, as <f type="i">Picard, Scot, Lombard,
Flemming, French, Bigod</f>, that is, superstitious, or Norman.
(For so the Frenchmen calld the Normans, because at every
other word they would sweare,) By God; <f type="i">Bretton, Britaine,
Bret, Burgoin, Germain, Westphaling, Dane, Daneis, Man, Gas&shy;
coigne, Welsh, Walsh, Walleys, Irish, Cornish, Cornwallis, Faster&shy;
ling, Maigne, Champneis, Poitevin, Angevin, Loring</f>, that is, <f type="i"><term lang="la">de 
Lotharingia</term></f>, &amp;c. And these had commonly <f type="i">Le</f> pr&aelig;fixed 
</page>

<page no="106">
in Records and Writings, as <f type="i">Le Flemming, Le Picard, Le
Bret, &amp;c. viz</f>. the <f type="i">Flemming</f>, the <f type="i">Picard</f>. 

In respect of situation to other neere places rise these v&shy;
suall names, <f type="i">Norrey, North, South, East, Weast</f>; and likewise 
<f type="i">Northcote, Southcote, Estcote, Westcote</f>; which also had ori&shy;
ginally <f type="i">At</f> set before them. Yea the names of Kitchin,
Hall, Sellar, Parler, Church, Lodge &amp;c. may seeme to have
been borrowed from the places of birth, or most frequent
abode; as among the Greekes, <f type="i">Anatolius</f>. i. East <f type="i">Zephirius</f>,
i. Weast, &amp;c.

Whereas therefore these locall denominations of fami&shy;
lies are of no great antiquitie, I can not yet see why men
should thinke that their Auncestours gave names to pla&shy;
ces, when the places bare those very names, before anie
men did their Surnames. Yea the very terminations of
the names are such as are onely proper and appliable to
places, and not to persons in their significations, if any will
marke the Locall terminations which I lately specified.
Who would suppose Hil, Wood, Field, Ford, Ditch, Pole,
Pond, Towne, or Ton, and such like terminations to bee
convenient for men to beare in their names, vnlesse they
could also dreame Hilles, Woods, Fieldes, Fordes, Ponds,
Pounds &amp;c. to have beene metamorphosed into men by
some supernaturall transformation.

And I doubt not but they will confesse that Townes
stand longer then families continue.

It may also be prooved that many places which nowe
have Lordes denominated of them, had Lordes and ow&shy;
ners of other Surnames, and families not many hundred
yeeres since. But a sufficient proofe it is of antient descent
where the inhabitant had his Surname of the place where
he inhabiteth, as <f type="i">Compton</f> of <f type="i">Compton, Terringham</f> of <f type="i">Ter&shy;
ringham, Egerton</f> of <f type="i">Egerton, Portington</f> of <f type="i">Portington, Skef&shy;
fington</f> of <f type="i">Skeffington, Beeston</f> of <f type="i">Beeston</f>, &amp;c.

I know neverthelesse, that albeit most Townes have
borrowed their names from their situation, and other re&shy;
spectes; yet some with apt terminations have their names 
</page>

<page no="107">
from men, as <f type="i">Edwarston, Alfredston, Vbiford, Malmesbury</f>,
corruptly for <f type="i">Maidulphsbury</f>. But these names were from
fore-names or Christian names, and not from Surnames.
For <f type="i">Ingulphus</f> plainely sheweth, that <f type="i">Wiburton</f>, and <f type="i">Lef&shy;
frington</f> were so named, because two knights, <f type="i">Wiburt</f>, and 
<f type="i">Leofric</f> there sometimes inhabited. But if any should af&shy;
firme that the Gentlemen named <f type="i">Leffrington, Wiburton,
Lancaster</f>, or <f type="i">Leicester, Bossevill</f>, or <f type="i">Shordich</f>, gave the
names to the places so named, I woulde humbly, without
preiudice, crave respite for a further day before I beleeved
them. And to say as I thinke, verily when they shall better
advise themselves, and marke well the terminations of
these, and such like Locall names, they will not presse me
over eagrely heerein.

Notwithstanding, certaine it is that Surnames of fami&shy;
lies have beene adioyned to the names of places for distin&shy;
ction, or to notifie the owner as <f type="i">Melton Mowbray, High&shy;
am Ferrers, Minster-Lovel, Stansted Rivers, Drayton-Bas&shy;
set, Drayton-Beauchamp</f>, &amp;c. for that they were the posses&shy;
sions of <f type="i">Mowbray, Ferrers, Level</f>, &amp;c. Neither do I denie,
but some among vs in former time, aswell as now, dream&shy;
ing of immortalitie of their names, have named their hou&shy;
ses after their owne names, as <f type="i">Camois-Court, Hamons,
Bretts, Bailies, Theobaldes</f>, whenas now they have posses&shy;
sors of other names. And the olde verse is, and alwayes
will be verified of them, which a right worshipfull friend
of mine not long since writ vpon his new house: 

<term lang="la">Nunc mea, mox huius, sed poste&agrave; nescio cuius.</term>
 
Neither must all, having their names from places, sup&shy;
pose that their Auncestors were either Lordes, or posses&shy;
sors of them; but may assure themselves, that they origi&shy;
nally came from them, or were borne at them. But the
Germans and Polonians doe cleare this errour by placing
<f type="i">In</f> before the Locall names, if they are possessours of the
place, or <f type="i">Of</f>, if they onelie were borne at them, as <f type="i">Marti&shy; 
</page>

<page no="108">
nus Cromerus</f> noteth. The like also seemeth to be in vse in
the Marches of <f type="i">Scotland</f>, for there you shall have <f type="i">Trotter</f>
of <f type="i">Folshaw</f>, and <f type="i">Trotter</f> in <f type="i">Fogo, Haitly</f> of <f type="i">Haitly</f>, and <f type="i">Hait&shy;
ly</f> in <f type="i">Haitly</f>. 

Whereas since the time of king <f type="i">Henry</f> the third the
Princes children tooke names from their natall places, as
<f type="i">Edward</f> of <f type="i">Carnarvon, Thomas</f> of <f type="i">Brotherton, Ioanne</f> of <f type="i">A&shy;
cres, Edmund</f> of <f type="i">Woodstocke, Iohn</f> of <f type="i">Gaunt</f>, who named his 
children by <f type="i">Cath. Swinford, Beaufort</f> of the place wher they
wer born is nothing to our purpose, to make further men&shy;
ti<expan type="o+_">on</expan> of th<expan type="e+_">em</expan> whenas they never descended to their posteritie.

After this locall names, the most names in number
have beene derived from Occupations, or Professions, as
Taylor, Potter, Smith, Sadler, Arblaster, that is, <f type="i">Balistarius</f>, 
Archer, Taverner, Chauser, <f type="i">i</f>. Hosier, Weaver, Pointer,
Painter, Walker, <f type="i"><term lang="la">id est</term></f>, Fuller in olde English, Baker, Bax&shy;
ter, <f type="i">Boulengem</f>, all one in signification, Collier, Carpenter,
Ioyner, Salter, Armorer, Spicer, Grocer, Monger, <f type="i"><term lang="la">id est</term></f>,
Chapman, Brewer. Brasier, Webster, Wheeler, Wright,
Cartwright, Shipwright, Banister, <f type="i"><term lang="la">id est</term>, Balneator</f>, Forbi&shy;
sher, Farrar, Goff, <f type="i"><term lang="la">id est</term></f>, Smyth in Welsh. And most which
end in <f type="i">Er</f> in our tongue, as among the Latines, Artificers
names have <f type="i">arius</f>, as <f type="i">lintear<emend err="iar"></emend>ius, vestiarius, calcearius</f>, &amp;c.
or <f type="i">eo</f>, or <f type="i">io</f> for their terminations, as <f type="i">Linteo, Pellio Phrygio</f>.
Neither was there any trade, craft, arte, profession, occupa&shy;
tion never so meane, but had a name among vs common&shy;
ly ending in <f type="i">Er</f>, and men accordingly denominated, but
some are worne out of vse, and therefore the significati&shy;
on vnknowne, and other have beene mollified ridiculou&shy;
sly by the bearers, lest they should seeme vilified by them.
And yet the like names were am<expan type="o+_">on</expan>g the noblest Romans,
as <f type="i"><term lang="la">Figulus, Pictor, Fabritius, Scribonius, Salinator, Rusticus, A&shy;
gricola, Carbo, Funarius</term></f>, &amp;c. And who can deny but they
so named may be Gentlemen, if Virtue which is the soule
of Gentrie shall ennoble them, and <f type="i">Virtus</f> (as one saieth)
<f type="i"><term lang="la">Nulli pr&ealig;lusa est omnibus patet.</term></f> Albeit Doctour <f type="i">Turner</f> in
a Booke against <f type="i">Stephan Gardiner</f> saieth the contrary, ex&shy; 
</page>

<page no="109">
emplifying of their owne names. At which time wise was
the man that tolde my Lord Bishop that his name was not
<f type="i">Gardiner</f>, as the English pronounce it, but <f type="i">Gardiner</f> with
the French accent, and therefore a Gentleman.

Hitherto may be referred many that end in <f type="i">Man</f>, as,
Tubman, Carreman, Coachman, Ferriman, Clothman,
Chapman, Spelman, <f type="i"><term lang="la">id est</term></f>, Learned man, Palfriman, Hors&shy;
man, &amp;c.

Many have beene assumed from offices,as, <f type="i">Chambers,
Chamberlaine, Cooke, Spenser</f>, that is, <f type="i">Steward, Marshall, Lati&shy;
mer</f>, that is, <f type="i">Interpretour, Staller</f>, that is, Constable or Stan&shy;
dard-bearer, <f type="i">Reeve, Woodreeve, Sherife, Sergeant, Parker,
Foster</f>, that is, <f type="i">Nourisher, Forrester</f>, contractly <f type="i">Forster, Hun&shy;
ter, Kempe</f>, that is, Souldier in olde English; (for <f type="i">Alfricus</f> 
translateth <f type="i"><term lang="la">Tiro</term>, <term lang="oe">Yong-Kempe</term>) Faulconer, Fowler, Page, But&shy;
ler, Clarke, Proctor, Abbot, Frier, Monke, Priest, Bishop, Spi&shy;
gurnell</f>, that is, a sealer of Writs, which office was heredita&shy;
rie for a time to the <f type="i">Bohunes</f> of <f type="i">Midherst</f>. Deacon, Deane, 
Bailive, Franklin, Leach, Warder, <f type="i">i</f>. Keeper; &amp; fr<expan type="o+_">om</expan> thence
Woodward, Millward, Steward, Dooreward, that is, Por&shy;
ter, Beareward, Heyward, Hereward, that is, Conserver of
the armie, Bond, that is, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Paterfamilias</term></f>, as it is in the booke
of olde termes belonging sometimes to Saint <f type="i">Augustine</f>
in <f type="i">Canterbury</f>, and we retaine it in the compound <f type="i">Husbond</f>.
In which booke also <f type="i">Horden</f> is interpreted a Steward.

Names also have beene taken of honours, dignities, or
estate, as King, Duke, Prince, Lord, Baron, Knight, Valu&shy;
asor, or Vavasor, Squire, Castellan, partly for that their
ancestours were such, served such, acted such parts, or were
Kings of the Beane, Christmas Lords, &amp;c. And the like
names we reade among the Greeks and Romans, as <f type="i"><term lang="la">Basi&shy;
lius, Archias, Archelaus, Regulus, Servius, Flaminius, C&aelig;sari&shy;
us, Augustulus</term></f>: who notwithstanding were neither Kings,
Priests, Dukes, or <f type="i">C&aelig;sars</f>. Others from the qualities of the
minde, as Good, Through good, Goodman, Goodchild,
Wise, Hardie, Plaine, Light, Meeke, Bold, Best, Prowd,
Sharpe, Still, Sweete, Speede, Quicke, Sure, &amp;c. As those 
</page>

<page no="110">
old Saxon names, <f type="i">Shire</f>, that is, Cleere, <f type="i">Dyre</f>, that is, Wel&shy;
beloved, <f type="i">Blith</f>, that is, merry, <f type="i">Drury</f>, that is, jewell. Also 
these French names, <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Galliard</term></f>, that is, Frolicke, <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Musard</term></f>, that 
is, Delayer, <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Bland</term></f>, that is, Fairespoken, <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Coigne</term></f>, that is, Vali&shy;
ant, <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Baud</term></f>, that is, Pleasant, <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Barrat, Kus, Rush</term></f>, that is, Subtile,
and so is <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Prat</term></f> in the old booke of <f type="i">Petreborrough, Huttin</f>, 
that is, Mutiner. As among the Grecians, <f type="i"><term lang="gkt">Agathias, Andra&shy;
gathius, Sophocles, Eubulus, Eumenius; Thraseas</term></f>. Among
the Romans, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Prudentius, Lepidus, Cato, Pius, Valeus, Con&shy;
stans, Asper, Tacitus, Dulcitius</term></f>, &amp;c. And accordingly names
were borrowed, as <f type="i">Plutarch</f> saieth, from the nature of the
man, from his actions, from some marke, forme or defor&shy;
mitie of his body, as <f type="i"><term lang="la">Macrinus</term></f>, that is, Long, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Torquatus</term></f>, 
that is, Chained, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Sulla</term></f> that is, White and Red: And in like
sort, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Mnemon</term></f>, that is, Mindefull, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Grypus</term></f>, that is, Hawkes-&shy;
nose, <f type="i"><term lang="gkt">Callinicus</term></f>, that is, Faire Victor. From the habitudes
of body, and the perfections or imperfections thereof, ma&shy;
ny names have beene imposed, as <f type="i">Strong, Armstrong, Long,
Low, Short, Broad, Bigge, Little, Speed, Faire, Goodbody, Free
body, Bell</f>, that is, Faire, <f type="i">Bellot</f>, that is, <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Bellulus</term></f>, proper
in <f type="i">French: Helder</f>, that is, Thinne, <f type="i">Heile</f>, that is, Health&shy;
full, <f type="i">Fairfax</f>, that is, Faire-lockes, in antient English Whit&shy;
locks. As those British names still in vse amongst vs, <f type="i">Va&shy;
chan</f>, that is, Little, <f type="i">Moel</f>, that is, Bald, <f type="i">Gam</f>, that is, Crooked,
<f type="i">Fane</f>, that is, Sclender, <f type="i">Grim</f>, that is, Strong, <f type="i">Krich</f>, that is,
Curlepate, <f type="i">Grig</f>, or <f type="i">Krig</f>, that is, Hoarse. No more to bee 
disliked than this Greeke and Roman names, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Nero</term></f>, that is,
Strong, as also <f type="i"><term lang="la">Romulus, Longus, Longinus, Minutius, Macros,
Megasthenes, Calistus, Callisthenes, Paulus, Cincinnatus, Cri&shy;
spus, Caluus, Terentius</term></f>, that is, tender according to <f type="i">Varro</f>. 
<f type="i"><term lang="la">Gracchus</term></f>, that is, Thinne, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Bassus</term></f>, that is, Fatte, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Salustius</term></f>, that
is, Healthfull, and <f type="i"><term lang="la">Cocles</term></f> one-eye. As <f type="i">Papirius Masonius</f> 
reporteth that <f type="i">Philippus Augustus</f> King of <f type="i">France</f>, was sur&shy;
named <f type="i">Borgne</f> for his blinking with one eye. 

Others in respect of age have received names, as <f type="i">Yong,
Olde, Baby, Child Stripling, as with the Romans, Senecio,
Priscus, Iuvenalis, Iunius, Virginius</f>, &amp;c.
</page>

<page no="111">
Some from the time wherein they were borne, as <f type="i">Win&shy;
ter, Summer, Christmas, Day, May, Sunday, Holiday, Munday,
Pascall, Noel, Penticost</f>: as with the ancient Romans, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Ia&shy;
nuarius, Martius, Manius, Lucius, Festus</term></f>, and <f type="i"><term lang="la">Vergilius</term></f> borne
at the rising of the <f type="i"><term lang="la">Vergili&aelig</term></f>;, or seaven starres, as <f type="i">Pontanus</f>
learnedly writeth against them which write his name <f type="i">Vir&shy;
gilius</f>. 

Some from that which they commonly carried, as <f type="i">Pal&shy;
mer</f>, that is, Pilgrime, for that they carried <f type="i">Palme</f> when
they returned from <f type="i">Hierusalem</f>. <f type="i">Long-sword, Broad-speare,
Fortescu</f>, that is, Strong-shield, and in some such respect,
<f type="i">Breake-speare, Shake-Speare, Shotbolt, Wagstaffe, Ba&shy;
got</f>, in the old Norman, the same with <f type="i">Scipio</f>, that is, a stay
or walking staffe with the Latines, which became a sur&shy;
name, for that <f type="i">Cornelius</f> served as a stay to his Blinde fa&shy;
ther. Likewise <f type="i">Biliman, Hookeman, Talevas</f>, of a shield so cal&shy;
led, whereof <f type="i">William</f> sonne of <f type="i">Robert de Belisme</f> Earle of 
<f type="i">Shrewsbury</f> had his name.

Some from parts of the body, as <f type="i">Head, Redhead, White&shy;
head, Legge, Foote, Pollard, Arme, Hand, Lips, Hart</f>, as <f type="i"><term lang="la">Corcu&shy;
lum, Capito, Pedo, Labeo, Naso</term></f>, among the Romans.

Garments have also occasioned names, as <f type="i">Hos, Hosatus,
Hat, Cap, Frocke, Petycote, Gaicoat</f>: as with the Romans, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Ca&shy;
ligula, Caracalla, Fimbria</term></f>, and <f type="i">Hugh Capet</f>, from whom this
last house of <f type="i">France</f> descended, was so called, for that hee
vsed when he was young to snatch off his fellowes caps, if
we believe <f type="i">Du Tillet</f>.

Not a few from colours of their complexions, gar&shy;
ments, or otherwise have gotten names, as <f type="i">White, Blacke,
Browne, Red, Greene</f>, and those Norman names: <f type="i">Rous</f>,
that is, Red, <f type="i">Blunt</f> or <f type="i">Blund</f>, that is, Flaxen haire, and from 
these <f type="i">Russell</f> and <f type="i">Blundell</f>, <f type="i">Gris</f>, that is, Gray, <f type="i">Pigot</f>, that is,
Speckled, <f type="i">Blanch</f> and <f type="i">Blanc</f>, that is, White, with those Bri&shy;
tish or Welsh names, who whereas they were wont to
depaint themselves with sundry colours, have also bor&shy;
rowed many names from the said colours, as <f type="i">Gogh</f>, that is,
Red, <f type="i">Gwin</f>, that is, White, <f type="i">Dee</f>, that is, Blacke, <f type="i">Lhuid</f> or 
</page>

<page no="112">
<f type="i">Flud</f>, that is, Russet: Names to be no more disliked than 
<f type="i"><term lang="la">Albinus, Candidus, Flavius, Fulvius, Fuscus, Burrhus, Coc&shy;
ceius, Rutilius, Rufus, Niger, Nigrinus</term></f>, among the Romans;
and <f type="i"><term lang="gkt">Pyrrhus, Chlorus, Leucagus, Chryses, Melanthius</term></f>, &amp;c. a&shy;
mong the Gr&aelig;cians. 

Some from flowers and fruits, as Lilly, Lis, Rose,
Peare, Nut, Filbert, Peach, Pescod, Vetch, as faire names,
as <f type="i"><term lang="la">Lentulus, Piso, Fabius</term></f>, among the Romans. Others
from beasts, as Lambe, Lion, Boare, Beare, Bucke, Hind,
Hound, Fox, Wolph, Hare, Hog, Roe, Broc, Bad&shy;
ger, &amp;c. Neither are these &amp; such like to be disliked, when&shy;
as among the noblest Romans, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Leo, Ursicinus, Catulus, Lu&shy;
pus, Leporius, Aper, Apronius, Canimus, Castor</term></f>, &amp;c. and <f type="i">Cy&shy;
rus</f>, that is, Dog, with the Persians were very vsuall.

From fishes likewise, as Playce, Salmon, Trowt, Cub,
Gurnard, Herring, Pike, Pikerill, Breme, Burt, Whiting,
Crab, Sole, Mullet, Base, &amp;c. nothing inferiour to the Ro&shy;
man names, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Murena, Phocas, Orata</term></f>, that is, <f type="i">Gilthed</f>, &amp;c. for
that happily they loved those fishes more than other.

Many have beene derived from birds, as <f type="i">Corbet</f>, that
is, Raven, <f type="i">Arondell</f>, that is, Swallow: the Gentlemen of
which name, do beare those birds in their Coat-armours,
<f type="i">Bisset, .i.</f> Dove, Larke, Tisson, Chaffinch, Nitingall, Iay-&shy;
Cocke, Peacocke, Sparrow, Swanne, Crow, Woodcocke,
Eagle, Alecocke, Wilcocke, Handecocke, Hulet or How&shy;
let, Wren, Gosling, Parret, Wilde goose, Finch, Kite, &amp;c.
As good names as these, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Corvinus, Aquilius, Milvius, Gal&shy;
lus, Picus, Falco, Livia, .i.</term></f> Stockedove, &amp;c Therefore I can&shy;
not but mervaile why one should so sadly mervaile such
names of beasts and birds to be in vse in <f type="i">Congo</f> in <f type="i">Africa</f>, 
when they are and have beene common in other Nations,
as well as they were among the <f type="i">Troglodites</f> inhabiting neere 
<f type="i">Congo</f> in former times.

Of Christian names as they have been without change,
many more have beene made, as <f type="i">Francis, Herbert, Guy,
Giles, Leonard, Michael, Lewis, Lambert, Owen, Heowel, Iosce&shy;
lin, Humfry, Gilbert, Griffith, Griffin, Constantine, Iames, Tho&shy; 
</page>

<page no="113">
mas, Blaze, Anthony, Foulke, Godfrey, Gervas, Randall, Alex&shy;
ander, Charles, Daniel</f>, &amp;c. 

Beside these &amp; such like, many surnames are derived from
those Christian names which were in vse about the time of
the Conquest, &amp; are found in the Record calld <f type="i">Doomesday</f>
book, &amp; else where; as <f type="i">Achard, Alan, Alpheg, Aldelme, Au&shy;
cher, Anselin, Anselm, Ansger, Askaeth, Hascuith, Alberic, Ba&shy;
got, Baldric, Bardolph, Belchard, Berenger, Berner, Biso, Bri&shy;
ent, Canute, Knout or Cnute, Carbonell, Chettell, Colf, Corbet
Corven, Crouch, Degory, Dod, Done, Donet</f>, as it seemeth
fr<expan type="o+_">om</expan> <f type="i">Donatus, Dru, Duncan, Durand, Eadid, Edolph, Egenulph,
Elmer, Eudo or Ede, Fabian, Fulcher, Gamelin, Gernegan, Girth,
Goodwin, Godwin, Goodrich, Goodlucke, Grime, Grimbald,
Guncelin, Guthlake, Haco</f> or <f type="i">Hake, Hamon, Hamelin, Har&shy;
ding, Hasting, Herebrand</f>, and many ending in <f type="i">Brand, Her&shy;
man, Hervye Herward, Howard, Heward, Hubald, Hubert,
Huldrich, Iollan, Ioll</f>, contractly from <f type="i">Iulian, Iuo</f>, or <f type="i">Iue, 
Kettell, Leofwin, Lewin, Levin, Liming, Macy, Maino, May&shy;
nerd, Meiler, Murdac, Nele, Norman, Oddo</f> or <f type="i">Hode, Oger,
Olave, Orso</f> or <f type="i">Vrso, Orme, Osborne, Other, Payne, Picotte,
Pipard, Pontz, Puntz, Reyner, Remy, Rolph, Rotroc, Saer,
Searle, Semar, Sewal, Sanchet, Siwald, Siward, Staverd, Star, 
calf, Swain, Sperwicke, Talbot, Toly, Tovy, Turgod, Turrold,
Turstan, Turchill, Vctred</f>, or <f type="i">Oughtred, Vde, Vivian, Voner,
Wade, Walarand, Wistan, Winoc, Walklin, Warner, Winebald,
Wigod, Wigan, Wimarc, Woodnot</f>, &amp;c. 

And not onely these from the Saxons and Normans,
but also many Britan or Welsh Christian names, as well
in ancient time, as lately hath been taken vp for Surnames,
when they came into <f type="i">England</f>, as <f type="i">Chun, Blethin, Kenham</f>,
fr<expan type="o+_">om</expan> <f type="i">Cynan</f> or <f type="i">Conanus, Gittin-Mervin, Bely, Sitsil</f>, or <f type="i">Cesil, Ca&shy;
radoc, Madoc, Rhud, Ithell, Meric, Meredith, Edern, Bedow</f>,
from the English <f type="i">Bede</f>, .i. A devout prayer, beside the
Welsh Christian names vsuall and knowne to all<emend err="">.</emend> As in
like manner many names were made from the <f type="i"><term lang="la">Pr&aelig;nomi&shy;
na</term></f> among the Romans, as <f type="i"><term lang="la">Spurilius, Statilius, Titius</term></f>, from 
<f type="i"><term lang="la">Spurius, Statius, Titus</term></f>, And as <f type="i">Quintilian</f> saieth, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Agnomina</term> 
</page>

<page no="114">
<term lang="la">&amp; cognomina vim nominum obtinuerunt, &amp; pr&aelig;nomina nomi&shy;
num.</term></f> 

By contracting or rather corrupting of Christian names,
we have <f type="i">Terry</f> from <f type="i">Theodoric</f>, <f type="i">Frerry</f> from <f type="i">Frederic, Col&shy;
lin</f> and <f type="i">Cole</f> from <f type="i">Nicholas, Tebald</f> from <f type="i">Theobald, Iessop</f>,
from <f type="i">Ioseph, Aubry</f>, from <f type="i">Alberic, Amery</f>, from <f type="i">Almeric, 
Garret</f>, from <f type="i">Gerrard, Nele</f>, from <f type="i">Nigel, Elis</f>, from <f type="i">Elias,
Bets</f>, from <f type="i">Beatus</f>, as <f type="i">Bennet</f>, from <f type="i">Benedict</f>, &amp;c. 

By addition of <f type="i">S</f> to Christian names, many have beene
taken, as <f type="i">Williams, Rogers, Peters, Peirs, Davies, Harris,
Roberts, Simonds, Guyes, Stevens, Richards, Hughes, Iones</f>,
&amp;c.

From Nicknames or Nursenames, came these (pardon
me if it offend any, for it is but my coniecture,) <f type="i">Bill</f> for
<f type="i">William</f>, <f type="i">Clem</f> for <f type="i">Clement</f>, <f type="i">Nat</f> for <f type="i">Nathaniel</f>, <f type="i">Mab</f> for <f type="i">A&shy;
braham</f>, <f type="i">Kit</f> for <f type="i">Christopher</f>, <f type="i">Mund</f> for <f type="i">Edmund</f>, <f type="i">Hall</f> for
<f type="i">Harry</f>, <f type="i">At</f> and <f type="i">Atty</f> for <f type="i">Arthur</f>, <f type="i">Cut</f> for <f type="i">Cuthberd</f>, <f type="i">Mill</f> for
<f type="i">Miles</f>, <f type="i">Baul</f> and <f type="i">Bald</f> for <f type="i">Baldwin</f>, <f type="i">Ran</f> for <f type="i">Randal</f>, <f type="i">Crips</f>
for <f type="i">Crispin</f>, <f type="i">Turk</f> for <f type="i">Turktetill</f>, <f type="i">Sam</f> for <f type="i">Sampson</f> or <f type="i">Samu&shy;
ell</f>, <f type="i">Pipe</f> for <f type="i">Pipard</f>, <f type="i">Gib</f> for <f type="i">Gilbert</f>. <f type="i">Dan</f> for <f type="i">Daniel</f>, <f type="i">Grig</f> for
<f type="i">Gregory</f>, <f type="i">Bat</f> for <f type="i">Bartholmewe</f>, <f type="i">Law</f> for <f type="i">Lawrence</f>, <f type="i">Tim</f> for
<f type="i">Timothy</f>, <f type="i">Rol</f> for <f type="i">Rolland</f>, <f type="i">Ieff</f> for <f type="i">Ieffrey</f>, <f type="i">Dun</f> for <f type="i">Duncan</f> or
<f type="i">Dunstan</f>, <f type="i">Duke</f> for <f type="i">Marmaduke</f>, <f type="i">Daye</f> for <f type="i">David</f>, <f type="i">God</f>. For
<f type="i">Godfrey</f> or <f type="i">Godard,</f> for otherwise I cannot imagine how
that most holy name vnfit for a man, and not to be tolera&shy;
ted, should be appropriate to any man: and many such like
which you may learne of Nurses.

By adding of <f type="i">S</f> to these Nicknames or Nursenames,
in all probabilitie we have <f type="i">Robins, Nicks, Nicolls, Thoms,
Dickes, Hickes, Wills, Sims, Sams, Iocks, Iucks, Collins, Ienks,
Munds, Hodges, Hobs, Dobs, Saunders</f> from <f type="i">Alexander, Gibs
<f type="i">from</f> Gilbert, Cuts</f> from <f type="i">Cuthberd, Bats</f> from <f type="i">Bartholmewe,
Wats</f> from <f type="i">Walter, Philips</f> from <f type="i">Philip, Haines</f> from <f type="i">Anul&shy;
phus,</f> as some will, for <f type="i">Ainulphesbury</f> in Cambridgshire is 
contracted to <f type="i">Ainsbury,</f> and such like.

Many likewise have beene made by adioyning <f type="i">Kins</f>
and <f type="i">Ins</f> to those nursenames, making them in <f type="i">Kins</f> as it 
</page>

<page no="115">
were diminutives, &amp; those in <f type="i">Ins</f>, as <f type="i">Patronymica</f>. For so <f type="i">Al&shy;
fric</f> Archbishop of <f type="i">Canterbury</f>, &amp; the most ancient Saxon 
Grammarian of our Nation, noteth that names taken
from Progenitours, do end in <f type="i">Ins</f>; so <f type="i">Dickins</f>, that is, little 
<f type="i">Dick</f>, <f type="i">Perkins</f> from <f type="i">Peir</f> or <f type="i">Peter</f>, little <f type="i">Petre</f>; so <f type="i">Tomkins, Wil&shy;
kins, Hutchins, Huggins, Higgins, Hitchins</f>; from <f type="i">Hughe,
Lambkins, Hopkins, Hobkins</f>; from <f type="i">Hob, Dobbins, Robbins,
Atkins</f>; from <f type="i">Arthur, Gibbins, Simkins, Hodgekins, Hoskins,
Watkins, Ienkins, Iennings, Tipkins</f>; from <f type="i">Tibald</f>, <f type="i">Daukins</f>;
from <f type="i">Davy</f>, <f type="i">Rawlins</f> from <f type="i">Raoul</f>, that is, <f type="i">Rafe</f>, and <f type="i">Hankin</f>
for <f type="i">Randoll</f> in Cheshire. In this manner did the Romans
vary names, as <f type="i"><term lang="la">Constans, Constantius, Constantinus; Iustus,
Iustulus, Iustinus, Iustinianus: Aurelius, Aureolus, Aurelianus,
Augustus, Augustinus, Augustinianus, Augustulus</term></f>, &amp;c<emend err=",">.</emend> 

Beside these, there are also other diminitive names af&shy;
ter the French <f type="i"><term lang="la">Analogie</term></f> in <f type="i">Et</f> or <f type="i">Ot</f>, as <f type="i">Willet</f>; from <f type="i">Will</f>,
<f type="i">Haket</f>; from <f type="i">Hake</f>, <f type="i">Bartlet</f>; from <f type="i">Bartholmew</f>, <f type="i">Millet</f> from 
<f type="i">Miles</f>, <f type="i">Huet</f> from <f type="i">Hughe</f>, <f type="i">Allet</f> from <f type="i">Allan</f>, <f type="i">Collet</f> from
<f type="i">Cole</f>, <f type="i">Guyet</f> from <f type="i">Guy</f>, <f type="i">Eliot</f> from <f type="i">Elias</f> and <f type="i">Beckvet</f>, that is,
Little Sharpe nose.

But many more by addition of <f type="i">Son</f>, to the Christian
or Nickname of the father, as <f type="i">Williamson, Richardson, Dick&shy;
son, Harryson, Gibson</f>; for <f type="i">Gilbertson, Simson, Simondson, Ste&shy;
venson, Dauson</f>; for <f type="i">Davison, Morison, Lawson</f>, <term lang="la">id est</term>, <f type="i">Law&shy;
renson, Robinson, Cutbertson, Nicholson, Tomson, Willson, Lew&shy;
eson, Iobson, Waterson, Watson, Peerson</f>, and <f type="i">Pierson, Peterson,
Hanson</f> from <f type="i">Hankin, Wilkinson, Danison</f> for <f type="i">Daniel, Be&shy;
nison</f>, and <f type="i">Benson</f> from <f type="i">Bennet, Denison, Patison</f>; from <f type="i">Pa&shy;
trick, Ienkinson, Matison</f>; from <f type="i">Mathew, Colson</f>; from <f type="i">Cole</f>, 
or <f type="i">Nicholl, Rogerson, Herdson</f>; from <f type="i">Herdingson, Hodgskinson, 
Hugheson, Hulson</f>; from <f type="i">Huldric, Hodson</f> from <f type="i">Hod</f> or <f type="i">Oddo,
Nelson</f> from <f type="i">Neale</f> or <f type="i">Nigell, Davidson, Saunderson, Iohn&shy;
son, Raulson</f>; from <f type="i">Raoul</f> or <f type="i">Ralf</f>. So the ancient Romans 
vsed <f type="i"><term lang="la">Publipor, Marcipor, Lucipor</term></f>; for <f type="i"><term lang="la">Publij puer, Marci puer, 
Lucij puer</term></f>, according to <f type="i">Varro</f>: As afterwards in the <f type="i">Capi&shy;
tolin</f> Tables, they were wont to note both father and
grandfather for proofe of their gentry in abbreviations, 
</page>

<page no="116">
as <f type="i"><term lang="la">A. Sempronius, Aulifilius Lucij Nepos</term></f>, that is, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Aulus Sem&shy;
pronius</term></f>, sonne of <f type="i"><term lang="la">Aulus</term></f>, grandchild or nephew of <f type="i"><term lang="la">Lucius,
C. Martius, L. F. C. N.</term></f> &amp;c. Neither is it true which
some say, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Omnia nomina</f> in</term> <f type="i">Son <term lang="la">sunt boralis generis</term></f>, whenas
it was vsuall in every part of the Realme.

Some also have had names from their mothers, as <f type="i">Fitz-&shy;
parnell, Fitz-Isabell, Fitz-Mary, Fitz-Emme, Maudlens,
Susans, Mawds, Grace, Emson</f>, &amp;c. As <f type="i">Vespasian</f> the Empe&shy;
rour, from <f type="i">Vespasia Polla</f> his mother, and <f type="i">Pop&aelig;a Sabina</f> the 
Empresse, from her grandmother.

In the same sence it continueth yet in them which
descended from the Normans, <f type="i">Fitz-Hughe, Fitz-william,
Fitz-Herbert, Fitz-Geffrey, Fitz-Simon, Fitz-Alan, Fitz-&shy;
Owen, Fitz-Randoll</f>, being names taken from their Pro&shy;
genitours, as among the Irish, <f type="i">Mac-william, Mac-Cone,
Mac-Dermot, Mac-Mahon, Mac-Donell, Mac-Arti</f>, .i. the
sonne of <f type="i">Arthur</f>.

So among the Welsh-Britans likewise, <f type="i">Ap-Robert, Ap-&shy;
Evans, Ab-Yihel, Ap-Hary, Ap-Hughe, Ap-Rice, Ap-&shy;
Richard, Ap-Howell, Ap-Enion, Ap-Owen, Ap-Henry, Ap-&shy;
Rhud</f>, which be contracted into <f type="i">Probert, Bevans, Bythell,
Parry, Pughe, Price, Prichard, Powell, Benion, Bowen, Peurbye,
Prud</f>, &amp;c.

So in the borders of <f type="i">England</f> and <f type="i">Scotland, Gawis Iok</f>,
for <f type="i">Iohn</f> the sonne of <f type="i">Gawin, Richies Edward</f>, for <f type="i">Edward</f>
the sonne of <f type="i">Richard, Iony Riches Will</f>, for <f type="i">William</f> the
sonne of <f type="i">Iohn</f>, sonne of <f type="i">Richard</f>. The like I have heard to
be in vse among the meaner sort in <f type="i">Cornwall</f>.

Daintie was the devise of my Host at <f type="i">Grantham</f>, which
would wisely make a difference of degrees in persons, by
the terminations of names in this word <f type="i">Son</f>, as betweene
<f type="i">Robertson, Robinson, Robson, Hobson; Richardson, Dickson</f>, and 
<f type="i">Dickinson; Willson, Williamson</f>, and <f type="i">Wilkinson, Iackson, Iohn&shy;
son, Ienkinson</f>, as though the one were more worshipful than
the other by his degrees of comparison.

The names of aliance, have also continued in some for
surnames, as where they of one family being of the same 
</page>

<page no="117">
Christian name, were for distinction called <f type="i">R. Le Frere, Le
Fitz, Le Cosin</f>, that is, Brother, the Sonne, &amp;c. all which pas&shy;
sed in time into Surnames. 

Many names also given in merriment for By-names or
Nicke-names have continued to posteritie: as <f type="i">Malduit</f> for
ill schollership, or ill taught, <f type="i">Mallieure</f> commonly <f type="i">Mally&shy;
very</f>, i. <f type="i"><term lang="la">Malus Leporarins</term></f>, for ill hunting the hare, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Pater no&shy;
ster</term></f> for devout praying. The Frenchman, which craftily,
and cleanely conveyed himselfe and his prisoner, <f type="i">T: Crioll</f>
a great Lord in <f type="i">Rent</f>, about the time of King <f type="i">Edward</f> the 
second out of <f type="i">Fraunce</f>, and had therefore <f type="i">Swinfield</f> given
him by <f type="i">Crioll</f>, as I have read, for his fine conveyance, was
then called <f type="i">Fineux</f>, and left that name to his posteritie. So 
<f type="i">Baldwin le-Pettour</f>, who had his name, and held his land
in <f type="i">Suffolke, <term lang="la">Per saltum, sufflum &amp; pettum, sive bumbulum</term></f>, for 
dauncing, pout-puffing, and dooing that before the King
of <f type="i">England</f> in Christmasse holy dayes, which the worde
<f type="i">Pet</f> signifieth in French. Inquire if you vnderstand it not
of <f type="i">Cloacinas</f> chaplaines, or such as are well read in <f type="i">Ajax</f>.

Vpon such like occasions names were given among the
Romans, as <f type="i"><term lang="la">Tremellius</term></f> was called <f type="i"><term lang="la">Scropha</term></f> or Sow, because
when he had hid his neighbours Sow vnder a padde, and
commanded his wife to lie downe thereon; he sware when
the owner came in to seeke the Sow, that hee had no Sow
but the great Sow that lay there, poynting to the padde,
and the Sow his wife. So one <f type="i"><term lang="la">Cornelius</term></f> was surnamed <f type="i"><term lang="la">A&shy;
sina</term></f>, for that when he was to put in assurance for payment
of certaine summes in a purchase, hee brought his Asse la&shy;
den with money, and made ready payment. So <f type="i"><term lang="la">Augustus</term></f>
named his dwarfe <f type="i"><term lang="la">Sarmentum</term></f>, i. sprigge, and <f type="i"><term lang="la">Tiberius</term></f> cal&shy;
led one <f type="i"><term lang="la">Tricongius</term></f>, for carowsing three gallons of wine. So 
<f type="i"><term lang="la">Servilius</term></f> was called <f type="i"><term lang="la">Ala</term></f>, for carrying his dagger vnder his 
arme-pit, when hee killed <f type="i"><term lang="la">Spurius</term></f>. So <f type="i"><term lang="la">Pertinax</term></f> the Em&shy;
peror being stubbernly resolute in his youth to be a wood
monger as his father was, when hee woulde have made
him a Scholler, was named <f type="i">Pertinax</f>. So the father of <f type="i">Va&shy;
lens</f> the Emperour, who was Camp-maister heere in <f type="i">Bri&shy; 
</page>

<page no="118">
taine</f>, for his fast holding a rope in his youth, which tenne
souldiers could not plucke from him, was called Funarius.
About which time also <f type="i">Paul</f> a Spaniard, a common In&shy;
former in <f type="i">Britaine</f> was named <f type="i">Catena</f>, i. the Chaine, for
that he chained and fettered many good men heere, with
linking together false surmises, to their vtter vndooing in
the time of <f type="i"><term lang="la">Constantinus</term></f>, who also that I may remember it
in passage, named his attendant scholler by no vnfitting
name, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Musonius</term></f>. But what names the beastly monster, ra&shy;
ther than Emperour <f type="i"><term lang="la">Comm<emend err="m"></emend>odus</term></f> gave to his attendants, I
dare not mention, lest I should be immodestly offensive to
chaste eares, and modest mindes. But hitherto with mo&shy;
desty may be referred this of the familie of <f type="i"><term lang="gkt">Gephyri</term></f>, i. <f type="i">Brid&shy;
ges</f> in <f type="i">Greece</f>, who tooke their name from a Bridge; for
when their mother was delivered of nine children at a
birth, and in a foolish feare had privily sent seven of them
to be drowned at a bridge, the father sodainely comming
to the bridge, saved them, and thereupon gave them that
name. Of these, and the like, we may say, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Propiora sunt ho&shy;
nori, qu&agrave;m ignomini&aelig</term></f>;. Infinite are the occasions which in 
like manner have made names to persons, I will onely re&shy;
porte one or two French examples, that thereby you may
imagine of others in other places and former ages. 

In the first broyles of <f type="i">Fraunce</f>, certaine companies ran&shy;
ging themselves into troupes, one Captaine tooke newe
names to himselfe and his company from the furniture of
an horse. Among these new named gallants, you might
have heard of, <f type="i">Monsieur Saddle</f>, (to english them) <f type="i">Moun&shy;
sieur Bridle, Le Croupier, Le Girte, Horshooe, Bitte, Trappiers,
Hoofe, Stirrope, Curbe, Musrole, Frontstall</f>, &amp;c. Most of the
which had their pasport, as my Author noteth, by <f type="i">Seigneur
de la Halter</f>. Another Captaine there also gave names to
his, according to the places where he found th<expan type="e+_">em</expan>, as <f type="i">Hodge,
Hieway, River, Pond, Vine, Stable, Street, Corner, Gallows, Ta&shy;
verne, Tree</f>, &amp;c. And I have heard of a consort in <f type="i">England</f>, 
who when they had served at Sea, tooke names from the
equipage of a shippe, when they would serve themselves 
</page>

<page no="119">
at land, as, <f type="i">Keele, Ballast, Planke, Fore-decke, Decke, Loope-&shy;
hole, Pumpe, Rudder, Gable, Anchor, Misen saile, Capson,
Maste, Bolt</f>.. So that is true which <f type="i">Isidore</f> saieth, <f type="i">Names
are not alwayes given according to Nature, but some after our
owne will and pleasure, as we name our landes and servants ac&shy;
cording to our owne liking.</f> And the Dutchmans saying may
be verified, which when he heard of, Englishmen called
God and Divell, saide, that the English borrowed names
from all things whatsoever, good or bad. 

It might be heere questioned, whether these surnames
were assumed and taken at the first by the persons them&shy;
selves, or imposed and given vnto them by others. It may
aswell seeme that the locall names of persons were partely
taken vp by themselves, if they were owners of the place,
as given by the people, who have the soveraignty of words
and names, as they did in the Nicke-names before Sur&shy;
names were in vse. For who would have named himselfe,
Peaceable, Vnready, Without-land, Beauclerke, Strong&shy;
bow, Gagtooth, Blanch-mayne, Bossue, i. Crook-backe,
but the concurrent voyce of the people, as the women
neighbours gave the name to <f type="i">Obed</f> in the booke of <f type="i">Ruth</f>;
and likewise in Surnames. In these pretty names, as I may
terme them, from floures, fishes, birdes, habitudes, &amp;c: it
may bee thought that they came from Nurses in former
times heere, as very many, or rather most in <f type="i">Ireland</f> and 
<f type="i">Wales</f> doe at this present. These Nicke-names of one sil&shy;
lable turned to surnames, as <f type="i">Dickes, Nickes, Toms, Hobbes,
&amp;c</f>. may also seeme to proceed from Nurses, to their Nur&shy;
slings; or from fathers and maisters to their boyes and ser&shy;
vants. For, as according to the old proverb, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Omnis herus
servo Monosyllabus</term></f>, in respect of their short commands: so
<f type="i"><term lang="la">Omnis servus hero Monosyllabus</term></f> in respect of the curtolling
their names, as <f type="i">Wil, Sim, Hodge</f>, &amp;c. Neither is it improba&shy;
ble, but that many names that seeme vnfitting for men, as
of brutish beasts, &amp;c. came fr<expan type="o+_">om</expan> the very signes of the hou&shy;
ses where they inhabited; for I have heard of them which
said they spake of knowledge, that some in late time dwel&shy;
</page>

<page no="120">
ling at the signe of the Dolphin, Dull, White-horse, Rac&shy;
ket, Peacocke, &amp;c. were commonly called <f type="i">Thomas</f> at the
<f type="i">Dolphin, Will</f> at the <f type="i">Bull, George</f> at the <f type="i">White horse, Robin</f>
at the <f type="i">Racket</f>, which names as many other of like sort, with
omitting <f type="i">At</f>, became afterward hereditarie to their childr<expan type="e+_">en</expan>. 

Heereby some insight may bee had in the originall of
Surnames, yet it is a matter of great difficultie, to bring
them all to certain heads, whenas our language is so great&shy;
ly altered, so many new names daily brought in by Aliens,
as French, Scots, Irish, Welch, Dutch, &amp;c. and so many old
words worne out of vse. I meane not only in the old En&shy;
glish, but also the late Norman, for who knoweth nowe
what these names were, <f type="i">Giffard, Basset, Gernon, Mallet, Ho&shy;
ward, Peverell, Paganell</f>, or <f type="i">Paynell, Taile boise, Talbot, Lovet, 
Pancevolt, Tirrell</f>, &amp;c. which are nothing lesse than locall,
and certainely significative, for they are never noted, as I
said before in olde evidences with <f type="i">De</f> as locall names, but
alwayes absolutely, as <f type="i">W. Giffard, R. Basset</f>, as Christian
names are, when they are made Surnames; and yet I will
not affirme, that all these heere mentioned were at anie
time Christian names, although doubtlesse some were.

For wee knowe the significations of some of them, as
<f type="i">Mallet</f>, an Hammer, <f type="i">Bigot</f>, a Norman, or superstitious 
<f type="i">Tailebois</f>. i. <f type="i">Cutwood</f>, <f type="i">Lovet</f>, Little Woolfe, and <f type="i">Basset</f>, (as
some thinke) Fatte; <f type="i">Giffard</f> is by some interpreted Libe&shy;
rall; and <f type="i">Howard</f>, High Warden, or Guardian (as it seem&shy;
eth an office now out of vse) whenas <f type="i">Heobeorg</f> signified in
olde English High defence, and <f type="i">Heoh-f&aelig;der</f>, Patriarch or
High father. Certaine it is, that the first of that right no&shy;
ble family who was knowne by the name of <f type="i">Howard</f>, was
the sonne of <f type="i">William de Wigenhall</f>, as the honourable Lord
<f type="i">William Howard</f> of <f type="i">Naworth</f>, third sonne to <f type="i">Thomas</f> late
Duke of <f type="i">Norffolke</f>, an especial searcher of Antiquities, who
equalleth his high parentage with his vertues, hath lately
discovered. 

To find out the true originall of Surnames, is full of dif&shy;
ficulty, so it is not easie to search all the causes of alterati&shy; 
</page>

<page no="121">
ons of Surnames, which in former ages have beene verie
common among vs, and have so intricated, or rather, ob&shy;
scured the trueth of our Pedegrees, that it will be no little
hard labour to deduce many of them truly from the Con&shy;
quest; Somewhat neverthelesse shall be said thereof, but
more shall be left for them which will sound deeper into
this matter. 

To speake of alteration of names, omitting them of
<f type="i">Abraham</f>, and <f type="i">Sara, Iacob</f>, and <f type="i">Israel</f>, in holy Scriptures,
I have observed that the change of names, hath most com&shy;
monly proceeded from a desire to avoyd the opinion of
basenes. So <f type="i">Codomarus</f> when he succeeded <f type="i">Ochus</f> in the
kingdome of <f type="i">Persia</f>, called himselfe by the princely name 
<f type="i">Darius</f>. So new names were given to them which were
deified by the Paganish consecration, as <f type="i"><term lang="la">Romulus</term></f> was cal&shy;
led <f type="i"><term lang="la">Quirmus</term></f>, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Melicertus</term></f> was called <f type="i"><term lang="la">Portunus</term></f>, and <f type="i"><term lang="la">Pal&aelig;mon</term></f>,
Likewise in adoptions into better families and testament,
as the sonne of <f type="i"><term lang="la">L. &AElig;milius</term></f>, adopted by <f type="i"><term lang="la">Scipio</term></f>, tooke the
name of <f type="i"><term lang="la">Scipio Africanus</term></f>. So <f type="i"><term lang="la">Augustus</term></f> who was first na&shy;
med <f type="i"><term lang="la">Thureon</term></f>, tooke the name of <f type="i"><term lang="la">Octavian</term></f> by testament:
by enfranchising also into new Citties, as he which first
was called <f type="i"><term lang="la">Lucumo</term></f>, when he was infranchised at <f type="i">Rome</f>,
tooke the name of <f type="i"><term lang="la">Lucius Tarquinius Priscus</term></f>. So <f type="i"><term lang="la">Deme&shy;
trius Mega</term></f> when he was there made free of the Citty, was
called <f type="i"><term lang="la">Publius Cornelius</term>. Cicero Epist. 36. lib</f>. 13.

Likewise slaves when they were manumised, tooke
often their masters names, whenas they had but one name
in their servile state. As they which have read <f type="i"><term lang="la">Artemidorus</term></f>,
do know, how a slave, who when he dreamed he had <f type="i"><term lang="la">tria
virilia</term></f>, was made free the next morning, and had three
names given him.

Neither is it to be forgotten, that men were not forbid&shy;
den to change name or surname, by the rescript of <f type="i">Diocle&shy;
sian L. Vinc. c. de mutat. nom</f>. so be that it were <f type="i"><term lang="la">Sine aliqua
fraude, iure licito</term></f>. As that great Philosopher which was
first called <f type="i">Malchus</f> in the <f type="i">Syrian</f> tongue, tooke the name
of <f type="i">Porphyrius</f>, as <f type="i">Eunapius</f> reporteth: as before <f type="i">Suetonius</f> 
</page>

<page no="122">
the Historian <emend err="l">t</emend>ooke to surname Tranquillus, whenas his fa&shy;
ther was <f type="i">Suetonius Lenis</f>. Those notwithstanding of
strange base parentage were forbidden <f type="i"><term lang="la">L. super statu c.
de qu&aelig;st</term></f>. to insert, or inthrust themselves into noble and ho&shy;
nest families by changing their names, which will growe
to inconvenience in <f type="i">England</f>, as it is thought, by reason that
Surnames of honourable and worshipfull families are gi&shy;
ven now to meane mens children for Christian names,
as it is growen nowe in <f type="i">Fraunce</f>, to the confusion of their
Gentry, by taking new names from their purchased landes
at their pleasures. Among the Romans nevertheles they
that were called <f type="i"><term lang="la">ad Equestrem ordinem</term></f>, having base names,
were new named <f type="i"><term lang="la">nomine ingenuorum veterum<expan type="q',">que</expan> Romano&shy;
rum</term></f>, lest the name should disgrace the dignitie, when ac&shy;
cording to <f type="i">Plato</f>, comely things should have no vncomely
names. 

It was vsuall amongest the Christians in the Primitive
church, to change at Baptisme the names of <f type="i"><term lang="la">Catechumeni</term></f>,
which were in yeeres, as that impious <f type="i"><term lang="la">Renegado</term></f>, that was
before called <f type="i"><term lang="la">Lucius</term></f>, was in his Baptisme called <f type="i">Lucianus</f>.
So the Popes vse to change their names, when they enter
into the Papacie, which as <f type="i">Platina</f> saith, was begunne by
Pope <f type="i">Sergius</f> the second, who first changed his name, for
that his former name was <f type="i">Hogges-mouth</f>, but other referre
the change of names in Popes to Christ, who changed <f type="i">Si&shy;
mon</f> into <f type="i">Peter, Iohn</f> and <f type="i">Iames</f> into <f type="i">Bonarges</f>: Onely <f type="i">Mar&shy;
cellus</f>, not long since chosen Pope, refused to chaunge his
name, saying, <f type="i">Marcellus</f> I was, and <f type="i">Marcellus</f> I will be, I 
will neither change Name nor Manners. Other religious
men also when they entred into some Orders, chaunged
their name in times past, following therein, (as they re&shy;
port) the Apostle, that chaunged his name from <f type="i">Saule</f> to 
<f type="i">Paule</f>, after he entred into the Ministery, borrowing (as
some say) that name from <f type="i"><term lang="la">Sergius Paulus</term></f> the Roman liev&shy;
tenant, but as other will from his lowe stature, for hee was
but three cubites high, as Saint <f type="i">Chrysostome</f> speaking of
him. <f type="i"><term lang="la">Tricubitalis ille tamen c&oelig;lum ascendit.</term></f> 
</page>

<page no="123">
Of changing also Christian names in Confirmation we
have saide before; but overpassing these forraine matters
let vs say somewhat as concerning chaunge of names in
<f type="i">England</f>. 

As among the French in former time, and also nowe,
the heire tooke the fathers surname, and the yonger sonnes
tooke names of their landes allotted vnto them. So like&shy;
wise in times past did they in <f type="i">England</f>; and the most com&shy;
mon alteration proceeded from place of habitation. As if
<f type="i">Hugh</f> of <f type="i">Suddington</f> gave to his second sonne his Mannour
of <f type="i">Frydon</f>, to his third sonne his Mannour of <f type="i">Pantley</f>, to
his fourth his Wood of <f type="i">Albdy</f>; the sonnes calld themselves
<f type="i">De Frydon, De Pantley, De Albdy</f>; and their posteritie remo&shy;
ved <f type="i">De</f>. So <f type="i">Hugh Montfortes</f> second sonne called <f type="i">Richard</f>
being Lord of <f type="i">Hatton</f> in <f type="i">Warwickeshire</f> tooke the name of 
<f type="i">Hatton</f>. So the yongest sonne of <f type="i">Simon de Montfort</f> Earle of 
<f type="i">Leicester</f> staying in <f type="i">England</f>, when his father was slaine, and 
brethren fled, tooke the name of <f type="i">Welsborne</f>, as some of that
name have reported. So the name of <f type="i">Ever</f> came from the
Mannour of <f type="i">Ever</f>, neere <f type="i">Vxbridge</f>, to yonger sonnes of L. 
<f type="i">Iohn Fitz-Robert de Clavering</f>, from whom the Lorde <f type="i">E&shy;
vers</f>, and sir <f type="i">Peter Evers</f> of <f type="i">Axholme</f> are descended So sir
<f type="i">Iohn Cradocke</f> knight great grandfather of sir <f type="i">Henry New&shy;
ton</f> of <f type="i">Somersetshire</f> tooke first the name of <f type="i">Newton</f>, which
was the name of his habitation: as the issue of <f type="i">Huddard</f> in 
<f type="i">Cheshire</f> tooke the name of <f type="i">Dutton</f>. 

B<emend err="n">u</emend>t for varietie and alteration of names in one familie 
vpon divers respects, I will give you one <f type="i">Cheshire</f> exam&shy;
ple for all, out of an antient roule belonging to sir <f type="i">William
Brerton</f> of <f type="i">Brerton</f> knight, which I sawe twenty yeares
since. Not long after the Conquest <f type="i">William Bellward</f> lord
of the moietie of <f type="i">Malpasse</f>, had two sonnes, <f type="i">Dan-David</f> of 
<f type="i">Malpasse</f>, surnamed <f type="i">Le Clerke</f>, and <f type="i">Richard; Dan-David</f> 
had <f type="i">William</f> his eldest sonne surnamed <f type="i">De Malpasse</f>, from
whom the Baron <f type="i">Dudley</f> is descended by heire generall.
His second sonne was named <f type="i">Philip Gogh</f>, one of the issue
of whose eldest sonnes tooke the name of <f type="i">Egerton</f>; a third 
</page>

<page no="124">
sonne tooke the name of <f type="i">David Golborne</f>, and one of his
sonnes the name of <f type="i">Goodman. Richard</f> the other sonne of
the aforesaid <f type="i">William Belward</f> had three sonnes, who tooke
also divers names, <f type="i">viz. Thomas de Cotgrave, William de O&shy;
verton</f>, and <f type="i">Richard Little</f>, who had two sonnes, the one
named <f type="i">Ken-clarke</f>, and the other <f type="i">Iohn Richardson</f>. Heerein
you may note alteration of names in respect of habitation
in <f type="i">Egerton, Cotgrave, Overton</f>, in respect of colour in <f type="i">Gogh</f>, 
that is, Red, in respect of qualitie in him that was called
<f type="i">Goodman</f>, in respect of stature in <f type="i">Richard Little</f>, in respect 
of learning in <f type="i">Ken-clarke</f>, in respect of the fathers Christi&shy;
an name in <f type="i">Richardson</f>, all descending from <f type="i">William Bell&shy;
ward</f>. And verily the Gentlemen of those so different
names in <f type="i">Cheshire</f> would not easily be induced to beleeve
they were descended from one house, if it were not war&shy;
ranted by so antient a proofe. 

In respect of stature I could recite to you other exam&shy;
ples, but I will onely adde this which I have read, that a
yong Gentleman of the house of <f type="i">Preux</f>, being of tall sta&shy;
ture attending on the Lord <f type="i">Hungerford</f>, Lord Treasourer
of <f type="i">England</f>, was among his fellowes called Long <f type="i">H</f>: who
after preferred to a good marriage by his Lorde, was called
<f type="i">H. Long</f>, that name continued to his posteritie, knights and
men of great worship.

Other took their mothers surnames, as <f type="i">A. Audley</f> yon&shy;
ger brother to <f type="i">Iames</f> lord <f type="i">Audley</f>, marrying the daughter
and heire of <f type="i">H. de Stanley</f> left a sonne <f type="i">William</f>, that tooke 
the name of <f type="i">Stanley</f>, from whome <f type="i">Stanley</f> Earle of <f type="i">Derby</f>,
a<emend err="u">n</emend>d other of that name are descended. <f type="i">Geffrey</f> the sonne of
<f type="i">Robert Fitz-Maldred</f>, and <f type="i">Isabel</f> his wife, heire of the Nor&shy;
man house of the <f type="i">Nevilles</f>, tooke the name of <f type="i">Nevill</f>, and
left it to his posteritie which was spread into very manie
honourable families of <f type="i">England</f>. In like manner the sonne
of <f type="i">Ioscelin</f> of <f type="i">Lovan</f> a yoonger sonne to the Duke of <f type="i">Bra&shy;
bant</f>, when he had married <f type="i">Agnes</f> the onely daughter of 
<f type="i">William</f> Lord <f type="i">Percy</f> so named of <f type="i">Percy</f> forrest in the coun&shy;
ty of <f type="i">Maen</f>, from whome they came, (and not of piercing 
</page>

<page no="125">
the king of <f type="i">Scots</f> through the eie, as <f type="i">Hector Boetius</f> fableth) 
his sonne and posteritie vppon a composition with the
same Lady, tooke her name of <f type="i">Percy</f>, but retained theyr
olde coate armour, to shew from whome they descended;
So <f type="i">Adam de Montgomery</f> marrying the daughter and heire
of <f type="i">Carew</f> of <f type="i">Molesford</f>, her sonne relinquishing his owne,
left to his posteritie his mothers name <f type="i">Carew</f>, from whom
the Barons <f type="i">Carew</f>, the <f type="i">Carews</f> of <f type="i">Haccomb</f>, of <f type="i">Berry</f>, of <f type="i">An&shy;
thony, Beddington</f>, &amp;c. have had their names and originall.
Likewise <f type="i">Ralph Gernon</f> marrying the daughter of <f type="i">Caven&shy;
dish</f>, or <f type="i">Candish</f>, left that name to his issue, as <f type="i">Th: Talbot</f>,
a learned Genealogist hath prooved. So <f type="i">Robert Meg</f> the
great favourite of king <f type="i">Iohn</f> took the name of <f type="i">Braybrooke</f>, 
whereof his mother was one of the heires. So sir <f type="i">Iohn de
Haudlow</f> marrying the daughter and heire of the Lorde
<f type="i">Burnell</f>, his posteritie tooke the name of <f type="i">Burnell</f>. So sir 
<f type="i">Tibauld Russell</f> tooke the name of <f type="i">De Gorges</f> to him and
his issue, for that his mother was sister and one of the heirs 
of <f type="i">Ralfe de Gorges</f>, as it appeareth in the controversie be&shy;
tweene <f type="i">Warbleton</f> and the saide <f type="i">Tibauld de Corges</f> for the 
coate of Armes <f type="i">Lozengy, Or</f>, and <f type="i">Azure</f>, 21. of <f type="i">Edward</f> 
the third, before <f type="i">Henry</f> Earle of <f type="i">Lancaster</f>, and others, at 
the siege of Saint <f type="i">Margaret</f>. Not many years since, when 
<f type="i">Iames Horsey</f> had married the daughter of <f type="i">De-Le-vale</f> of 
<f type="i">Northumberland</f>, his issue tooke the name of <f type="i">De-la-vale</f>. 

Heerevnto may they also bee referred who changed
their names in remembrance of their Progenitours being
more honourable, as the sonnes of <f type="i">Geffrey Fitz-Petre</f>,
tooke the name of <f type="i">Magnavilla</f> or <f type="i">Mandevile</f>, when they
came to be Earles of <f type="i">Essex</f>, because their grandmother <f type="i">Be&shy;
atrix</f> was of the house of <f type="i">Mandevile</f>, as appeareth by the
Abby booke of <f type="i">Walden</f>. So <f type="i">Thomas de Molton</f> tooke the
name of <f type="i">Lucy</f>, and many other, which I omit.

Others also have taken the name of them whose lands
they had: As when King <f type="i">Henry</f> the first gave the lands of
the attainted <f type="i">Robert Moubray</f> Earle of <f type="i">Northumberland</f>,
being 120. Knights fees in <f type="i">Normandy</f>, and 140. in <f type="i">Eng&shy;  
</page>

<page no="126">
land</f>, to <f type="i">Nigall</f> or <f type="i">Neale de Albeney</f> his bow-bearer, who in
the battell at Trenchbray, tooke <f type="i">Robert</f> Duke of Norman&shy;
dy prisoner: he commanded withall, that his posteritie
should take the Surname of <f type="i">Mombray</f>, which they accor&shy;
dingly did, and retained the same as long as the issue male
continued, which determined in <f type="i">Iohn Moubray</f> Duke of
<f type="i">Norfolke</f>, in the time of King <f type="i">Edward</f> the fourth: whose
heires were married into the families of <f type="i">Howard</f> and
<f type="i">Barkeley</f>. 

Remembrance of benefits made others to change their
names, as <f type="i">William Mortimer</f> descended from those of <f type="i">Ri&shy;
chards</f> Castle, tooke the name of <f type="i">La-Zouch</f>, and named
his sonne <f type="i">Alan de la-Zouch</f>, for some favour received from
the Lord <f type="i">Zouch</f> of <f type="i">Ashby de la-Zouch</f>, as appeareth by <f type="i">In&shy;
quisition</f>. 11. &amp; 21. <f type="i">Ed</f>. 3.

In respect of adoption also, very many in all ages have
changed their names: I neede not particulate it, for all know
it. Some of their owne dislike of their names, have alte&shy;
red them: for as I have read in the booke of <f type="i">Fornesse, Wil&shy;
liam Fitz-Gilbert</f> Baron of <f type="i">Kendall</f>, obtained licence of
King <f type="i">Henry</f> the second, to change his name and call him&shy;
selfe and his posteritie <f type="i">Lancaster</f>, from whom the <f type="i">Lanca&shy;
sters</f> in <f type="i">Westmorland</f> &amp;c. are descended.

Heerevpon some thinke that without the Kings li&shy;
cence new names cannot be taken, or old names given a&shy;
way to others. Yet <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Tiraquell</term></f> the great <f type="i">Civilian</f> of <f type="i">France, in
Leg. quin. Conub. Tit</f>. 92. seemeth to incline, that both name
and Armes may be transferred by will and testament, and
produceth <f type="i">Augustus</f>, who by his Testament commanded
<f type="i">Tiberius</f> and <f type="i">Livia</f> to beare his name. How in former times 
<f type="i">Herevile</f>, <f type="i">Dunvile, Clanwowe</f>, gave and granted away their 
Armes, which are as silent names, distinctions of families;
and the same was thought vnlawfull afterward, when the
Lord <f type="i">Hoo</f> would have done the same, shall be declared in
more convenient place. But the inconvenience of change
of names, hath beene discovered to be such in <f type="i">France</f>, that
it hath beene propounded in Parlament at <f type="i">Dijon</f>, that it 
</page>

<page no="127">
should not be permitted but in these two respects, eyther
when one should bee made heire to any with especiall
words, to assume the name of the testatour; or when any
one should have a donation surmounting a thousand
crownes, vpon the same condition. But to retyre to our
purpose. 

Not a few have assumed the names of their fathers
Baronies, as in former times the issue of <f type="i">Richard Fitz-Gil&shy;
bert</f>, tooke the name of <f type="i">Clare</f>, which was their Barony: and
in late time, since the <f type="i">Suttons</f> came to the Barony of <f type="i">Dud&shy;
ley</f>, all their issue tooke the name of <f type="i">Dudleyes</f>: that I may
omit others. The dislike of others hath caused also a
change of names, for King <f type="i">Edward</f> the first, disliking the
iteration of <f type="i">Fitz</f>, commanded the Lord <f type="i">Iohn Fitz-Robert</f>,
a most ancient Baron, (whose Ancestours had continued
their Surnames by their fathers Christian names,) to leave
that manner, and to be called <f type="i">Iohn Clavering</f>, which was
the capitall seate of his Barony. And in this time, many that
had followed that course of naming by <f type="i">Fitz</f>, tooke them
one setled name, and retained it as <f type="i">Fitz Walter</f>, <emend err="andothers">and others</emend>.

Also at that time the names of <f type="i">Thomson, Richardson,
Willson</f>, and other of that forme began to be setled, which
before had varied according to the name of the father. <f type="i">Ed&shy;
ward</f> the fourth likewise (as I have heard,) loving some
whose name was <f type="i">Picard</f>, would often tell them that hee
loved them well, but not their names, wherevpon some of
them changed their names: and I have heard that one of
them which tooke the name of <f type="i">Ruddle</f>, being the place of
his birth in that respect. And in late yeares in the time of
King <f type="i">Henry</f> the eight, an ancient worshipfull gentleman
of <f type="i">Wales</f>, being called at the panniell of Iury by the name
of <f type="i">Thomas Ap William, Ap Thomas, Ap Richard, Ap Hoel,
Ap Evan Vaghan</f>, &amp;c. was advised by the Iudge to leave
that old manner. Wherevpon he after called himselfe <f type="i">Mo&shy;
ston</f>, according to the name of his principall house, and left
that Surname to his posterity.

Offices have brought new names to divers families, as 
</page>

<page no="128">
when <f type="i">Edward Fitz-Theobald</f> was made Butler of <f type="i">Ireland</f>,
the Earles of <f type="i">Ormond</f> and others descended from them,
tooke the name of <f type="i">Butler</f>. So the distinct families of the 
<f type="i">Constables</f> in the County of <f type="i">Yorke</f>, are saide to have ta&shy;
ken that name, from some of their Ancestours which bare
the office of Constables of some Castles. In Like manner
the <f type="i">Stewards, Marshalls, Spencers</f>. That I may say nothing
of such as for well acting on the stage, have carried away
the names of the personages which they acted, and have
lost their owne names among the people.

Schollers pride hath wrought alteration in some names
which have beene sweetned in sound, by drawing them to
the Latine <f type="i">Analogie</f>. As that notable Non-resident in our
fathers time Doctor <f type="i">Magnus</f>, who being a foundling at
<f type="i">Newark</f> vppon <f type="i">Trent</f>, where hee erected a Grammer
schoole, was called by the people <f type="i">T. Among vs</f>, for that he
was found among them: But he profiting in learning, tur&shy;
ned <f type="i">Among vs</f>, into <f type="i">Magnus</f>, and was famous by that name,
not onely heere, but also in forraine places where he was
often Ambassadour.

It were needelesse to note heere againe, how many
have taken in former times the Christian name of their
father, with prefixing of <f type="i">Fitz</f> or <f type="i">Filz</f>, as <f type="i">Fitz-hugh, Fitz-&shy;
alan, Fitz-william</f>, or adding of <f type="i">Son</f>, as <f type="i">Richardson, Tomson, 
Iohnson</f>, &amp;c. and so altered their Surnames if they had any.
Whereas divers ancient Gentlemen of <f type="i">England</f> do beare
Coates of Armes, which by old roules and good proofes
are knowne to belong to other names and families, and
cannot make proofe that they matched with those fami&shy;
lies, it is worth observation, considering how religious
they were in elder times in keeping their owne Armes:
whether they were not of those ancient houses whose
Armes they beare, and have changed their names in re&shy;
spect of their habitation, or partitions and lands gotten by
their wives. As <f type="i">Pickering</f> of the North, beareth Ermin a
Lion rampant Azure crowned, Or, which, as it is in the
old Abby booke of <f type="i">Furnesse</f>, was the coat of <f type="i">Roger de Miy&shy; 
</page>

<page no="129">
thorp</f>. In the same booke the coate of <f type="i">Dacre, Gules 3. Esca&shy;
lopes Arg</f>. is the coate of <f type="i">R. Gerneth</f> of <f type="i">Cumberland</f>, and so
the three pillowes Ermin of <f type="i">Redman</f> of <f type="i">Northumberland</f>, is
the coate of <f type="i">Ran. de Greystock</f>. So <f type="i">Vfford</f> E. of <f type="i">Suffolk</f>, and 
<f type="i">Peiton, Fetiplace</f>, and <f type="i">Hide</f>, and many other Gentlemen of
the same Armes, may seeme to haue beene of the same
stocke, and to haue varied their names in diuers respects.

Finally, among the common people which sway all in
names, many Surnames haue bene changed in respect of
occupations, and not a few have beene changed in respect
of masters, for in every place we see the youth very com&shy;
monly called by the names of their ocupations, as <f type="i">Iohn</f> Ba&shy;
ker, <f type="i">Thomas</f> Taylor, <f type="i">Will</f> Butcher, <f type="i">Dic</f> Barber, and many
by their masters names, as <f type="i">Iohn Pickering, Thomas Watkins,
Nicholas French</f>, whenas they served maisters of those
names, which often were conveyed to their posteritie, and
their owne Surnames altogether forgotten. Some other
causes of alteration of names may be found, as for crimes
committed when men have beene enforced to leave their
countries. But heereby it may be vnderstood that an <f type="i">Alias</f>
or double name cannot preiudice the honest: and it is
knowne that when Iudge <f type="i">Catiline</f> tooke exception at one
in this respect, saying, that no honest man had a double
name, &amp; came in with an <f type="i">Alias</f>. The party asked him what
exception his Lordship could take to Iesus Christ, Alias,
Iesus of <f type="i">Nazareth</f>.

I doubt not but some men among vs in changing their
names, do imitate olde Goffer <f type="i">Simon</f> the Cobbler in <f type="i">Lu&shy;
cian</f>, who when he grew fat in the purse, would needes be
called for Goodman <f type="i">Simon</f>. Master <f type="i"><term lang="gkt">Simonides</term></f>, as some wo&shy;
men do follow the good Greeke wench, <f type="i"><term lang="gkt">Melissarion</term></f>, that
is, Pretty honny-Bee, who when of a Commediant shee
became a wealthy mans wife, would be saluted Madam
<f type="i"><term lang="gkt">Pithias</term></f>, or <f type="i">Prudence</f>. And some likewise can change them&shy;
selves from shee, to hee, and conseque<emend err="e">n</emend>tly their name, as
<f type="i"><term lang="gkt">Cenis</term></f> the wench, into <f type="i"><term lang="gkt">Ceneus</term></f> the yong man, as you may
see in <f type="i">Ovid</f>. 
</page>

<page no="130">
Among the alteration of names, it may also be remem&shy;
bred how Kings of Armes, Herolds, and Pursivants are
new named with a bole of wine powred vpon their heads
by the Prince or Earle Marshall, when they are invested,
and the Kings crowned, as <f type="i">Garter, Clerenceux, Norrey: Lan&shy;
caster, Yorke, Richmond, Somerset</f>, &amp;c. which is as ancient
as the time of King <f type="i">Edward</f> the third. For we reade, that
when newes was brought him at <f type="i">Windsor</f>, by a Pursuvant,
of the victory at the battell of <f type="i">Auroy</f>, hee bountifully re&shy;
warded him, and immediately created him Herold, by the
name of <f type="i">Windsor</f>. 

Heere might I note that women with vs at their mar&shy;
riage do change their surnames and passe into their hus&shy;
bands names, and iustly for that then <f type="i"><term lang="la">Non sunt duo, sed caro
vna</term></f>: And yet in <f type="i">Fraunce</f> and the Netherlands, the better
sort of women will still retaine their owne name with their
husbands, as if <f type="i">Mary</f> daughter of <f type="i">Villevill</f> be married to 
<f type="i">A. Vavin</f>, she will write herselfe <f type="i">Mary Vavin Villevill</f>. But 
I feare husbands will not like this note, for that some of
their dames may be ambitiously over-pert and too-too
forward to imitate it.

Beside these former alterations, the tyran Time which
hath swallowed many names, hath also in vse of speach,
changed more by contracting syncopating, curtelling and
mollifying them, as beside them before mentioned. <f type="i">Adre&shy;
cy</f> is now turned into <f type="i">Darcy</f>, <f type="i">Aldethelight</f> into <f type="i">Awdley</f>, <f type="i">Sa&shy;
brigworth</f> into <f type="i">Sapsford</f>, <f type="i">Sitsil</f> into <f type="i">Cecil</f>, <f type="i">Mountjoy</f> into
<f type="i">Mungey</f>, <f type="i">Dunevet</f> into <f type="i">Knevet</f>, if you beleeve <f type="i">Leland, Grin&shy;
vile</f> into <f type="i">Greenefeild</f>, <f type="i">Haverington</f> into <f type="i">Harrington, <f type="i">Bourg&shy;
chier</f> into <f type="i">Bowcer</f>, <f type="i">Le Daiherell</f> into <f type="i">Dairell</f>, <f type="i">Ravensford</f> into
<f type="i">Rainsford</f>, <f type="i">Mohune</f> into <f type="i">Moone</f>, <f type="i">Danvers</f> into <f type="i">Davers</f>, <f type="i">Ger&shy;
negan</f> into <f type="i">Ierningham</f>, <f type="i">Cahors</f> into <f type="i">Chaworth</f>, <f type="i">Dinant</f> into
<f type="i">Dinham</f>, <f type="i">Wooderington</f> into <f type="i">Witherington</f>, <f type="i">Estlegh</f> into
<f type="i">Astly</f>, <f type="i">Turbervile</f> into <f type="i">Troublefeild</f>, <f type="i">De Oileio</f> into <f type="i">Doiley</f>, 
<f type="i">Pogli</f> into <f type="i">Polye, <f type="i">De Alanson</f> into <f type="i">Dalison</f>, <f type="i">Purefoy</f> into <f type="i">Pur&shy;
frey</f>, <f type="i">Cavendish</f> into <f type="i">Candish</f>, <f type="i">Veinour</f> into <f type="i">Fenner</f>, <f type="i">Hare&shy;
court</f> into <f type="i">Harcot</f>, <f type="i">Sanctpaul</f> into <f type="i">Sampol</f>, <f type="i">Fortesen</f> into <f type="i">Fos&shy; 
</page>

<page no="131">
ku, Ferrers</f> into <f type="i">Ferris, Throckmorton</f> into <f type="i">Frogmorton,Cul&shy;
wen</f> into <f type="i">Curwen</f>, <f type="i">Poitevin</f> into <f type="i">Petsin</f>, <f type="i">Berenger</f> into <f type="i">Benger</f>. 
<f type="i">Montacute</f> into <f type="i">Montague</f>, <f type="i">Gernons</f> into <f type="i">Garnish</f>, <f type="i">Pulliston</f>
into <f type="i">Pilston</f>, <f type="i">Cholmondley</f> into <f type="i">Cholmley</f>, <f type="i">Grosvenour</f> into
<f type="i">Gravener</f>, <f type="i">Meisuilwarin</f> into <f type="i">Manwaring</f>, after into <f type="i">Man&shy;
nering</f>, <f type="i">Fitz-Gerard</f> into <f type="i">Garret</f>, <f type="i">Okover</f> into <f type="i">Oker</f>, <f type="i">Vvedale</f>
into <f type="i">Vdall</f>, <f type="i">Damprecourt</f> first into <f type="i">Dabrigecourt</f>, now into
<f type="i">Dabscot</f>, <f type="i">Leventhrop</f> into <f type="i">Lenthrop</f>, <f type="i">Wilburnhame</f> into <f type="i">Will&shy;
bram</f>, <f type="i">Askow</f> from <f type="i">Ascouth</f>, and that from the old Christi&shy;
an name <f type="i">Ascuith</f>, which in Latine was <f type="i"><term lang="la">Hasculphus</term></f> and <f type="i"><term lang="la">Ha&shy;
stulphus</term></f>, that is, <f type="i">Speedy helpe</f>, &amp;c.

It may not seem from this purpose, if I here set down &amp;
compare a few names of ancient good families, as they are
written in old Latine Records and histories, with them
now in vse: whereof many are as it were so transformed
in common pronunciation from the originall, as they will
scantly seeme to have beene the same.
