<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Scales(pn)">SCALES, </form> <xpln lang="la"><f type="i">De Scalarijs.</f></xpln></wordentry>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Straunge(pn)">STRAVNGE, </form> <xpln lang="la"><f type="i">Extraneus.</f></xpln></wordentry>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Vipount(pn)">VIPOVNT, </form> <xpln lang="la"><f type="i">De Veteri-ponte.</f></xpln></wordentry>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="De La Zouch(pn)">DE LA ZOVCH, </form> <xpln lang="la"><f type="i">De Stipite sicco.</f></xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

For <f type="i">William de la Zouch</f> Archbishop of <f type="i">Yorke</f>, is so called
in this verse, for his valour in an encounter against the
Scottishmen at <f type="i">Bearparke</f>. 1342.

<f type="i"><term lang="la">Est pater invictus sicco de stipite dictus, &amp;c.</term></f>

For <f type="i">Zouch</f> signifieth the stocke of a tree in the French
tongue. And this translation of names into Greeke or
Latine, is still in vse among the Germans, for hee whose
name is <f type="i"><term lang="gr">Ertswert</term></f> or <f type="i">Blackland</f>, will be <f type="i">Melancthon</f>; if <f type="i">New&shy;
man</f>, <f type="i">Neander</f>; if <f type="i">Holieman</f>, <f type="i">Osiander</f>; if <f type="i">Brooke</f>, <f type="i">Torrentius</f>; if
<f type="i">Fenne</f>, <f type="i">Paludanus</f>, &amp;c. which some amongest vs beganne
lately to imitate.

To drawe to an end, no man whatsoever is to be disli&shy;
ked in respect either of originall, or of signification, for 
</page>

<page no="134">
neither the good names doe grace the bad, neither do evil
names disgrace the good; if names are to bee accounted
good or bad. In all countries both good and bad have bin
of the same surnames, which as they participate one with
the other in glory, so somtimes in shame. Therfore for an&shy;
cestors, parentage and names (as he said) let every man say
<f type="i"><term lang="la">Vix ca nostra voco</term>.</f> Time hath intermingled &amp; confused all,
&amp; we are com al to this present, by successive variable des&shy;
cents from high and lowe: or as hee saieth more plainely,
the low are descended from the high, and contrariwise, the
high from low. 

If any doe vaunt of their names, let them looke to it, lest
they have <f type="i"><term lang="la">inania nomina</term></f>; you know who saith, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Vestra nomi&shy;
na nunquam sum admiratus, viros qui ea vobis reliquerunt,
magnos arbitrabor.</term></f> And if they glory in their auntient faire
names, and farre fetcht descents, with contempt of others,
happly some such like as <f type="i">Marius</f> was, may returne vppon
them <f type="i">Marius</f> wordes; <f type="i"><term lang="la">Si iure despiciunt nos, faciunt idem
maioribus suis, quibus vti nobis ex virtute nobilitas caepit. In&shy;
vident honori nostro: ergo invideant labori, innocenti&aelig;, pericu&shy;
lis etiam nostris, quoniam per h&aelig;c illum c&aelig;pimus.</term></f> Yea some
of these occupation and office names, which doe seeme so
meane to some, are as auntient in this Realme as most o&shy;
ther. For in that most authenticall Register <f type="i">Doomesday</f>
book in the Exchequer, ye shal have <f type="i"><term lang="la">Cocus, Aurifaber, Pic&shy;
tor, Pistor, Accipitrarius, Camerarius, Venator, Piscator Me&shy;
dicus</f></term>, i. Cook, Goldsmith, Painter, Baker, Falconer, Cham&shy;
berlaine, Huntsman, Fisher, Marshall, Porter, Leach, and
others, which then held land <f type="i"><term lang="la">in Capite</term></f>, and without doubt
left these names to their posteritie, albeit happly they are
not mentioned in those tables of <f type="i">Battaile Abbey</f>, of such as
came in at the Conquest: which whosoever consider well,
shall finde alwayes to be forged, and those names to be in&shy;
serted which the time in every age favoured, and were ne&shy;
ver mentioned in that authenticall Record.

If you please to compare the Roman names that seeme
so stately, because you vnderstand them not, you will dis&shy; 
</page>

<page no="135">
daine them in respect of our meanest names; <wordgroup1 type="t"><wordentry type="h"><form lang="la"><term lang="en">For what is</term>
<f type="i">Fronte</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="beetle-browed(a)">but Beetle-browed?</xpln></wordentry> <wordentry type="h"><form lang="la"><f type="i">Casius</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="cat's-eye(a)">Cattes-eies?</xpln></wordentry> <wordentry type="h"><form lang="la"><f type="i">Petus</f>

legged,</xpln></wordentry> <wordentry type="h"><form lang="la"><f type="i">Pedo</f></form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="Longshanks(n)">Long-shankes,</xpln></wordentry> <wordentry type="h"><form lang="la"><f type="i">Marcellus</f> </form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="hammer(n)">Hammer, for it
commeth from <f type="i">Marculus</f>,</xpln></wordentry> <wordentry type="h"><form lang="la"><f type="i">Hortensius</f> </form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="gardener(n)">Gardner,</xpln></wordentry> <wordentry type="h"><form lang="la"><f type="i">Cilo</f> </form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="petty long pate(n)">Pet&shy;

<f type="i"><term lang="la">Improbioribus labris homo</term></f></xpln>
<lemenote>
Not found in OED.
</lemenote>
</wordentry>. 

Those great names also <wordentry type="h"><form lang="la"><f type="i">Fabius, Lentulus, Cicero, Piso,
Stolo</f>,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="bean-man(n)" lexeme="lentil(n)" lexeme="chickpea(n)" lexeme="peascod-man(n)" lexeme="branch(n)"> are no more in our tongue then Bean-man, Lentill,
Chich-pease, Pescod-man, Braunch;</xpln></wordentry> for as <f type="i">Plinie</f> saieth,
these names were first appropriated to them, for skill in
sowing those graines. Neyther those from beasts which
<f type="i">Varro</f> reciteth in the second <f type="i">de Rustica, <wordentry type="h"><form lang="la">Taurus, Vitulus, O&shy;
vilius, Porcius, Caprilius</f>,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="bull(n)" lexeme="calf(n)" lexeme="sheep(n)" lexeme="hog(n)" lexeme="goat(n)"> were better than Bull, Calfe, Sheep,
Hogge, Goate, &amp;c. </xpln></wordentry></wordgroup1>

In respect of these names all the names of <f type="i">England</f> are
such as I thinke few would take the benefite of <f type="i">Dioclesians</f>
rescript, which I lately mentioned. But in <f type="i">France</f> (where
the fowle names <f type="i"><term lang="fr">Marmot, Merd'oyson, Boreau</term></f>) and in
<f type="i">Spaine</f> (where <f type="i"><term lang="sp">Verdugo</term></f>, i</f>. Hangman, <f type="i"><term lang="sp">Putanero</term></f>, and such like
are rife) it is no marvel that some procure licence from the
King to change their names: and that a Gentlewoman,
doctor <f type="i">Andreas</f> the great Civilians wife said; <f type="i">If faire names
were saleable, they woulde be well bought</f>. 

Thus much of Christian names and Surnames, or <f type="i">Pr&aelig;&shy;
nomina</f> and <f type="i">Nomina</f>. As for <f type="i"><term lang="la">Cognomina</term></f> and <f type="i"><term lang="la">Agnomina</term></f>, or
By-names were rare in our Nation; onely I remember
these three, <f type="i">Le Beuf</f> in the familie of the <f type="i">Giffardes</f>, of <f type="i">Me&shy;
uill</f> among the <f type="i">Darcies</f>, and <f type="i">Bouchard</f> in one house of the
<f type="i">Latimers</f>, and some say <f type="i">Algernoun</f> in the familie of <f type="i">Percies</f>:
but that as yet is out of the reach of my reading, vnlesse it
be the same that is corruptly in the descent of the Earles of 
</page>

<page no="136">
<f type="i">Bolleyn</f> belonging to the late Queene Mother of <f type="i">France</f>,
set downe <f type="i">Agernouns</f>, for <f type="i">Algernouns</f>; For so <f type="i">Eustace</f> the
second is there by-named, who in other olde Pedegrees is
called <f type="i">Eustace</f> with the cleare eyes.

As for additions given over and beside names, and sur&shy;
names in Law causes, that I may note them out of a Lawe
booke, they are either of estate, or degree, or mysterie, or
towne, or hamlet, or countie. Addition of estate are these;
Yeoman, Gentleman, Esquire. Addition of degree are
those which wee call names of dignitie, as Knight, Earle,
Marquesse, Duke. Additions of mysterie are such, Scrive&shy;
ner, Carpenter, Smith. Addition of townes, as of <f type="i">Pading&shy;
ton, Islington, Edelmeton</f>. And where a man hath houshold
in two places, he shall be saide to dwell in both of them, so
that his addition in one of them doth suffice.

By the Statute the first yeere of king <f type="i">Henry</f> the fift and
fift chapter, it was ordained, that in suites or in actions
where processe of Vtary lieth, such addition should be
to the name of the Defendant, to shew his estate, myste&shy;
rie, and place where he dwelleth, and that such Writts shall
abate, if they have not such additions, if the Defendant do
take exception thereat, they shall not abate by the office of
the court.

Also, Duke, Marquesse, Earle, or Knight be none of
that addition, but names of dignitie, which should have
beene given before the statute. A<emend err="u">n</emend>d this was ordained by
the sayde statute, made in the first yeare of king <f type="i">Henry</f> the
seaventh chap. 5. to the intent that one man may not be
grieved or troubled by the vtlary of an other, but that by
reason of the certaine addition every man might be cer&shy;
tainely knowne, and beare his owne burden.

How the names of them which for capitall crimes a&shy;
gainst <f type="i">Maiestie</f>, were creazed out of the publike Records,
Tables, and Registers, or forbidden to be borne by their
posteritie, when their memory was damned. I could shew
at large, but this and such l<emend err="t">i</emend>ke, with <f type="i">Misnomer</f> in our lawes,
and other Quidities, I leave to the professours of lawes.
</page>

<page no="137">
Somewhat might be said here of the adjuncts to names
or titles, which in most antient times were either none, or
most simple. For <f type="i">Augustus</f> was impatient to be called <f type="i"><term lang="la">Do&shy;
minus</term></f> yet <f type="i">Domitian</f> liked well to be called <f type="i"><term lang="la">Dominus Deus<expan type="q',">que</expan></term></f>
and <f type="i"><term lang="la">Dominus</term></f> was taken vp by everie private man, as ap&shy;
peereth by <f type="i">Seneca</f>, and the poore Gr&aelig;cian which refused
that title by alluding <term lang="gk">&#x039F;&#x1F50;&#x03BA; &#x1F10;&#x03B8;&#x03AD;&#x03BB;&#x03C9; &#x0394;&#x03CC;&#x03BC;&#x03B9;&#x03BD;&#x03B5;</term></f>, <term lang="gk">&#x03BF;&#x1F50; &#x03B3;&#x03AC;&#x03C1; &#x1F12;&#x03C7;&#x03C9; &#x03B4;&#x03CC;&#x03BC;&#x03B5;&#x03BD;&#x03B1;&#x03B9;</term></f>.
Nevertheles it was never vsed by the Emperors, from <f type="i">Do&shy;
mitian</f> to <f type="i">Dioclesianus</f>, as <f type="i">Victor</f> noteth; but afterward it
was continued by the Christian Emperors, yea vpon their
Coines. 

And that which is more strange, they vsed then as ap&shy;
peareth in the Constitutions, for themselves. <f type="i"><term lang="la">&AElig;ternitas
nostra, Perennitas nostra, Numen nostrum;</term></f> and to their prin&shy;
cipall officers, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Vir illustris, Vir spectabilis, Magnifica cellitudo,
Sublimis magnitudo tua, Illustris magnificentia, Sublimitas,
Miranda sublimitas, Eminentia tua, Excellentia tua, Pr&aelig;celsa 
magnificentia tua, &amp;c.</term></f> As appeareth in the Volumes of the 
Civill Lawe. So as I know not whie that Spite king <f type="i">Bu&shy;
chanan</f> should envy lesser titles to Princes, the verie Tipes
of Gods maiestie, yea verie Gods in earth, and brand
them with the marke of <f type="i"><term lang="la">Sericati nebulones,</term></f> which honour
Princes therewith.

The Romans vnder the latter Emperours had a verie
curious and carefull observation, in giving titles to men of
reputation, which as I have read were onely five; <f type="i"><term lang="la">Illustris</term></f>
was the highest appropriated to the <f type="i"><term lang="la">Pr&aelig;fecti Pr&aelig;torio</term></f> of <f type="i">I&shy;
taly</f> and <f type="i">Gallia</f>, the <f type="i"><term lang="la">Pr&aelig;fectus</term></f> of the Cittie of <f type="i">Rome, <term lang="la">Magi&shy;
ster Equitum, Magister Peditum, Qu&aelig;stor Palatij</f>, Comes Lar&shy;
gitionis</term></f>, &amp;c. and all that had voice in the Senate. <f type="i"><term lang="la">Spectabi&shy;
lis</term></f> was the second title due to the Lievtenants generall, and
<f type="i"><term lang="la">Comites</term></f> of Provinces &amp;c. So <f type="i"><term lang="la">in Notitia Provinciarum, Vi&shy;
carius Britanniarum, Comes Littoris Saxonici per Britanni&shy;
am. Dux Britanni&aelig;</term></f> are stiled <f type="i"><term lang="la">Viri spectabiles</term></f>. <f type="i"><term lang="la">Clarissimus</term></f>
was the third title peculiar onelie to the <f type="i"><term lang="la">Consulares, Corre&shy;
ctores</term></f>, and <f type="i"><term lang="la">Pr&aelig;sides</term></f> of Provinces. <f type="i"><term lang="la">Perfectissimus</term></f> was the
fourth. <f type="i">Egregius</f> the fift. And as <f type="i"><term lang="la">Clarissimus</term></f> was a title to 
</page>

<page no="138">
those great officers above specified, so no other could have
that, as neither of <f type="i"><term lang="la">Perfectissimus</term></f>, and <f type="i"><term lang="la">Egregius</term></f>, but graun&shy;
ted by Patents. And in that age, as it is in the Code of
<f type="i"><term lang="la">Theodosius, Titulo, Vt Dignitatem ordo servetur. Si quis in&shy;
debitum sibi locum vsurpaverit, nulla seignoratione defendat,
sit<expan type="q',">que</expan> plan&egrave; sacrilegij reus</term></f>. 

Amongst vs the Kings had these adiuncts, when they
were written and spoken vnto, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Gloriosus, Gloriosissimus, Pr&aelig;&shy;
cellentissimus, Charissimus Dominus, Rex illustris,</term></f> lately <f type="i"><term lang="la">Po&shy;
tentissimus, Invictissimus, Serenissimus;</term></f> Our liege Lord, Our 
Soveraigne. Our Dread Soveraigne &amp;c.

As for <f type="i">Grace</f>, it beganne about the time of <f type="i">Henry</f> the
fourth. <f type="i">Excellent Grace</f> vnder <f type="i">Henry</f> the sixt. <f type="i">High and 
mighty Prince</f> vnder <f type="i">Edward</f> the fourth. And <f type="i">Maiestie</f>
which first beganne to the Roman Emperours about the
time of <f type="i">Gallienus</f>, came hither in the time of King <f type="i">Henry</f> the 
eight, as <f type="i">Sacred Maiestie</f> lately in our memory. Whereas a&shy;
mong Christians it was appliable onely in former ages to
God, as among the old Romans to the Goddesse <f type="i">Maiestie</f>
the daughter of <f type="i">Honour</f> and <f type="i">Reverence</f>. 

Among other men in former ages <f type="i">Dan</f> corrupted from
<f type="i"><term lang="la">Dominus</term></f>, was the greatest attribute both to Spirituall and
Temporall, and afterward <f type="i">Worshipfull</f>, and <f type="i">Right Worship&shy;
full</f>, hath been thought convenient among vs for the great
Dukes and Earles; but wee nowe beginne so to overlade
men with additions, as Spaniardes did lately, vntill they were
restrained by the Pragmaticall 1586. At which time
<f type="i">Pasquil</f> at <f type="i">Rome</f> being demanded why <f type="i">Philip</f> of <f type="i">Spaine</f> had
so taken away all titles from all sortes of men, aunswered
merrily, albeit not religiously. That it may be verified of
him which is saide, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Tu solus Dominus, tu solus altissimus</term></f>, in
respect of his voluminous long Title which will tire the
Reader.

Thus farre had I proceeded in names, when it was hie
time to stay, for I am advertised that there is one, which by
Arte Trochilick, will drawe all English surnames of the
best families out of the pitte of Poetrie, as <f type="i">Bourchier</f> from 
</page>

<page no="139">
<f type="i">Busyris</f> the tyrant of <f type="i">&AElig;gypt</f>; <f type="i">Percy</f> from flying <f type="i">Perseus</f>;
<f type="i">Darcy</f> from <f type="i">Dircaus Apollo</f>; <f type="i">Lee</f> from <f type="i">L&aelig;tus</f> turned into a 
Swanne in <f type="i">Ovid</f>; <f type="i">Iakeson</f> from <f type="i">Iason</f>: well hee may satisfie 
them herein, whom I cannot. As for my selfe, I acknow&shy;
ledge that I cannot satisfie neither them, nor my selfe in all
particularities: and well therefore I doe like him that said,
He dooth not teach well which teacheth all; leaving no&shy;
thing to subtill wittes to sift out. And sure I am scrupu&shy;
lous Diligence lieth open to Envie. But for such as wil not
be content with that which is said, I wish sir <f type="i">Iohn de Bil&shy;
bao</f> would coniure vp <f type="i">William Ockam</f> the father of the No&shy;
minalles (as <f type="i">Appion</f> did <f type="i">Homer</f>,) for their better satisfacti&shy;
on heerein. Meane while I desire no man will take offence
at any thing heere spoken, when as I have beene so farre
from giving offence, that I dare protest it in that solemne
auntient forme, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Superos, &amp; Sydera testor</term></f>. Hating it in o&shy;
thers, and condemning it in my selfe, even vnto the bot&shy;
tomles pitt of hell. 
</section>
</page>
